** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE VARGAS-CASTRO
File: AXXX XX9 440 - Phoenix, AZ
August 20, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nicomedes E. Suriel, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jennifer I. Gaz
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled (found)
 
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; reopening; termination
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s September 17, 2014, decision. The respondent has also filed a motion to terminate during the pendency of this appeal. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion to terminate. The appeal will be dismissed and the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion and judgment. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On September 17, 2014, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent pre-conclusion voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(a)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (the “Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a)(1). The Immigration Judge’s order reflects that, in exchange for the grant of voluntary departure, the respondent, with the assistance of counsel, waived appeal (Tr. at 37-40). See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i)(D). We are not persuaded that the respondent’s conclusory statement in the Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26), maintaining that he was pressured by the Immigration Judge to accept the grant of voluntary departure, is sufficient to demonstrate that his waiver of appeal was not knowing and voluntary, especially because the respondent was represented by counsel before the Immigration Judge and has not suggested that his prior attorney committed ineffective assistance of counsel. See Biwot v. Gonzales, 403 F.3d 1094, 1097-98 (9th Cir. 2005) (explaining that a waiver of the right to appeal must be knowing and voluntary to be effective); Matter of Rodrigeuz-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320, 1322 (BIA 2000) (same). We therefore lack jurisdiction over the respondent’s appeal, including his claim that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his request for administrative closure.1 See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39 (explaining that an Immigration Judge’s order becomes final upon issuance if both parties waive appeal); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697, 699 (BIA 1993); Saenz-Kobel v. Holder, 459 Fed. Appx. 591, 592 (9th Cir. 2011); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i)(B) (explaining that in accepting a grant of pre-conclusion voluntary departure, an alien must waive all additional applications for relief).
 
*2 Finally, the respondent has filed a motion requesting that proceedings be terminated so that he may seek adjustment of status before the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services. The respondent wishes to pursue an application for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i). Inasmuch as the Immigration Judge’s decision became final upon the respondent’s waiver of appeal, this request must comply with the requirements of a motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b), 1003.2(b). However, the respondent has not established prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act (cf. Tr. at 33). See Lopez-Vasquez v. Holder, 706 F.3d 1072, 1080 (9th Cir. 2013); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992) (explaining that a party requesting reopening bears a “heavy burden” of establishing that the outcome of proceedings would likely be different if the motion were granted).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed for lack of jurisdiction.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to terminate is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We also note that the respondent indicated on the Notice of Appeal that he would file a separate statement or brief in support of his arguments but has not done so. Accordingly, if we had jurisdiction over this matter, the respondent’s appeal would be subject to summary dismissal pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(E).

2015 WL 5180644 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PEDRO DOMINGO-TORREZ A.K.A. LUCIANO PALENCIA
File: AXXX XX2 520 - Oakdale, LA
August 19, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Luke H. Abrusley, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lorraine L. Griffin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Act
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated May 20, 2015, denying his request for voluntary departure under the provisions of section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a), as a matter of discretion and ordering his removal from the United States.1 The respondent’s appeal, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security, will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent’s arguments on appeal do not persuade this Board that he has demonstrated that he merits the privilege of voluntary departure as matter of discretion. See section 240(c)(4)(A)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d); Matter of Arguelles-Campos, 22 I&N Dec. 811, 820 (BIA 1999); Matter of Gamboa, 14 I&N Dec. 244 (BIA 1972). Even though the record does not contain formal conviction documents, the respondent, through counsel, has conceded that has he has been convicted of driving under the influence on two occasions (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 7; Exh. 2). See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986) (holding that, absent a showing of egregious circumstances, an attorney’s concessions are binding upon an alien as an admission). There can be little dispute that drunk driving is an extremely serious offense. See, e.g., United States v. Chapa-Garza, 262 F.3d 479, 485 (5th Cir. 2001). The respondent also has other convictions, and was previously granted a voluntary return after apprehension (Exh. 2). The respondent has not meaningfully articulated any positive factors, i.e., family ties to the United States or a history of gainful employment, which would warrant a holding that, despite his history of drunk driving, he warrants voluntary departure as a matter of discretion. Thus, upon a balancing of the appropriate factors, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s request for voluntary departure as a matter of discretion (I.J. at 2).
 
*2 Finally, there is an absence of indicia that the respondent’s former counsel attempted to him to call him to testify regarding his positive equities or present evidence of positive equities. The respondent has not complied with the evidentiary standards for bringing forth an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Moreover, the respondent has not articulated the testimony or evidence that he would have presented in these proceedings. Thus, he has not established that he was prejudiced by his former attorney’s handling of this case. See Anwar v. INS, 116 F.3d 140, 144 (5th Cir. 1997). As such, we decline to remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of the respondent’s request for voluntary departure. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
For the reasons set forth above, we decline to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to order the respondent’s removal from the United States to Guatemala. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent, through counsel, has conceded that is subject to removal from the United States because he is an alien who is present in this country without being admitted or paroled by an immigration officer or who arrived at any time or place other than as designated by the Attorney General (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 4; Exh. 1). See section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c).

2015 WL 5244403 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EZEQUIEL PENA-GONZALEZ A.K.A. EZEQUIEL GONZALEZ PENA
File: AXXX XX5 771 - San Diego, CA
August 17, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joseph Mbacho, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
O. El-Bogdadi
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s August 14, 2014, decision that denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. We find no reason to disturb the decision of the Immigration Judge. In this case, the record reflects that the respondent was personally served with a Notice to Appear (NTA) (Exh. 1). On April 8, 2014, the San Diego Immigration Court personally served a hearing notice on the respondent’s attorney of record, Russell Bolin, rescheduling the respondent’s hearing for June 20, 2014. The respondent failed to appear for his hearing as scheduled and he was ordered removed in absentia.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent received proper notice inasmuch as written notice of his hearing was provided to his attorney of record. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 259 (BIA 1985) (finding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (providing that whenever a person is required to be given notice, such notice shall be given to the attorney or representative of record, or to the person himself if unrepresented). Moreover, the record contains a Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative Before the Immigration Court (Form EOIR-28) for Mr. Bolin, as required by regulation. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.4(a).
 
To the extent that the respondent argues exceptional circumstances because his prior attorney did not. inform him about the date and time of his hearing, his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not satisfy any of the procedural requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A.G. 2009) (directing the Board and Immigration Judges to apply “the Lozada framework” to claims of ineffective assistance of counsel “pending the issuance of a final rule”). We also decline to reopen proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations). Moreover, we are not persuaded by counsel for the respondent’s argument that the respondent’s failure to appear was due to a clerical error in his office. As the Immigration Judge observed, Attorney Mbacho had not entered his appearance in this matter prior to the April 8, 2014, hearing and there is no indication in the record that Attorney Mbacho entered his appearance by filling a form EOIR-28 with the Immigration Judge prior to the in absentia order. We therefore decline to further address this issue.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order shall be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 5244403 (BIA)

2015 WL 5180635 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JANG WOO LEE
File: AXXX XX3 783 - Seattle, WA
August 17, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Catherine Susan Willmore, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ryan A. Kahler
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)] - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i)
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)] - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of South Korea, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s June 24, 2014, decision, which denied the respondent’s request for a continuance. The respondent was granted the privilege of voluntary departure. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent’s removability is not disputed. Indeed, the record confirms that the respondent, through his attorney, conceded his removability under both sections 237(a)(1)(A) and 237(a)(1)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(1)(A) and 1227(a)(1)(C)(i) as charged in the Notice to Appear (I.J. at 1; Tr. at 28; Exh. 1). However, we do acknowledge the respondent’s statement in the Notice of Appeal apparently challenging the charge under section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Act. In this regard, we have held that, absent egregious circumstances, a tactical decision by an attorney (in this case admitting to the allegations and conceding to the corresponding charges lodged in the Notice to Appear) is binding on the client. See Matter of Gawaran, 20 I&N Dec. 938, 942 (BIA 1995); Matter of Valasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986). The respondent has not alleged any egregious circumstances; nor do we find any such circumstances on this record. Indeed, the respondent has not alleged that his attorney was ineffective or complied with the procedural requirements, as set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Regardless, the respondent does not dispute on appeal his removability under section 237(a)(1)(C)(i) of the Act.
 
The remaining issue raised on appeal by the respondent is whether the Immigration Judge should have continued the removal proceedings for him to further pursue an administrative appeal of the denial of his U-visa application (Form I-918) by the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”). However, the respondent has failed to specify any legal or factual error in the Immigration Judge’s decision, and we are satisfied that the Immigration Judge applied the appropriate factors in denying the continuance. See Matter of Sanchez Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807 (BIA 2012).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
*3 Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2015 WL 5180619 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARACELY DEL CARMEN MENDOZA-ROBLES
File: AXXX XX4 672 - Los Angeles, CA
August 11, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joaquin A. Talleda, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Termination
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s October 23, 2013, decision denying her motion to suppress evidence and to terminate proceedings.1 The respondent also filed a motion asking that the Board hold her appeal in abeyance, pending the result of her application to the Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS”) for exercise of prosecutorial discretion. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s factual determinations, including credibility determinations, for clear error. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board uses a de novo standard of review for questions of law, discretion, judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Notice to Appear (“NTA”), alleging that the respondent is a native and citizen of Nicaragua unlawfully present in the United States, was personally served on the respondent on August 4, 2008 (Exh. 1). The service of the NTA was accomplished with the cooperation of the respondent’s counsel, after the respondent and several other female natives of Nicaragua were detained and questioned as part of an investigation into human trafficking in the Los Angeles, California, area (Exh. 5E -I-213). According to the respondent’s appeal brief and her motion to suppress, after the respondent and the other Nicaraguan natives were apprehended and questioned by officers with the Burbank, California, police department about potentially being human trafficking victims, they were questioned by officers with the DHS’s Immigration and Customs Enforcement (“ICE”) branch. See Respondent’s Motion to Suppress at 6; Brief at 2-3. After their release by the police department, the respondent was scheduled for an interview with ICE (Id.). Counsel for the respondent indicated that he worked with ICE to provide advance information so that the “NTA [could be] prepared in advance.” See Motion to Suppress at 8. Pursuant to this agreement, counsel for the respondent provided a completed “““Background Information Sheet” for the respondent in which she admitted that she was a native and citizen of Nicaragua. See Motion to Suppress at Exh. K. Based on this, the DHS prepared the Form I-213 in which the respondent is described as admitting that she is a native and citizen of Nicaragua (Exh. 5E).
 
*2 The respondent sought suppression of the I-213, arguing that the “““admission” referenced therein was not obtained directly by the undersigned officer via questioning of the respondent, but rather through the submission of the “Background Information Sheet” that was allegedly filled out by a secretary for the respondent’s counsel. See Motion to Suppress at 8-10, 13-14. The respondent therefore alleged that the information in the I-213 was unreliable hearsay. See id. Furthermore, the respondent argued that the information is unreliable because the secretary who obtained the information for the “Background Information Sheet” was later fired from counsel’s firm due to theft Id. at 9. The respondent repeats the same arguments on appeal.
 
We will dismiss the appeal. A request to exclude evidence is made via a motion to suppress that is supported by an affidavit or other objective evidence that explains why suppression is appropriate. Matter of Barcenas, 19 I&N Dec. 609 (BIA 1988); Matter of Wong, 13 I&N Dec. 820, 822 (BIA 1971). When an alien has come forward with adequate evidence in support of suppression, the burden shifts to the DHS to justify the manner in which it obtained the evidence. Matter of Barcenas, supra, at 611.
 
We will affirm the Immigration Judge’s assessment that the information with regard to the respondent’s alienage in the I-213, which was obtained via counsel’s provision of the “Background Information Sheet,” is sufficiently reliable (May 7, 2013, I.J. at 2). The respondent has not offered evidence to suggest that the information contained therein was false or obtained by coercion or duress. Matter of Barcenas, supra, at 611. The fact that it contains hearsay is not itself a compelling reason to suppress the Form I-213, since an Immigration Judge is authorized to consider hearsay evidence if it is reliable and if its use is fundamentally fair. Id.; see also Gu v. Gonzales, 454 F.3d 1014, 1021 (9th Cir. 2006); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 505 (BIA 1980).
 
Furthermore, we also will affirm the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that it was proper for the DHS to prepare the Form I-213 using counsel’s admissions on the behalf of the respondent. These admissions distinguish the respondent’s case from any where a Form I-213 may have been found insufficiently reliable on the basis of hearsay not involving representations by the alien’s own counsel. The respondent is bound by the admissions and concessions of her counsel in these proceedings. Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986). The respondent has neither argued nor presented egregious circumstances sufficient to conclude that she should not be bound by her counsel’s judicial admissions. Id. (“Absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal admission made before, during, or even after a proceeding by an attorney acting in his professional capacity binds his client as a judicial admission.”). Although the respondent stresses that the admissions on the “Background Information Sheet” were drafted by a secretary who was subsequently fired, they nevertheless were effectively adopted by counsel when he forwarded them to the DHS with a cover letter bearing his signature. See Motion to Suppress at Exh. K. We are also unpersuaded that the respondent should not be bound by admissions of her counsel that were made before he submitted an Entry of Appearance before the Immigration Court, since ordinary rules of professional responsibility provide adequate safeguards against counsel making unauthorized admissions. Moreover, the aforementioned signed cover letter from the respondent’s counsel to the DHS indicated that he was enclosing executed Form G-28’s (although the actual Forms are not included in the present record). In turn, Form G-28 is the proper form for entering an attorney appearance before the DHS, the agency with which the respondent was interacting at the time in question.
 
*3 The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held that an alien may be relieved of counsel’s admissions when not doing so would produce an unjust result (such as where the admission is inadvertent) or where the admissions contains untrue or incorrect information. See Santiago-Rodriguez v. Holder, 657 F.3d 820, 831-32 (9th Cir. 2011). The respondent has not established that either circumstance is present here, and we therefore find no cause to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion to suppress, and to find the respondent removable as charged. See Torres-Chavez v. Holder, 567 F.3d 1096, 1102 (9th Cir. 2009) (refusing to permit an alien to withdraw his attorney’s tactical decision to admit alienage).
 
While this appeal was pending before the Board, on June 4, 2015, the respondent filed a motion to hold her appeal in abeyance pending a resolution of her application to the DHS to exercise prosecutorial discretion in her case. We interpret this motion as seeking administrative closure of these proceedings in the exercise of discretion. The respondent’s appellate argument regarding her equities in this regard is unavailing here, because the DHS has sole authority to exercise prosecutorial discretion in this matter. Neither the Board nor the Immigration Judge has jurisdiction over issues of prosecutorial discretion. See Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 349-50 (BIA 1982). We recognize that our lack of jurisdiction over the actual exercise of prosecutorial discretion by the DHS does not also divest us of jurisdiction to order administrative closure pending the DHS’s determination. See Singh v. Holder, 771 F.3d 647 (9th Cir. 2014). Nevertheless, given the lack of any indication that the DHS will exercise its sole prosecutorial discretion authority in a manner favorable to the respondent, we conclude that an exercise of our own discretionary authority to administratively close proceedings is not appropriate. See Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012). Absent a showing of a readily available form of relief from removal, we find administrative closure unsupported on this record. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, supra, at 505 (once the DHS institutes proceedings, an Immigration Judge must order an alien deported if the evidence supports the charge). Consequently, we will deny the motion to hold this appeal in abeyance, and we will dismiss the appeal on the following orders.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s motion to suppress the Form I-213 and to terminate proceedings was denied in a May 7, 2013, decision by the Immigration Judge that will hereinafter be referenced as “May 7, 2013, I.J. at __.” The decision ordering her removal from the United States to Nicaragua, which was issued after continued proceedings to determine whether the respondent wished to file any application for relief from removal, is dated October 23, 2013. Both decisions are covered by this appeal. Although the Immigration Judge indicated in the October 23, 2013, decision that she had reviewed whether the respondent had received effective assistance of counsel, we note that the respondent does not argue on appeal that she received ineffective assistance of counsel below.

2015 WL 5180628 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA DE LOS ANGELES LOWE A.K.A. MARIA LOWE-ALARCON A.K.A. MARIA ALARCON-CERVANTES
File: AXXX XX7 912 - El Paso, TX
August 7, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Charles Spector, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brenda J. Thomas
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. She has filed a timely motion to reopen, alleging ineffective assistance of prior counsel. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the granting of the motion. The motion is denied.
 
The respondent contends that former counsel erroneously conceded her removability under section 237(a)(1)(E)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(E)(i). The record reflects, however, that former counsel denied removability, and contested the charge (Exh. 1; I.J. at 1). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s adjustment of status was an admission and since she was admitted within 5 years of her conviction for smuggling, she was removable as charged (I.J. at 2-3).
 
The respondent now argues she is not removable because her adjustment of status was not an admission, citing to Martinez v. Mukasey, 519 F.3d 532 (5th Cir. 2008)(section 212(h) waiver applies to aliens who adjust their status after entering the United States, but not to those who enter the United States as lawful permanent residents); Matter of J-H-J-, 26 I&N Dec. 563 (BIA 2015). However, Martinez did not hold that adjustment of status was not an admission. Further, we have consistently held that adjustment of status is considered an admission. See e.g. Matter of Bouchra Agour, 26 I&N Dec. 566, 573 (BIA 2015). Accordingly, we do not find that former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel to the respondent such that proceedings should be reopened and termination. Nor has the respondent shown the requisite prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2015 WL 5180605 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE OMAR MONROY SALAZAR
File: AXXX XX4 452 - New York, NY
August 3, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pablo Fernandez-Herrera, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 7, 2014, denying his motion for a continuance. On appeal, the respondent also moves for a remand based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion will be denied and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but we review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals de novo. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
We find that the Immigration Judge acted properly in concluding the proceedings and entering an order of removal. The pursuit of deferred action before the Department of Homeland Security does not constitute good cause for a continuance because the exercise of prosecutorial discretion is a matter over which the Immigration Court has no jurisdiction and the possibility that the respondent would be granted such relief was speculative. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987) (the decision to grant or deny a continuance for good cause is within the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge and will not be overturned on appeal unless it is shown that the respondent was denied a full and fair hearing); Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348 (BIA 1982) (Immigration Judge did not err in declining to adjourn hearing to allow alien to pursue deferred action); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 and 1240.6.
 
We find no merit in the respondent’s argument that the decision below requires reversal because the Immigration Judge made prejudicial remarks concerning his potential eligibility for deferred action. Under the circumstances, we find that the Immigration Judge merely gave a fair summary of the posture of the respondent’s potential application for deferred action. In any event, the respondent must demonstrate substantial prejudice affecting the outcome of the proceedings to establish a due process violation warranting reversal of the Immigration Judge’s decision. See Miller v. Mukasey, 539 F.3d 159, 164 (2d Cir. 2008). We are not persuaded that the respondent has done so. Absent a showing of good cause or readily available relief, the Immigration Judge acted properly in ordering the respondent’s removal. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 505 (BIA 1980) (citations omitted) (once removal proceedings are instituted, an Immigration Judge must order an alien deported if the evidence supports the charge). There is no merit in the respondent’s assertion that the Immigration Judge’s order of removal deprived him of the opportunity to persuade DHS counsel that he was a suitable candidate for deferred action on his removal since nothing prevents him from applying to the DHS to favorably exercise its prosecutorial discretion even after an order of removal is entered.
 
*2 We will deny the respondent’s motion to remand based upon ineffective assistance. As an initial matter, the claim fails because the respondent has not complied with any of the procedural requirements outlined in the Board’s decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The claim also fails because the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by the actions or inaction of prior counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (an alien asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must demonstrate prejudice). The substance of the respondent’s claim is that prior counsel failed to make a persuasive case for deferred action on his removal (Brief at 8). However, an application for deferred action is a collateral matter involving the DHS’ independent exercise of prosecutorial discretion, and outside the jurisdiction of the Immigration Court. Therefore, any deficiencies in representation concerning that application are insufficient to demonstrate ineffective assistance in the proceedings before the Immigration Court.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied and the appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 5180605 (BIA)

IN RE: ENOC HUMBERTO ABURTO-RAMIREZ A.K.A. HUMBERTO HUMBERTO ABURTO-RAMIREZ

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Juan Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Candice Kasperson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Nicaragua, has appealed the May 20, 2014, decision of the Immigration Judge denying a motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board may review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
As an initial matter, we note that the respondent has not submitted a brief in support of this appeal, despite indicating on his Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) that he intends to file a separate brief or statement. Hence, we will consider the limited statements contained on the. respondent’s Notice of Appeal.
 
The arguments contained on the respondent’s Notice of Appeal do not specifically challenge the Immigration Judge’s reasons for denying the motion to reopen. The respondent simply states: “The Immigration Judge erred in applying the facts and case law. The IJ did not address the merits of the motion to re-open. The IJ abused her discretion and did not meaningfully address the motion and reasons why Respondent did not seek relief earlier.” Inasmuch as the respondent has not specifically challenged the bases for the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen, we find that the respondent’s arguments on appeal do not establish error in the Immigration Judge’s decision. See, e.g., Matter of Cervantes, 22 I&N Dec. 560, 561 n.1 (BIA 1999) (expressly declining to address an issue not raised by party on appeal); Matter of Gutierrez, 19 I&N Dec. 562, 565 n.3 (BIA 1988) (same).1
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger

2015 WL 4761221 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OSCAR MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX2 630 - Boston, MA
July 9, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stephen A. Lagana, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals the Immigration Judge’s February 19, 2015, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. We review the Immigration Judge’s factual findings for clear error and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). The respondent’s appeal fee waiver request is granted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent based his motion to reopen on an apparent contention that his prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance because he only communicated with the respondent through his girlfriend when the respondent was detained. See Respondent’s Brief at 3. On appeal, the respondent argues that, inter alia, the Immigration Judge erred by finding that the motion to reopen was not timely filed. See Respondent’s Brief at 3-4. As the Immigration Judge alternatively found, however, even if the motion had been timely, the respondent did not satisfy the requirements for reopening (I.J. at 4, 5).
 
“A motion to reopen will not be granted unless the Immigration Judge is satisfied that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). In addition, to obtain reopening, the respondent must make a prima facie showing that he is statutorily eligible for the relief sought and that he warrants relief in the exercise of discretion. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 323 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 106 (1988). Prima facie eligibility is shown where the evidence reveals a reasonable likelihood that the statutory requirements for relief have been satisfied and that there is a reasonable likelihood that relief will be granted in the exercise of discretion. See Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413, 419 (BIA 1996).
 
In this case, the respondent requested that his proceedings be reopened but he did not submit an application for relief or supporting documentation. See Motion to Reopen; see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3) (motion to reopen to apply for relief must include the application for relief and supporting evidence). In his motion, the respondent stated only that he “was denied the opportunity to apply for political asylum.” Motion to Reopen at 3. He did not submit an asylum application or present material evidence that was unavailable at the former hearing. Therefore, because the respondent has not submitted an application or demonstrated prima facie eligibility for relief, he has not met the requirements for reopening.
 
*2 The respondent also argues that, when he waived his right to appeal on April 16, 2014, his waiver was not knowing and voluntary (Tr. at 8-10). See Respondent’s Brief at 5-6. The respondent did not raise this argument in his motion, however. See Motion to Reopen; Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260, 261 n.1 (BIA 2007) (matters not raised before the Immigration Judge are waived on appeal); see also Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629, 638 (BIA 2003); Matter of Edwards, 20 I&N Dec. 191, 196-97 n.4 (BIA 1990). Also, although the respondent argues that, because he waived his right to appeal, he lost his opportunity to apply for asylum, he again has not submitted an asylum application or demonstrated prima facie eligibility for asylum. Therefore, he has not shown that he was prejudiced by the error he alleges. See Toribio-Chavez v. Holder, 611 F.3d 57, 66 (1st Cir. 2010) (due process claim cannot succeed without a showing of prejudice).1
 
Further, as the Immigration Judge found, the respondent did not comply with at least some of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (to succeed on a motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent should submit an affidavit attesting to the relevant facts including the agreement that was entered into with former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken; former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed to respond; the motion should reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities; the respondent must show that he was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel); see also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (I.J. at 5). Additionally, the respondent did not demonstrate that he was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
For these reasons, we uphold the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD

On July 18, 2014, this Board dismissed the respondent’s prior appeal and found that the respondent, through counsel, conceded removability, acknowledged ineligibility for relief from removal, and waived his right to appeal. See Board’s July 18, 2014, decision. We noted that the respondent had not argued that his waiver was not knowing and intelligent.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARMANDO MAXIMILIANO MORALES
File: AXXX XX6 451 - Miami, FL
July 8, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vincent J. Benincasa, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on March 13, 2008, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen to rescind his order of removal (which was entered in absentia on May 24, 2007, due to his failure to appear at his hearing, as scheduled). On April 13, 2015, more than 7 years later, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with this Board alleging ineffective assistance provided by his former attorney (Esther A. Zaretsky), and a non-attorney (Fabian Sosa), at various points in time. The motion will be denied.
 
Insofar as the respondent is moving to reopen to rescind his May 24, 2007, in absentia removal order-whether based on a claim of lack of notice, or a new claim of “exceptional circumstances” beyond his control (including ineffective assistance of counsel) accounting for his failure to appear-his motion must be filed with the Immigration Judge.1 Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
The respondent also advances specific claims of ineffective assistance in conjunction with his June 18, 2007, motion to reopen to rescind his in absentia removal order (filed in Immigration Court), and his ensuing appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying that motion.2 Motion to Reopen (Motion) at 5-6. However, the respondent’s motion in this regard was filed April 13, 2015, more than 90 days after we issued our March 13, 2008, decision dismissing his appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision. Therefore, insofar as the instant motion to reopen is based on these particular claims of ineffective assistance, his motion is untimely by several years.
 
Notwithstanding this circumstance, the Eleventh Circuit otherwise recognizes that the 90-day filing deadline for motions to reopen based on claims of ineffective assistance of counsel may be equitably tolled under certain circumstances. Avila-Santoyo v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra at 1363-65. Pursuant to controlling case law, to establish eligibility for equitable tolling an alien must show that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way. Id. at 1363 n.5, and the cases cited therein.
 
As pertinent to whether equitable tolling is available to the instant motion to reopen, we note that the respondent admits to having received our March 13, 2008, decision. Motion, Tab D, p. 15 (respondent’s affidavit, paragraph 6). He contends that because he could not read or understand our decision, he went to attorney Zaretsky for an explanation and was told that “everything was fine, and that [he] had nothing to worry about.” Ibid. Further, the respondent indicates that he did not pursue a motion to reopen because he never understood or was informed that he was subject to a removal order--that is until October 2014, when he brought his stepson to the office of his current attorney for immigration counseling. Ibid. at paragraph 7. He goes onto explain that during that counseling, his current attorney questioned him about his status, and advised that he was likely subject to a removal order.
 
*2 The evidence now presented is insufficient to demonstrate that the respondent exercised due diligence in discovering and pursuing his currently-articulated claims of ineffective assistance vis-à-vis his motion to reopen to rescind and Board appeal, and in filing a motion to reopen based on these claims. It appears that the last contact the respondent had with former counsel was in 2008. Although he maintains that attorney Zaretsky told him at that time that all was find and that he had nothing to worry about, the respondent makes no claim that he was told that he had actually acquired legal status and could lawfully remain in the United States, or that he personally believed that he had the legal right to remain in the United States, without further action or proceedings in his case. Absent such advice, or understandings, it is not unreasonable to expect the respondent to have made further inquiries about his case with his former attorney at some point after their meeting in 2008, and in the 7-year period that followed before his current motion to reopen was filed on April 13, 2015. On this record, there is no indication that the respondent took any action whatsoever regarding his case for several years.
 
Further, the respondent contends that it was in pursuing immigration counseling for his stepson that he first learned of his removal order, and that he received ineffective assistance from attorney Zaretsky. He has not explained why, in October 2014, he sought immigration counseling from a new attorney, and not attorney Zaretsky (on whose representation and counsel he previously relied, and about whose representation and counsel he had no reason to doubt at that point).
 
In addition, and notwithstanding his claims concerning immigration counseling for his stepson (during which he allegedly learned for the first time of the likelihood of his own removal order), it has not been established that the respondent actually has a stepson. Birth certificates have been submitted evidencing that the respondent is the father of two United States citizen children, respectively aged 11 and 7 years. Motion, Group Tab E. However, no documentation has been provided establishing that has a stepson, or is married. In addition, no affidavit was submitted from the respondent’s alleged stepson. Therefore, the respondent’s explanation for how and when he first came to learn that he was likely subject to a removal order, and was provided ineffective assistance by attorney Zaretsky, has not been adequately, and credibly, demonstrated by his motion submissions.
 
In the absence of demonstrating that equitable tolling applies to the 90-day filing deadline for the respondent’s motion to reopen, his motion remains untimely. We also decline to reopen the respondent’s proceedings sua sponte, as the evidence presented does not establish exceptional circumstances warranting such action. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*3 Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

Such motions are governed by their own rules, including a 180-day filing deadline if the alien claims his failure to appear was due to “exceptional circumstances” beyond his control. Id. Moreover, the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit has found that this 180-day filing deadline is subject to equitable tolling under certain circumstances. Avila-Santoyo v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 713 F.3d 1357, 1362 n. 4 (11th Cir. 2013).

2

For the reasons stated in our March 13, 2008, decision, we concluded that the respondent’s motion to reopen to rescind his in absentia removal order (which was based on a claim of lack of notice) was properly denied.

2015 WL 4873235 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DAMION IROD MICHAEL REID
File: AXXX XX9 240 - New York, NY
July 2, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fitzmore Harris, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 23, 2014, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his February 11, 2010, removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia under section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a reply to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent does not dispute that the Notice to Appear (NTA)(which contained the hearing notice for the February 11, 2010, hearing) was delivered to the correct address, but states that his spouse, with whom he resides, failed to give him the notice. The Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. When a hearing notice is delivered by mail but “does not reach the alien through some failure in the internal workings of the household, the alien can be charged with receiving proper notice.” Matter of M-D-, 23 I&N Dec. 540, 547 (BIA 2002); Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181, 189 (BIA 2001). Due process was satisfied here because the method of service was reasonably calculated to ensure that notice reached the alien. See Fuentes-Argueta v. INS, 101 F.3d 867 (2d Cir. 1996).
 
The respondent alleges that his former attorney also told him he did not have to appear for his hearing because a continuation request had been filed (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, page 3). Regarding exceptional circumstances for failing to appear, the Immigration Judge correctly found the motion to reopen was untimely (I.J. at 2). The respondent filed the motion almost four years after his in absentia order of removal became final, see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), (4)(ii), and he has not established the due diligence required for equitable tolling of the filing deadline (I.J. at 2-3). See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 131 (2d Cir. 2008).
 
Moreover, the respondent failed to substantially comply with the procedural requirements for bringing an ineffective assistance claim (I.J. at 3). The requirements include submitting (1) an affidavit describing in detail the agreement with counsel, (2) proof that counsel was informed of the allegations and had an opportunity to respond, and (3) a statement regarding whether a complaint was filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and, if not, why not. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). See also Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 47 (2d Cir. 2005) (holding that an “alien who has failed to comply substantially with the Lozada requirements ... forfeits her ineffective assistance of counsel claim.”). Here, the respondent did not submit any evidence that he informed counsel of the allegations against him and provide him with an opportunity to respond to the allegations.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent also argues that reopening was denied in error because he has new relief insofar as his U.S. citizen spouse has filed a visa petition on his behalf. Even if the respondent had not been given oral notice in his native language, in order to pursue an application for new relief, the respondent must comply with the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3). Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). Under these standards, the respondent’s May 21, 2014, motion to reopen is untimely, inasmuch as it was filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s February 11, 2010, decision, and he has not alleged that the motion would fall within any statutory or regulatory exception to the time limits. Hence, the respondent is also precluded from having his motion considered under the general time limitations for a motion to reopen.
 
The respondent has failed to establish that reopening is warranted pursuant to sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The respondent’s motion does not establish an “exceptional” situation sufficient to warrant reopening of his removal proceedings (I.J. at 3). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in her decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2015 WL 4873251 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAMI DOMINGUEZ-VITELA
File: AXXX XX3 827 - Phoenix, AZ
June 30, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marina N. Alexandrovich, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Cara O. Knapp
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s December 9, 2013, decision denying his motion to reopen. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded.
 
The respondent initially entered the United States in 1996. On January 17, 2008, U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services denied his application for adjustment of status. These removal proceedings were subsequently initiated on April 28, 2008 (Exh. 1). The respondent was represented by attorney Kevin Gibbons during the underlying removal proceedings.
 
At a March 6, 2009, master calendar hearing, the Immigration Judge set a January 3, 2011, deadline for the respondent to file his relief applications. The respondent did not comply with the filing deadline, and on January 20, 2011, the Immigration Judge deemed the respondent’s relief applications abandoned and ordered him removed from the United States to Mexico. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c) (the opportunity to file applications and related documents shall be deemed waived if not filed within the time set by the Immigration Judge). The Immigration Judge also vacated the next scheduled hearing on March 22, 2011.
 
On February 17, 2011, attorney Gibbons filed a timely motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, claiming that he did not know about the January 3, 2011, filing deadline. On March 9, 2011, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had not supplemented his motion with his relief applications as required by regulation. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). The Immigration Judge also deemed the respondent’s motion abandoned by regulation because he was physically removed from the United States on February 25, 2011. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(d), 1003.23(b)(i) (a motion to reopen or a motion to reconsider shall not be made by or on behalf of a person who is the subject of exclusion, deportation, or removal proceedings subsequent to his or her departure from the United States).
 
On November 15, 2013, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen through current counsel. The respondent claimed that reopening was warranted under the in absentia statutory and regulatory provisions, and also that proceedings should be reopened sua sponte, because he did not receive notice of the “““January 20, 2011 hearing.” The respondent asserted that he was ineffectively assisted by attorney Gibbons and provided evidence that attorney Gibbons had been suspended from practicing law in Arizona. On December 9, 2013, the Immigration Judge denied the motion. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s motion was numerically barred and that his prior motion was considered abandoned by regulation due to his removal. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(1), 1003.2(d). The Immigration Judge also explained that the respondent’s contention that he did not receive notice of a January 20, 2011, hearing was nonsensical because there was no hearing conducted on that date.
 
*2 The respondent appeals from this decision. The respondent continues to assert that he was ineffectively assisted by attorney Gibbons. He claims that he did not know that attorney Gibbons filed the first motion to reopen, and that he did not authorize that motion. He also claims that attorney Gibbons misled him into believing that he missed a hearing advanced from March 22, 2011, to January 20, 2011, when in reality there was no hearing on January 20, 2011. The respondent argues that under these circumstances, the numerical limitation on motions to reopen should not apply in his case and that his motion should be granted.
 
As previously noted, the respondent’s current counsel filed the second motion to reopen on November 15, 2013, arguing that attorney Gibbons committed ineffective assistance of counsel for failing to inform the respondent of a January 20, 2011 hearing. It is undisputed that there was no hearing on January 20, 2011; rather, the Immigration Judge issued his written decision on that date. Current counsel now argues on appeal that attorney Gibbons misled the respondent into believing that he missed a hearing on January 20, 2011.
 
Considering the totality of the circumstances and in the interest of justice, and without deciding the respondent’s allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel, we will reverse the Immigration Judge’s decision to the extent he declined to exercise his sua sponte authority to reopen the respondent’s case. See generally Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999). In the original motion to reopen, attorney Gibbons specifically admitted that he missed the January 2, 2011, filing deadline because of “an error by counsel’s office,” and asserted that he had misunderstood or had not heard the Immigration Judge. The Immigration Judge did not ever address this aspect of the respondent’s motion. We also observe that the record contains the respondent’s original application for adjustment of status, as well as an updated application filed in 2009 and a copy of the approved visa petition (Exh. 5). See Matter of Interiano-Rosa, 25 I&N Dec. 264 (BIA 2010) (holding that when an application for relief is timely filed but supporting documents are not submitted within the time established, the Immigration Judge may deem the opportunity to file the documents to be waived but may not deem the application itself abandoned).1 Finally, we note that the respondent’s removal from the United States did not preclude his ability initially to seek reopening within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. See Toor v. Lynch, - F.3d -, 2015 WL 3756503 (9th Cir. June 17, 2015); Reyes-Torres v. Holder, 645 F.3d 1073 (9th Cir. 2011); Coyt v. Holder, 593 F.3d 902 (9th Cir. 2010).
 
*3 Under these facts, we will sustain the respondent’s appeal and reverse the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to sua sponte reopen these removal proceedings. We express no view as to whether the respondent ultimately can demonstrate that he is eligible for and deserving of a grant of adjustment of status or other relief. Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained and the denial of his motion for sua sponte reopening of his removal proceedings is reversed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent submitted additional supporting documentation with his second motion to reopen.


2015 WL 4873251 (BIA)

2015 WL 4873226 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANTOS CHICAS MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX9 049 - Charlotte, NC
June 25, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mark J. Devine, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Scott D. Criss
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 8, 2014, denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reconsider. The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge did not consider the request for nunc pro tunc relief discussed in his motion to reopen. The respondent explains that the evidence relating to ineffective assistance of counsel was included in the motion to reopen to provide factual support for the equitable remedy he seeks.
 
The record reflects that the Immigration Judge issued a decision on January 16, 2014, granting the respondent pre-conclusion voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). The respondent filed a motion to reopen on March 21, 2014, seeking reopening on the basis of a nunc pro tunc equitable remedy. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, noting that the respondent was represented by counsel during his removal proceedings, and the respondent never expressed a fear of returning to El Salvador before the court. The Immigration Judge found that absent egregious circumstances, which are not present in this case, the respondent is bound by the tactical decisions and actions made by his attorney when such attorney is acting in her professional capacity. Matter of Gawaran, 20 I&N Dec. 938 (BIA 1995). See I.J. Dec. dated March 24, 2014.
 
The respondent claims that he was denied the opportunity to file an application for protection under the Convention Against Torture. However, the respondent was represented by counsel when he accepted a pre-conclusion voluntary departure order, and he waived appeal. On appeal and in his motion to reopen, the respondent suggests that his former counsel may have provided ineffective assistance, but he has not complied with any of the requirements discussed in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Moreover, the respondent did not establish any prejudice as a result of his prior attorney’s representation which would warrant reopening as an equitable remedy. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 561-62 (BIA 2003) (requiring prejudice stemming from prior counsel’s actions in addition to compliance with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra). The purpose of establishing ineffective assistance of counsel pursuant to Lozada would be rendered superfluous if the proceedings were reopened on the basis of a nunc pro tunc equitable remedy under the circumstances present in this case.1 The respondent did not file an application or other documents which establish a prima facie case for protection under the Convention Against Torture. The Immigration Judge correctly denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider as he did not identify any factual or legal errors in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2).
 
*2 We further find no basis for exercising our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings in this case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

“A nunc pro tunc order is an order that has retroactive legal effect.” Patel v. Gonzales, 432 F.3d 685, 693 (6th Cir. 2005).

2015 WL 4510729 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERGIO DELOYA-LEON
File: AXXX XX9 852 - Atlanta, GA
June 24, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Grace R. Kennedy, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider the Board’s March 26, 2015, decision, in which we denied his untimely and number-barred motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
In the motion, the respondent argues that the motion to reopen time period should be equitably tolled in his case, and that he exercised due diligence since the Board’s December 16, 2013, decision dismissing his appeal until he filed his motion to reopen on December 17, 2014. See Ruiz-Turcios v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 717 F.3d 847, 851 (11th Cir. 2013) (stating that equitable tolling generally requires a litigant to show “(1) that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way”) (citing Pace v. DiGuglielmo, 544 U.S. 408 (2005)). However, the respondent’s prior motion did not argue that the motion to reopen time period should be equitably tolled, or that the respondent has exercised diligence since the Board’s December 16, 2013, decision. A motion to reconsider based on arguments that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 58 (BIA 2006). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reconsider will be denied.1
 
To the extent the respondent now seeks to present the equitable tolling argument and has presented new documents, his motion constitutes a motion to reopen in substance. See Matter of 0-S-G-, supra, 57-58 (a motion to reconsider contests the correctness of the original decision based on the previous factual record, while a motion to reopen seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence). As a motion to reopen, the respondent’s motion is untimely and number-barred. Section 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000); Matter of L-V-K-, 22 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1999). Therefore, it will be denied as untimely and number-barred.
 
Furthermore, the arguments and evidence submitted do not show that equitable tolling of the motion time or number limitations is warranted. The respondent argues that, after he received the Board’s December 16, 2013, decision dismissing his appeal, he retained another lawyer and requested the DHS to file a joint motion to reopen, but the DHS denied this request on June 24, 2014; that he attempted to obtain an affidavit from his prior counsel, Joseph Azar, but attorney Azar merely kept promising and did not actually provide an affidavit; and that he eventually filed a bar complaint against attorney Azar on December 8, 2014, and filed his motion to reopen on December 17, 2014 (Motion, at 5-7; Motion Exh. A-C).
 
*2 The motion and the respondent’s affidavit submitted with the motion do not identify the new lawyer who represented him in June 2014, but the DHS’s June 24, 2014, letter declining to join in a motion to reopen was addressed to his current counsel, indicating that the respondent was represented by his current counsel at that time (Motion Exh. C). Therefore, the letter shows that the respondent was no longer being represented by attorney Azar but was being represented by his current counsel in June 2014.2 Nevertheless, the motion to reopen was not filed until December 2014, about 6 months later. Moreover, requesting the DHS to join in a motion to reopen does not necessarily show diligence in pursuing the ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and the respondent offers no explanation why he could not file a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel while requesting the DHS’s help. Removable aliens should not delay matters by pursuing multiple avenues of relief seriatim when they could have been pursued simultaneously. See, e.g., Galvez Pineda v. Gonzales, 427 F.3d 833, 839 (10th Cir. 2005).
 
The respondent also argues that his motion to reopen was further delayed because he was trying to obtain an affidavit from attorney Azar. However, the respondent does not argue that obtaining and submitting such an affidavit is one of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (see Motion, at 9). The respondent fails to explain why such an affidavit was necessary before he could file a bar complaint against the attorney or a motion to reopen based on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. In any event, the respondent eventually filed a bar complaint and the motion to reopen without having to obtain an affidavit from the prior attorney. Based on the above, the respondent did not show that he has been pursuing his ineffective assistance of counsel claim diligently, or that he was prevented by “some extraordinary circumstance” from filing a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. See Ruiz-Turcios v. U.S. Atty. Gen., supra. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
The respondent reiterates his request for us to sua sponte reopen his proceedings under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a) and to administratively close his proceedings to allow him to seek consular processing of his visa petition. We do not find that the respondent’s case presents an exceptional situation that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
*3 Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

C; Mar. 19, 2012 I.J. Motion Affidavit). The respondent now asserts in his statement submitted with the current motion (the “third statement”) that the first statement was prepared by attorney Azar and he signed it without reading it or knowing its contents (Motion Exh. A, at 6-7). However, the respondent fails to explain why this explanation could not be presented with his prior motion to reopen.
In any event, the respondent’s accounts provided in his second and third statements are not supported by the record in this case. The respondent stated in his second statement that at a hearing on September 13, 2011, the Immigration Judge gave his attorney the notice of the December 14, 2011, hearing (Dec. 17, 2014, Motion Exh. C, at 5). In his third statement, the respondent states that the Immigration Judge was not present at the September 13, 2011, hearing and the clerk of the Immigration Court gave his attorney the notice of hearing (Motion Exh. A, at 5-6; Dec. 17, 2014, Motion Exh. C, at 5). However, the record shows that the respondent was presented by a different attorney, not attorney Azar, at his prior hearings, and the notices of hearing were issued to the other attorney.

2015 WL 4510727 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUZ GUADALUPE TORRES-AREVALO
File: AXXX XX8 223 - Dallas, TX
June 24, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose Zamudio Marin, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(B)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(B)(i)(I)] - Nonimmigrant - no valid passport
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated March 09, 2015. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s February 17, 2015, motion to reopen the proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on December 3, 2014, pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A) (I.J. at 1-3). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5). We write separately to supplement the Immigration Judge’s decision with a few additional points, in light of the respondent’s appeal. First, the respondent acknowledges that she had actual notice for the December 3, 2014, hearing (I.J. at 2; Respondent’s Br. at 9). Second, the respondent claims in her declaration that she relied on the advice of a lawyer, whom she characterized as “my lawyer” (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tab C), not to appear for the December 3, 2014, hearing. She claims that the lawyer advised her “agent,” who then advised her, that the December 3, 2014, hearing would be “nullified” as a result of her release from custody on November 25, 2014 (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tab C).
 
The lawyer tells a different story, however. According to the lawyer’s declaration, he was not retained to represent the respondent at the time the respondent’s agent contacted him about the December 3, 2014, hearing (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tab E). Rather, the lawyer’s declaration reflects that he was only retained to represent the respondent in connection with her credible fear interview on October 31, 2014 (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tab E). This statement, we note, is corroborated by the fact that the lawyer did not enter an appearance on the respondent’s behalf at the November 24, 2014, master calendar hearing (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Tabs C, E).
 
While we have recognized that a lawyer’s affirmative direction not to appear for a scheduled hearing can constitute exceptional circumstances justifying the rescission of an in absentia removal order, such a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must comply with the requirements delineated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
*2 In this case, the respondent has not set forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with her former counsel, and in fact, the record shows that representation in these proceedings was not within the scope of the attorney-client agreement. Nor has a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities against the respondent’s former counsel; the respondent, in fact, has not even acknowledged the existence of this requirement (Respondent’s M.T.R. at 2-3). See Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 496 (5th Cir. 2000) (recognizing that Lozada’s “requirement regarding disciplinary allegations served both to deter meritless claims and to aid in policing the immigration bar” (citing Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639)).
 
Insofar as the respondent has not established a viable claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, she has not established exceptional circumstances warranting rescission of the in absentia removal order. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
