U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HIRAM ABECARDO GONZALEZ-SARAT
File: AXXX XX4 431 - San Antonio, TX
June 24, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated March 17, 2014, denying his motion to reopen. We will dismiss the appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. These removal proceedings were completed when, on October 28, 2013, the respondent was granted the privilege of voluntarily departure. As such, the respondent’s motion to reopen, filed on February 26, 2014, was untimely (I.J. at 1). See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of Goolcharan, 23 I&N Dec. 5 (BIA 2001).
 
Inasmuch as the respondent’s request for equitable tolling was not presented to the Immigration Judge, the request is not properly before this Board for the purposes of appellate review. See, e.g., Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629, 638 (BIA 2003); Matter of Jimenez, 21 I&N Dec. 567, 570 n.2 (BIA 1996); Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984). Moreover, the respondent’s generalized references to having pursued his rights diligently, without identifying specific actions that he actually took, do not persuade this Board that equitable tolling would be warranted, had it been properly presented to the Immigration Judge for consideration (Respondent’s Br. at 4-6). See Holland v. Florida, 560 U.S. 631, 649 (2010) (holding that equitable tolling is warranted only if the movant shows that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way and prevented timely filing); Irwin v. Dep’t of Veterans Affairs, 498 U.S. 89, 96 (1990).
 
Finally, we have considered the totality of the circumstances presented in this case, including the respondent’s claims to having been provided ineffective assistance of counsel and that he is eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1). Nonetheless, we are not persuaded that he has demonstrated that an exceptional situation exists which would warrant the sua sponte reopening of these proceedings. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 For the reasons set forth above, we will not disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 4510726 (BIA)

2015 WL 4510746 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AUGUSTIN CALDERON-LUGO
File: AXXX XX2 999 - San Antonio, TX
June 18, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul A. Esquivel, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 10, 2014, denying his motion to reopen to apply for asylum under section 208 of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1158, withholding of removal under section 241(b)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(b)(3), and protection under the Convention Against Torture (CAT), 8 C.F.R. § 1208.16. The appeal will be dismissed.1
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003(d)(3).
 
The record reflects that the respondent was granted post-conclusion voluntary departure on May 7, 2013, to depart on or before September 4, 2013. On December 16, 2013, the respondent filed a motion to reopen to apply for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, finding that the respondent did not establish ineffective assistance of counsel and he did not establish prima facie eligibility for asylum, withholding of removal, or for protection under the Convention Against Torture (I.J. at 2).
 
On appeal, counsel for the respondent states that he has attempted to obtain the necessary information from the respondent’s two previous attorneys to determine whether he can proceed with a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, but he has not been provided the file from either of the attorneys. The respondent indicates that he is attempting to compile the necessary evidence for a viable non-frivolous complaint, and that he will submit supplements, including a completed complaint with the proper agencies upon accumulation of evidence. To date, the respondent has not submitted any additional evidence in support of his claim that he would be pursuing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim and he has not complied with the procedural requirements pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). The respondent has not submitted evidence that he has notified his former attorney of his ineffective assistance allegations; and there is no evidence that a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authority. Matter of Lozada, supra. Consequently, the respondent has not established that reopening is warranted on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent seeks reopening due to his fear of returning to Mexico, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established changed circumstances or changed country conditions to warrant reopening of proceedings. The respondent was represented by counsel when he requested and was granted voluntary departure, after being fully advised of the consequences if he failed to depart. The alleged failure of former counsel to inform the respondent of the availability of asylum, withholding of removal, and of protection under the Convention Against Torture does not create “changed country conditions” or “previously unavailable evidence” when the basis for the asylum claim arose before the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 7, 2013. The respondent’s fear of harm could have been presented at his previous hearing. There also has been no prima facie showing that upon return to Mexico the respondent would face torture by or with the consent or acquiescence of government officials (to include the concept of willful blindness) for the purposes of the CAT. In light of the foregoing, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established that reopening is warranted to consider his applications for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the CAT. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16(c)(2), 1208.18(a)(1). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Despite the respondent’s claim that he has been unable to obtain an audio recording of his underlying removal proceedings, we decline to order a transcript of these proceedings to be prepared or further extend the briefing schedule in this matter. A transcript is not ordinarily prepared where a motion to reopen is filed, since the motion will be decided on the basis of its merits rather than on the basis of a review of the record. See Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1996) (noting transcript ordinarily not present when motion to reopen filed).

2015 WL 4510769 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MANDEEP SINGH
File: AXXX XX6 739 - Harlingen, TX
June 12, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eileen Collins Bretz, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On November 8, 2011, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent’s removal in absentia, and on January 17, 2012, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(4)(ii), 1003.26. The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision on May 9, 2013. This case was last before the Board on July 26, 2013, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our decision. The respondent has now filed another motion to reopen the removal proceedings to rescind the in absentia order of removal to enable him to apply for asylum and withholding of removal. The motion will be denied.
 
It is undisputed that the respondent failed to appear at his scheduled removal hearing. The respondent, however, now raises an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against Norma Sepulveda, Esquire, who represented him before the Immigration Judge. He also raises an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against Laura Perez, Esquire, who filed his motion to reopen and rescind with the Immigration Judge, and his appeal and motion to reconsider with the Board, for failing to raise an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against Attorney Sepulveda.
 
The respondent has attempted to comply with the procedural requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims before the Board. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). He has not, however, shown that prior counsel rendered deficient performance resulting in prejudice. Id. See also Miranda-Lores v. I.N.S., 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994) (“To prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel at a deportation proceeding, an alien must show (1) ineffective representation and (2) substantial prejudice, which occurred as a result of the ineffective representation”); Ogbemudia v. I.N.S., 988 F.2d 595, 598 (5th Cir. 1993).
 
The respondent alleges that Attorney Sepulveda did not properly inform him of the consequences of the failure to appear, and did not provide him with notice of the removal hearing. The Notice to Appear, however, reveals that on June 15, 2011, the respondent was provided oral notice in the Punjabi language of the consequences of the failure to appear. See Exhibit 1. The record further reveals that notice of the November 8, 2011, hearing, was properly served on the respondent’s counsel, Attorney Sepulveda, who in response to the allegations against her, claims that she attempted to contact the respondent to no avail at his address of record, i.e., the address provided by his bond obligor. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (recognizing that notice to the attorney of record constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5 (notice of hearing sent to an alien’s attorney of record is sufficient notice). Indeed, the respondent states in the instant motion that “[a]lthough (he) had contact with his bond obligor, his bond obligor did not notify him of his hearing until he received notice from ICE requesting that he deliver (the respondent) at a set time and date.” See Respondent’s Motion at 5. Given this record, it has not been shown that the respondent’s failure to appear at his removal hearing was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of Attorney Sepulveda. As the respondent has not shown ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of Attorney Sepulveda, his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against Attorney Perez lacks merit and will not be considered further.
 
*2 In sum, we are not persuaded to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order based on the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claims. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Further, as previously found, the respondent has not shown that his failure to appear at the November 8, 2011, removal hearing was the result of “exceptional circumstances” as defined at section 240(e)(1) of the Act. We also are not persuaded to reopen these proceedings to enable the respondent to apply for asylum and related relief as he has not set forth a claim of materially changed circumstances or conditions in his native country, India. See Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161 (BIA 2013); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii). Finally, the respondent has not set forth a case of exceptional circumstances warranting reopening in the exercise of our sua sponte authority. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 4510769 (BIA)

2015 WL 4537063 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN ISAC RIVERA-LOPES A.K.A. LUIS IDROVO URGILES A.K.A. MAURICIO TRELLES
File: AXXX XX8 905 - New York, NY
June 12, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brenna Torres, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s June 9, 2014, decision that denied his motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. The record before us does not contain a brief from the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that he did not receive proper notice for the October 6, 2000, hearing. We affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge.1 The respondent does not submit a sworn affidavit or an unsworn declaration under penalty of perjury, or satisfactorily explain why not. Leaving aside the “false name” issue, the respondent was obligated to provide to the Immigration Court an address at which he may be contacted regarding removal proceedings, and also promptly provide any change of address (Notice to Appear (Form I-862)). See sections 239(a)(1) and (2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229(a)(1) and (2). The respondent’s 2000 Federal income tax return (Motion Exh. G, at 73-75) was filed listing a Southampton, New York, address, and the W-2 lists an East Hampton, New York, address. He does not provide any evidence to show that he was living at the New York, New York, address [provided in his motion to change venue and provided for the Notice to EOIR: Alien Address (Form I-830)] during June of 2000 when the notice of hearing was sent.
 
To the extent that the respondent alleges ineffective assistance of former counsel (Gary Ortega), he does not satisfy any of the three requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) to properly make a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent requested reopening and administrative closure of his case. We look to the factors discussed in Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012). The reasons administrative closure is sought are not persuasive. The respondent can apply to the DHS for prosecutorial discretion without these proceedings being administratively closed, and voluntary departure is not a new form of relief. The DHS opposed the motion to reopen on the merits. Even if the respondent pursues prosecutorial discretion, he has not shown a likelihood of success. The delay from October of 2000 until March of 2014 is due to the respondent. He does not show that there would be a different ultimate outcome when the case is recalendared before the Immigration Judge or the appeal is reinstated before the Board. The factors in Matter of Avetisyan, supra, weigh against administrative closure.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We do not affirm one portion of the Immigration Judge’s decision. The DHS alleged in its opposition that the respondent had one arrest, and the Immigration Judge observed in his decision that the respondent did not respond to the allegation. However, the DHS did not submit any evidence of the arrest, and he now submits a certificate of disposition [which does not warrant a remand] which shows that the criminal record was sealed.

2015 WL 4510768 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAFAEL MARTINEZ A.K.A. CARLOS ANTONIO TOBON-LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX5 380 - Hartford, CT
June 12, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ramiro Alcazar, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; termination of proceedings
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 10, 2014. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable and ordered him removed. The respondent challenges denial of a continuance and challenges removability on appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was placed in proceedings on April 5, 2012. His first hearing was on May 14, 2012. He was granted a continuance to find an attorney (Tr. at 13). His second hearing was on September 10, 2012. He was granted a continuance for attorney preparation (Tr. at 15). On December 3, 2012, the respondent (through counsel) said he is not “Rafael Martinez,” denied all the factual allegations in the Notice to Appear (NTA), denied the charges of removability, and indicated that he intended to file a motion to suppress evidence and terminate proceedings. The Immigration Judge granted him a continuance to file that motion (Tr. at 19-21; Exhs. 2, 4-5).
 
On February 14, 2013, the respondent (through counsel) withdrew the motion to suppress evidence and terminate proceedings and withdrew his objection to consideration of the Record of Deportable/Inadmissible Alien (Form I-213), excepting the name of the respondent (Tr. at 48-50, 58-60, 63-66, 80, 94; I.J. at 2-3). On March 7, 2013, after the NTA was amended to add the respondent’s true name, the respondent admitted proper service of the NTA, admitted the factual allegations, conceded removability, designated Mexico for removal, and requested voluntary departure (Tr. at 58-60, 63-66; Exh. 6; I.J. at 2-3). The respondent was then granted continuances to pursue Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (DACA) and prosecutorial discretion (Tr. at 67-69; I.J. at 2; Exhs. 7-8). On March 10, 2014, the respondent indicated that DACA and prosecutorial discretion had been denied (Tr. at 73-74, 79, 92, 96-97; I.J. at 2).
 
The respondent (through counsel) then indicated that he was not seeking voluntary departure or other relief, indicated that he was amending the pleadings again to deny that proper service of the NTA was made and to deny the charges of removability, and he requested another continuance (Tr. at 97-98, 100-101; I.J. at 2-3). The Immigration Judge denied that request (I.J. at 3). The Immigration Judge found removability proved, regardless of the pleadings, based on the Form I-213 (Exh. 3) and ordered the respondent removed (I.J. at 3-4).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent1 challenges denial of the continuance. An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009). We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We agree that the respondent did not show good cause for another continuance.
 
Also on appeal, the respondent disputes removability. The respondent attempts to raise on appeal the issues raised in the motion to suppress evidence and terminate proceedings that he voluntarily withdrew before the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 48-50, 58-60, 63-66, 80, 94; I.J. at 2-3). The respondent argues that, despite the withdrawal of the motion, despite his withdrawal of objection to the Form I-213, despite his admission of the factual allegations and despite his concession of removability, he is not, in fact, removable. We find, however, that the prior withdrawal of the motion, withdrawal of objection to the Form I-213, admissions of fact, and concessions of removability are binding on the respondent. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986) (absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal concession made by counsel is binding on the alien). Cf. Hoodho v. Holder, 558 F.3d 184, 191 (2d Cir. 2009) (holding that facts admitted by a party are “judicial admissions” that bind that party throughout the litigation). Moreover, we note that the respondent does not assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

We note that the same counsel that represented the respondent below represents the respondent on appeal.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DUY A. NGUYEN
File: AXXX XX4 732 - Atlanta, GA
June 10, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Charles H. Kuck, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sirce E. Owen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on March 12, 2015, when we denied the respondent’s untimely and number-barred motion to reopen. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider our decision denying his motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion does not convince us that reconsideration of our previous decision is warranted. A party seeking reconsideration requests that the original decision be reexamined in light of alleged legal or factual errors, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). Here, the respondent largely raises the same or similar arguments in the motion to reconsider as were raised in his prior motion to reopen. We considered those arguments and found them unpersuasive when viewed within the record as a whole before we determined that the respondent had not established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and denied his motion to reopen. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 58 (stating that “a motion to reconsider is not a process by which a party may submit, in essence, the same brief presented on appeal...”). Disagreement with the result is not sufficient.
 
To the extent that the respondent is attempting to raise new arguments related to his compliance with the requirements laid out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec, 637 (BIA 1988), these arguments are not properly raised for the first time in a motion to reconsider. See O-S-G-, supra, at 58 (stating that “[a] motion to reconsider based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied” and “arguments for consideration on appeal should all be submitted at one time, rather than in piecemeal fashion.”) (citation omitted). A motion to reconsider is neither a vehicle for advancing supplemental legal arguments that could have been previously raised nor a mechanism for submitting a late-filed brief. Moreove, the respondent did not file his bar complaint until after the Board issued its March 12, 2015, decision, finding that the respondent did not substantially comply with Matter of Lozada, supra. Regardless, none of the arguments or evidence presented, including the response from the respondent’s prior counsel, Mr. Fogel, convinces us of error in our ultimate determination that the respondent has not established prejudice by any actions or inactions of his former counsel (Motion at Tab I). Therefore, the motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

2015 WL 4537102 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN GOMEZ-ALONZO
File: AXXX XX9 408 - Chicago, IL
June 4, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Royal F. Berg, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anastasie M. Senat
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s April 7, 2014, denial of his motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed. The respondent’s requests for oral argument, and en banc or 3-member panel review, are denied.
 
The Board reviews Immigration Judges’ findings of fact, including credibility determinations, for clear error, and we review all other matters on appeal de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
“A motion to reopen will not be granted unless the Immigration Judge is satisfied that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). Here, the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen as the respondent did not satisfy the requirements of reopening. See Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
There is no question that the evidence was previously available. The respondent could have presented the evidence at the former hearing, but did not do so because he expressed no concerns about returning to Guatemala. Since the respondent did not comply with the requirements of reopening, the Immigration Judge properly denied the motion.
 
In addition, as noted by the Immigration Judge, the DHS considered the respondent’s request for prosecutorial discretion and denied the request. Neither the Immigration Judge, nor this Board, has authority to exercise prosecutorial discretion in the respondent’s case. See Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281, 284 (BIA 1998). A request for prosecutorial discretion is a collateral matter, and the Immigration Judge did not need to continue the proceedings to allow the respondent to make such a request to the DHS. See generally Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348 (BIA 1982) (explaining that prosecutorial discretion is a function of the DHS; and that it was not error for the Immigration Judge to decline to continue the hearing for the alien to apply for a form of prosecutorial discretion for his or her case). Moreover, the respondent has not shown he was prejudiced by the continuance denial, as he has not shown he has been granted prosecutorial discretion by DHS; nor has he shown that he is eligible for any forms of relief from removal. See Bathula v. Holder, 723 F.3d 889, 903 n. 34 (7th Cir. 2013); Rapheal v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 521, 533 (7th Cir. 2008).
 
*2 The respondent contends that his due process rights were violated because, inter alia, he may not have been adequately advised regarding his avenues of relief. We disagree. In order to establish a denial of the right to due process, the respondent must show that he was prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s actions. See Hasanaj v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 780, 785 (7th Cir. 2004); Wigglesworth v. INS, 319 F.3d 951, 960 (7th Cir. 2003). Here, the respondent was represented by counsel at his last two hearings and a Spanish interpreter was provided at all hearings. The motion was properly denied because while the respondent provided his statement and his Form I-589 application, the respondent did not establish that the alleged facts were new and previously unavailable. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3).
 
We also find without merit the respondent’s contention that he should have been able to withdraw pleadings. See generally Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986) (a respondent is bound by the tactical decisions made by his counsel). The respondent has not alleged ineffective assistance of counsel; nor has he complied with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent requests a transcription of the proceedings. The request is denied. Normally, a transcription of the hearing is not ordered from the appeal of a denial of a motion to reopen. If a review of the record indicates that transcription is important to adjudicate the appeal, the transcript will be ordered. Here, we are not persuaded that a transcript is necessary to resolve this appeal, because the digital audio recording of the hearing is available. Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 4537102 (BIA)

2015 WL 4537109 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WAYNE LEWIS
File: AXXX XX9 875 - New York, NY
June 3, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Audrey A. Thomas, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Trinidad and Tobago, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s September 9, 2014, decision which denied his motion to rescind his in absentia removal order. The appeal will be dismissed.1
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the credibility determination, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues involving questions of law, judgment and discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in federal or state custody and failed to appear through no fault of her own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or death of an immediate relative, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1). In determining whether exceptional circumstances exist to excuse an alien’s failure to appear, the “totality of circumstances” pertaining to the alien’s case must be considered. Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503, 509 (BIA 1996).
 
The respondent received personal service of the Notice to Appear on October 28, 2008 (Exh. 1). On November 6, 2008, the respondent appeared, conceded removability as charged in the Notice to Appear, and indicated his intention to apply for adjustment of status. On September 23, 2009, he failed to appear and was ordered removed in absentia. On November 2, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, which was granted. On September 15, 2010, he appeared with counsel who requested a continuance which was granted, and the matter was reset until September 29, 2010. He failed to appear on September 29, 2010, reportedly because he experienced a panic attack after seeing immigration officers and departed the building. The Immigration Judge adjourned the matter until October 1, 2010.
 
*2 Following a hearing conducted in absentia on October 1, 2010, at which the respondent failed to appear and the Department of Homeland Security (DBS) presented evidence, an Immigration Judge found him subject to removal as charged in the Notice to Appear, and ordered him removed from the United States.2 See generally section 240 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a. His counsel appeared and indicated that she did not know the respondent’s location. She requested to withdraw as counsel in February 2011.
 
On April 29, 2014, the respondent, through new counsel, filed a motion to rescind his in absentia removal order. He alleged that he did not appear on October 1, 2010, because his attorney did not inform him of the October 1, 2010, hearing date, that he was experiencing financial difficulties, and essentially was homeless at the time. The respondent indicated that he did not attempt to contact his previous attorney because he owed her legal fees and believed that she would not speak to him.
 
The DHS filed a response in opposition to the motion to reopen, noting that the motion failed to address why the respondent did not appear on September 29, 2010, and did not offer any medical evidence to excuse his failure to appear. In addition, the DHS observed that notice to the respondent’s attorney for the hearings on September 29, and October 1, 2010, constituted effective notice to him, and that he had not filed an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his prior attorney.
 
On September 9, 2014, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to rescind the in absentia removal order because the respondent had failed to explain why he did not appear on September 29, 2010. She noted that his prior counsel had informed her on September 29, 2010, that the respondent did not appear because he had a panic attack after seeing immigration officers at the Immigration Court and had visited an emergency clinic. However, the respondent had not submitted any medical evidence to support this contention with his motion to reopen (I.J. at 3). In addition, the Immigration Judge noted that the respondent had not complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) to establish an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and had failed to establish due diligence by waiting almost 4 years to file a motion to reopen with any explanation for the delay (Id.). Finally, the Immigration Judge determined that notice to the respondent’s counsel for the October 1, 2010, hearing constituted effective notice to him, and that he had not established exceptional circumstances to excuse his failure to appear (I.J. at 4).
 
The respondent submitted a Notice of Appeal which contained a short statement of reasons which alleged without elaboration that the Immigration Judge erred in her decision. His brief claimed that he was sick on September 29, 2010, and was vomiting, dizzy and had diarrhea. However, the brief does not address why he did not appear on October 1, 2010, and it does not reiterate the contention made in the motion to reopen that he failed to appear because his attorney did not inform him of the hearing date. Although he filed a brief, he requested and received an extension of the filing deadline to submit a supplemental brief with medical evidence. His request for a second extension was denied. He has not submitted any medical evidence to date.
 
*3 The respondent does not contest that he is subject to removal as charged, and he does not allege any error in the Immigration Judge’s decision that he failed to establish that his in absentia deportation order should be rescinded. In addition, his brief on appeal indicates that he did not appear because he was sick, which contradicts the claim in his motion that he did not appear because his prior attorney did not inform him of the hearing date.
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. Service of the Notice of Hearing on the alien’s representative is proper. Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (notice to alien’s counsel constitutes notice to alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). The respondent has not established that his absence at the scheduled hearing was the result of a lack of notice because his prior attorney received proper notice of the hearing. Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996) (alien seeking to reopen in absentia proceedings based upon her unsuccessful communications with attorney about hearing date did not establish exceptional circumstances where she failed to satisfy all of requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
To the extent that the respondent claimed that his prior attorney (who represents him on appeal) purportedly did not tell him he had a hearing on October 1, 2010, this allegation, without more, does not constitute ineffective assistance of counsel, especially in light of the respondent’s concession that he did not remain in contact with his attorney. In addition, the respondent could have contacted the Immigration Court directly. Furthermore, he has not complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) to establish ineffective assistance of counsel because he has not filed a state bar complaint.
 
Moreover, for the reasons stated in the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent did not establish that his absence at the scheduled hearing was the result of exceptional circumstances, as required by section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. See section 240(e)(1) of the Act. See also Matter of B-A-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 1998) (“twisted foot” does not constitute exceptional circumstance); Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33 (BIA 1998) (strong headache did not constitute exceptional circumstance). See also Cabrera v. Gonzales, 229 Fed. Appx. 483 (9th Cir. 2007) (stomach infection, diarrhea and abdominal pain did not constitute exceptional circumstances or serious illness sufficient to merit reopening of in absentia order). To the extent that the respondent alleges that his panic attack on September 29, 2010, constituted an exceptional circumstance to excuse his failure to appear, he has not provided any medical evidence to substantiate his claim, or alleged that a panic attack on September 29, 2010, also was the reason he failed to appear on October 1, 2010. In addition, he failed to address or explain, either in his motion or on appeal, why he waited almost 4 years to file his motion to reopen.
 
*4 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent has filed a request for a stay of removal. Given our disposition in this order the stay of removal is moot.

2

On April 7, 2010, the DHS filed lodged charges under section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), alleging that the respondent had been convicted of two crimes involving moral turpitude not arising out of a single scheme of criminal misconduct. On September 9, 2014, the Immigration Judge did not sustain this charge because the DHS had not submitted evidence of the record of conviction for one of the convictions (I.J. at 2).

2015 WL 3896291 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARMANDO RAMIREZ-CASTANEDA1
File: AXXX XX9 596 - York, PA
May 28, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Elizabeth M. Trinidad, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
William E. Lore
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the April 25, 2013, decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings.2 The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) requests summary affirmance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge did not consider Leslie v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 611 F.3d 171 (3d Cir. 2010), or certain defects in the Notice to Appeal. We affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. The Immigration Judge’s findings of fact and analysis support his conclusion that the respondent did not satisfy his burden of proof as it relates to his motion to reopen (I.J. at 2-3).
 
Citing Leslie v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge committed reversible error, as there is no evidence that the Immigration Judge gave him a pro bono list of legal service providers (Resp. Br. at 5, 7-10). We disagree. The respondent does not deny that he was personally served with the Notice to Appear (“NTA”) (Form I-862) or that the NTA bears his signature. While the respondent argues that he may have been in deportation proceedings, the Immigration Judge found that “the original and only charging document in this case was a Notice to Appear, Form I-862, and dated February 17, 1998” (I.J. at 2). Further, the Immigration Judge reasonably noted that the NTA’s Certificate of Service indicates that the “respondent was provided the pro bono list” (I.J. at 2, n.2).
 
A motion to reopen may not be granted, unless the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4); Matter of Ige, 20 I&N Dec. 880, 884 (BIA 1994). The United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit (“Third Circuit”), in whose jurisdiction this case arises, has found that “the prima facie case standard for a motion to reopen . . . requires the applicant to produce objective evidence showing a ‘reasonable likelihood’ that he or she can establish” entitlement to relief. Guo v. Ashcroft, 386 F.3d 556, 563 (3d Cir. 2004) (citation omitted). Moreover, the Third Circuit has explained that “[p]rima facie scrutiny entails consideration of ‘the evidence that accompanies the motion as well as relevant evidence that may exist in the record of the prior hearing, in light of the applicable statutory requirements for relief.”DD’ Id. at 563 n.7 (citation omitted).
 
*2 The respondent has not established that these proceedings should be reopened on account of lack of proper notice. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent argues on appeal that the NTA may not have been signed by someone authorized to do so (Resp. Br. at 6, 12-13). However, as the respondent has not shown that any statute or regulation prohibits another immigration officer from signing on behalf of the issuing officer listed on the Notice to Appear, it follows that he has not shown that it constitutes a violation of a regulation. In addition, there is a presumption that public officers, including DHS employees, properly discharge their duties. See U. S. v. Chemical Foundation, Inc., 272 U.S. 1, 14-15 (1926); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27, 37 (BIA 1995) (citations omitted).
 
On this record, the respondent’s argument is insufficient to rebut the presumption of regularity. There is also no evidence that the respondent was prejudiced by the alleged defect in the Notice to Appear. The Notice to Appear included the allegations and charge against him and the alleged defect in the Notice to Appear did not prejudice the respondent because it did not interfere with his understanding of the charge against him or his ability to respond to that charge. See e.g., Kohli v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 1061 (9th Cir. 2007) (finding the Board complied with the regulations that vest jurisdiction in the Immigration Court and the alleged defect in the NTA did not deprive the Immigration Court of jurisdiction).
 
We concur with the Immigration Judge that the NTA was not defective for lacking the date, time, and place of the hearing (I.J. at 2). The respondent asserts that the NTA contravenes section 239(a)(1)(G) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1129(a)(1)(G) because the form itself does not state the date, time and place for the commencement of proceedings (Resp. Br. at 12-14). The respondent maintains that the NTA is thus defective. Contrary to the respondent’s appellate contentions, the absence of a specific date, time, and place for the initial removal hearing in the NTA does not render it “defective” (Resp. Br. at 12-14). See Matter of Camarillo, 25 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 2011). As noted in Matter of Camarillo, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.18(b) permits the NTA to include such scheduling information “where practicable.” The Immigration Judge reasonably determined that the respondent’s arguments are also undercut by the subsequent notice of hearing sent to the respondent’s counsel of record (I.J. at 2). Popa v. Holder, 571 F.3d 890 (9th Cir. 2009) (holding that a NTA that failed to include a date or time of alien’s deportation hearing but states that a date and time will be set later, is not defective as long as the notice of hearing is in fact later sent to the alien). In view of the circumstances presented, we are not persuaded by the arguments on appeal that the NTA was defective and jurisdiction was not conferred to the court.
 
*3 Finally, even if the NTA was defective, the violation of a regulation does not necessarily invalidate a removal proceeding. See Matter of Hernandez, 21 I&N Dec. 224, 226 (BIA 1996). Rather, we have held that the violation of a regulatory requirement invalidates a proceeding only where the regulation provides a benefit to the alien and the violation prejudiced the interest of the alien who was to be protected by the regulation. See Matter of Garcia-Flores, 17 I&N Dec. 325 (BIA 1996). The respondent does not contend that he is in this country pursuant to a lawful admission and is not subject to removal from the United States, nor does he allege that he is currently eligible for any form of relief from removal. Thus, he has not demonstrated that the regulation provides a benefit to him or that he was prejudiced by the individual signing the NTA.
 
The respondent moves that the proceedings be administratively closed so that he may apply for prosecutorial discretion before the DHS (Resp. Br. at 15-18). See Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012) (setting forth the factors to consider in determining whether administrative closure of a proceeding is appropriate even when it is opposed by one of the parties). The exercise of prosecutorial discretion is a matter within the authority of the DHS, not of the Immigration Judge or the Board.3 See Matter of Bahta, 22 I&N Dec. 1381, 1391 (BIA 2000) (observing that the DHS has prosecutorial discretion, which includes the discretion to address the equities of individual cases in a manner that the rigid application of a broadly drawn statute often will not allow); see also Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281 (BIA 1998); Matter of Singh, 21 I&N Dec. 427, 435 (BIA 1996); Matter of Yazdani, 17 I&N Dec. 626, 630 (BIA 1981). As a general matter, the DHS is in the best position to assess its own priorities and whether its prosecutorial discretion should be exercised in a particular case. The potential exercise of discretion does not represent an “immigration benefit” over which the Immigration Court or this Board has jurisdiction or to which a respondent may claim entitlement.4 See generally, section 240 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a; 8 C.F.R. § § 1003.1(b), (d)(1)(i). The DHS has not joined in the respondent’s motion. Neither the Board nor the Immigration Judge has jurisdiction to compel the DHS to exercise prosecutorial discretion which may be granted at any stage of the proceedings. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2009) (holding that the Board lacks the authority to reopen a proceeding to allow an alien an opportunity to seek relief from removal if the Board and the Immigration Judge have no authority to grant the requested form of relief, especially where reopening is sought simply as a mechanism to stay the final order while the collateral matter is resolved by the agency or court having jurisdiction to do so).
 
*4 The Immigration Judge did not err in denying the respondent’s motion to terminate proceedings. The Immigration Judge only has the authority delegated to him or her by the Attorney General. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.10(b). It is the function of the Immigration Judge to adjudicate the case and not to determine when or whether charges are brought. See 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(a) and (b); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.1(a) (authority of the Immigration Judge). The initiation of removal proceedings through the issuance of a Notice to Appear is covered by section 239 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229; see 8 C.F.R. §§ 2.1, 239.2(a) issuance of Notice to Appear). The DHS, not an Immigration Judge, has prosecutorial discretion to commence proceedings. Matter of Bahta, 22 I&N Dec. 1381, 1391 (BIA 2000) (INS has unreviewable prosecutorial discretion); Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281 (BIA 1998); Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 350 (BIA 1982). Further, section 240 of the Act confers prosecutorial discretion upon the DHS to commence proceedings, and once proceedings have been commenced, it is the responsibility of the Immigration Judge to adjudicate them. See Matter of Lujan-Quintana, 25 I&N Dec. 53, 56 (BIA 2009) (“DHS has inherent flexibility, in the exercise of prosecutorial discretion, to pursue removal of an alien in either expedited removal proceedings or proceedings under section 240 of the Act.”); see also Matter of Bahta, supra; Matter of G-N-C-, supra; Matter of Quintero, supra.
 
The respondent’s motion does not present “exceptional circumstances” that would warrant the exercise of our discretionary authority to reopen his proceedings sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Having considered the respondent’s appellate contentions (Resp. Br. at 15-18), reopening and administrative closure is also not warranted because we do not have the authority to grant relief from removal solely on equitable or humanitarian grounds. See Matter of Silva-Rodriguez, 20 I&N Dec. 448 (BIA 1992); Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734, 742 (BIA 1988). Under these circumstances, administrative closure of the proceedings is not warranted under Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012).
 
*5 We acknowledge that enforcement of the removal order may result in hardship to the respondent and family members. However, the Immigration Judge and the Board do not have jurisdiction to fashion equitable remedies or grant general humanitarian relief. See Matter of Medina, supra.
 
In general, motions to reopen must be filed within 90 days from the date “on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceedings sought to be reopened,” see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); see also section 240(c)(7)(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C), although this time limit is subject to equitable tolling. Alzaarir v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 639 F.3d 86, 90 (3d Cir. 2011). The respondent does not dispute that his motion to reopen was filed more than 90 days after the administratively final decision. Rather, he argues that the Immigration Judge abused his discretion by refusing to equitably toll the 90-day time limitation for filing a motion to reopen on the basis of the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel (Resp. Br. at 14-15).
 
Ineffective assistance of counsel can serve as a basis for equitable tolling, but it must be substantiated and accompanied by a showing of due diligence. See Mahmood v. Gonzales, All F.3d 248, 252 (3d Cir. 2005). An alien seeking to equitably toll the limitation period based on ineffective assistance of counsel must demonstrate that he has exercised due diligence in raising the claim, and that prejudice resulted from counsel’s alleged errors. Mahmood v. Gonzales, supra, at 251-52; Fadiga v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 488 F.3d 142, 159 (3d Cir. 2007). Prejudice requires an alien to show that there was a “reasonable likelihood that the result would have been different if the error[s] ... had not occurred.” Fadiga v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, at 159. “Periods of unaccounted-for delay reveal a lack of diligence.” Mahmood v. Gonzales, supra, at 253; see also Alzaarir v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, at 90 (“Due diligence must be exercised over the entire period for which tolling is desired.”). “This includes both the period of time before the ineffective assistance of counsel was or should have been discovered and the period from that point until the motion to reopen is filed.” Alzaarir v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra. The failure to exercise due diligence in asserting an ineffective assistance of counsel undermines any claim for equitable tolling. “Equitable tolling is an extraordinary remedy which should be extended only sparingly.” Mahmood v. Gonzales, supra, at 253 (quoting Hedges v. U.S., 404 F.3d 744, 751 (3d Cir. 2005)).
 
*6 We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s “““assertions of counsel ineffectiveness are without merit” (I.J. at 2). On this record, the respondent has not demonstrated compliance with the procedural requirements for establishing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988).5 The Third Circuit has adopted Lozada’s procedural requirements, but strict compliance is not always required. Pajow v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 515 F.App’x 119, 121 (3d Cir. 2013) (unpublished); Rranci v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 540 F.3d 165, 172-74 (3d Cir. 2008). Having considered the respondent’s appellate contentions, we conclude that he has not demonstrated substantial compliance with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra (Resp. Br. at 7, 14-15). Moreover, the respondent has not accounted for the period of delay in pursuing his ineffective assistance of counsel claim.
 
To the extent the respondent asserts a due process violation, we are unpersuaded. On this record, the respondent has not shown that the Immigration Judge denied the respondent due process of law by denying his motion to reopen. The respondent has not demonstrated prima facie eligibility for a form of relief that either the Immigration Judge or the Board has the authority to grant. Moreover, although the Immigration Judge did not discuss every piece of evidence referred to by the respondent, he did consider the relevant evidence of record. Huang v. Att’y Gen. of U.S., 620 F.3d 372, 388 (3d Cir. 2010) (finding that Immigration Judges need not discuss every piece of evidence mentioned by an asylum applicant). We do not conclude that the respondent was in any way prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s handling of her case, or that the hearing was unfair and violated due process. See Delgado-Sobalvarro v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 625 F.3d 782, 787 (3d Cir. 2010); see also Khan v. Att’y Gen. of U.S., supra, at 236; Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105 (BIA 1984); Matter of Exame, 18 I&N Dec. 303 (BIA 1982).
 
Further, the respondent has not demonstrated that the Immigration Judge abandoned his impartial role. An Immigration Judge has the authority to “““interrogate, examine, and cross-examine the alien” in removal proceedings. See section 240(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(1). See generally Matter of Rhee, 16 I&N Dec. 607 (BIA 1978). Due process requires the alien be afforded a full and fair hearing; however, an Immigration Judge has broad discretion in how he conducts that hearing. See generally Romanishyn v. Att’y Gen. of U.S., 455 F.3d 175, 185 (3d Cir. 2006); Khan v. Att’y Gen. of U.S., 448 F.3d 226, 236 (3d Cir. 2006); Abdulrahman v. Ashcroft, 330 F.3d 587, 596 (3d Cir. 2003). We have reviewed the record and find no basis to conclude that the Immigration Judge was biased or that the Immigration Judge’s questioning or comments in any way exceeded judicial bounds. See Liteky v. United States, 510 U.S. 540, 556 (1994); Abdulrahman v. Ashcroft, supra, at 597; cf. Offutt v. United States, 348 U.S. 11 (1954) (finding that the record reveals an attitude inconsistent with the restraints of conventional judicial demeanor).
 
*7 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LESY ERENIO SANCHEZ-MATUTE
File: AXXX XX8 848 - San Antonio, TX
May 14, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paula J. Ferreira, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s July 7, 2014, decision denying her motion to reopen. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on July 8, 2013. The appeal will be sustained, the proceedings reopened and the record remanded.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent claimed in her motion to reopen that exceptional circumstances prevented her from attending her July 8, 2013, removal hearing (Resp. Mot. to Reopen at 3-6). Specifically, respondent, through counsel, asserted that her attorney advised her that her pending motion to change venue would be granted and she was not required to attend the hearing; and also that her counsel suffered a computer virus that made his calendar inaccessible (Resp. Mot. to Reopen at 3-6). The Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding that the mere existence of a pending motion to change venue did not relieve the respondent of her duty to attend the hearing, and that the computer virus did not amount to exceptional circumstances (I.J. at 2). On appeal, the respondent, through the same counsel, maintains her claim of exceptional circumstances and further asserts that she is not required to comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), because she is not claiming ineffective assistance of counsel, only that counsel’s incorrect advice constitutes an exceptional circumstance (Resp. Br. at 7).
 
The respondent is correct that both this Board and the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this matter arises, have found that the erroneous advice of counsel may constitute an exceptional circumstance excusing the respondent’s failure to appear for a hearing. See Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (1996); see also Galvez-Vergara v. Gonzales, 484 F.3d 798 (5th Cir. 2007). However, in such a scenario, substantial compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, is still required.1 See Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 496-98 (5th Cir. 2000); see also Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 648 (5th Cir. 2014); Rodriguez-Manzano v. Holder, 666 F.3d 948, 953 (5th Cir. 2012).
 
*2 Based on the totality of the circumstances in this case, we will grant the respondent’s motion to reopen to rescind her in absentia order pursuant to our sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, supra, and Galvez-Vergara v. Gonzales, supra, each of the respondents were represented by new counsel and had already complied with Matter of Lozada, supra. See Galvez-Vergara, supra, at 800, 800 n.2; see also Grijalva-Barrera, supra, at 473-74. Here, the respondent is represented by the same counsel who incorrectly advised her on her need to appear for the hearing and has not filed any complaint regarding this conduct. Further, it appears counsel still has not advised the respondent regarding the need to comply with Matter of Lozada, supra.

2015 WL 3932301 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MUSTAPHA JALLOW A.K.A. MUSTAHPA GALLOW
File: AXXX XX3 922 - Baltimore, MD
May 12, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stuart A. Yeager, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)] - Inadmissible at time of entry
 
 
APPLICATION: Voluntary Departure
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of The Gambia, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s April 24, 2014, decision. That decision found the respondent subject to removal as charged, but granted post-hearing voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b). The respondent submitted a brief concerning the Immigration Judge’s decision, while the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) did not respond to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). All other issues, including whether or not the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, we review de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The respondent’s case arises after May 11, 2005, and is therefore governed by the provisions of the REAL ID Act.
 
The respondent’s sole argument on appeal is that the Immigration Judge erred by not granting 120 days’ pre-conclusion voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Act. However, at the April 24, 2014, removal hearing, counsel for the respondent said that the respondent was seeking post-hearing voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act (Tr. at 24). This was after the Immigration Judge noted that a respondent cannot seek pre-conclusion voluntary departure unless the individual waives the right to appeal to this Board. Matter of Ocampo, 22 I&N Dec. 1301 (BIA 2000)(voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Act requires a waiver of the right to appeal); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(D); Tr. at 24. The respondent makes no claim that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from the attorney who represented him at the removal hearing. The appeal will, therefore, be dismissed.
 
The record reflects that the respondent submitted timely proof of having paid the voluntary departure bond. The period of voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act will, therefore, be reinstated.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the DHS. See section 240B(b) of the Act; see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
*2 NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
David B. Holmes

IN RE: EDWARD PSZCZOLA
File: AXXX XX5 199 - Chicago, IL
May 8, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Edwin T Gania, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Geoffrey P. Gilpin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; adjustment of status
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 17, 2013, ordering him removed after denying his request for a continuance. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) opposes the respondent’s appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994).
 
The Immigration Judge granted the respondent 2 continuances prior to his final hearing (I.J. at 2-3; Tr. at 9-11, 21-24). In denying the respondent’s request for yet another continuance, the Immigration Judge considered the factors outlined in Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009), and Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009) (I.J. at 3). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 and 1240.6; Matter of Villarreal-Zuniga, 23 I&N Dec. 886, 891 (BIA 2006). The Immigration Judge specifically considered the respondent’s lengthy residence and family ties in this country, and other positive factors such as his business and ownership of rental properties (I.J. at 3-4). The Immigration Judge also considered the DHS’s opposition to any further continuances based on the respondent’s criminal record and his undesirability as a permanent resident (I.J. at 3). The Immigration Judge determined that even if the underlying visa petition were approved, the respondent did not warrant adjustment of status as a matter of discretion (I.J. at 4-5). Pawlowska v. Holder, 623 F.3d 1138, 1140-41 (7th Cir. 2010) (where Immigration Judge denied a continuance based on the conclusion that he would ultimately deny adjustment of status as a discretionary matter).
 
Although the underlying visa petition has since been approved (Respondent’s Brief, Exh. A), we find no basis to remand this matter, or to otherwise disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision, as we agree that the respondent has not demonstrated that he warrants adjustment of status as a matter of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
*2 The Immigration Judge properly weighed and balanced the factors relevant to the exercise of discretion in this case. Although the respondent has many equities and his removal will result in some hardship, as described by the Immigration Judge and the. respondent on appeal (Respondent’s Brief at 5-9), he also has an extensive history of drinking and driving, which the Immigration Judge properly considered as a significant negative factor. Matter of Teixeira, 21 I&N Dec. 316, 321 (BIA 1996) (evidence of criminal activity should be considered in discretionary determinations). The Immigration Judge did not clearly err in finding the respondent’s repeated drank driving violations were serious and that the respondent was a danger to society (I.J. at 5). Portillo-Rendon v. Holder, 662 F.3d 815, 816 (7th Cir. 2011) (finding that the alien exhibited a “disdain for the rules that govern the use of automobiles. Driving while intoxicated or without a license reflects both indifference to the welfare of other drivers and pedestrians and defiance of known legal obligations.”). The respondent’s repeated disregard for the welfare of others demonstrates a pattern of knowingly placing others at risk of harm. For the reasons discussed above and by the Immigration Judge we agree that the respondent’s positive equities are outweighed by the negative factors, and that the respondent has failed to carry his burden of proving that he merits adjustment of status as a matter of discretion. Id; Matter of Blas, 15 I&N Dec. 626, 628-30 (BIA 1974; A.G. 1976); Matter of Arai, 13 I&N Dec. 494 (BIA 1970).
 
While we sympathize with the respondent’s family members, who will undoubtedly experience some hardship in connection with his removal, our overriding obligation is to safeguard the interests of the United States, and not just those of the respondent and those close to him.
 
Although the respondent argues that his former counsel’s inadequate preparation negatively impacted his case (Respondent’s Brief at 3-5), he has failed to meet the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). For example, the respondent has not provided an affidavit setting forth his agreement with former counsel, has not shown that former counsel was given the opportunity to respond to the allegations, and has not filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority. Matter of Lozada, supra. Further, he has not established prejudice. Apouviepseakoda v. Gonzales, 475 F.3d 881, 885 (7th Cir. 2007) (alien must establish prejudice before court would vacate the agency’s decision); Boci v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 762, 768 (7th Cir. 2007) (alien cannot prevail without showing that granting a continuance likely would have affected the result of the hearing). Additionally, the respondent’s disagreement with the Immigration Judge’s decision does not render his conduct a violation of due process (Respondent’s Brief at 3-5). See Khan v. Filip, 554 F.3d 681, 689 (7th Cir. 2009); Zamora-Mallari v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 679, 694 (7th Cir. 2008). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 3932324 (BIA)

2015 WL 3932325 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PAU SOC CHAU
File: AXXX XX9 220 - York, PA
May 8, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Johanna K. Dennis, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeffrey T. Bubier
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 14, 2015. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction. The record will be remanded.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Notice to Appear alleges that, on October 31, 2013, the respondent was convicted of Robbery - Threat of Immediate Physical Injury, in violation of Pennsylvania law, and sentenced to 11.5 months to 23 months in prison. A record of this conviction was entered into the record of proceedings (Exh. 2(C)). See section 240(c)(3)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(3)(B); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.41(a). In addition, the respondent admitted the conviction (Tr. at 6). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.41(d), 1240.10(c). Based on this evidence, the Immigration Judge found the conviction proved (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 6-7). The respondent does not expressly challenge this finding on appeal.
 
The respondent was charged with being removable for having been convicted of an aggravated felony involving theft (Exh. 1). The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 7).1 The respondent does not expressly challenge this finding on appeal.
 
On appeal, the respondent indicates he was denied his right to counsel and received insufficient time to hire an attorney to represent him in removal proceedings.
 
Respondents in immigration proceedings have the statutory and regulatory “““privilege of being represented” by counsel of their choice at no expense to the Government. See sections 240(b)(4)(A), 292 of the Act; 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(4)(A), 1362. See also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.16(b), 1240.3, 1240.11(c)(1)(iii). Where the statutory and regulatory privilege of legal representation is not expressly waived, in order to meaningfully effectuate the privilege, the Immigration Judge must grant a reasonable and realistic amount of time and provide a fair opportunity for a respondent to seek, speak with, and retain counsel. See Matter of C-B-, 25 I&N Dec. 888, 889-890 (BIA 2012). See also sections 239(a)(1)(E) and 239(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229(a)(1)(E), (b)(1).
 
*2 We note that, on November 12, 2014, the Immigration Judge did advise the respondent of his rights concerning representation, provided him a list of free and low cost attorneys, and granted him two months to hire an attorney (Tr. at 2). Moreover, at the final hearing, the respondent said he wished to represent himself (Tr. at 4; I.J. at 1). Because (1) the respondent did not request additional time to seek, speak with, and retain counsel, and (2) the respondent advised the Immigration Judge that he wished to represent himself, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent waived his statutory and regulatory privilege of legal representation. See Matter of C-B-, supra. We agree that the statutory and regulatory privilege of legal representation was waived.
 
On appeal, the respondent also alleges that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of his conviction. While he is free to file a motion to vacate his criminal conviction before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010),2 such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
Finally on appeal, the respondent says the Immigration Judge erred in ordering him removed to Vietnam. At the hearing on January 15, 2015, the respondent admitted the allegation in the Notice to Appear that he is a native and citizen of Vietnam (Tr. at 6, 11). However, the Memorandum of Creation of Record of Lawful Permanent Residence in the record indicates that he was born in Thailand and is a citizen of Cambodia (Exh. 2(E) at 54). In light of that, we find it appropriate to remand the record to have the Department of Homeland Security make the appropriate amendment to the Notice to Appear, to have the Immigration Judge reconsider his factual findings concerning the respondent’s country of nativity and country of citizenship, and to reconsider the country of removal.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and entry of a new decision.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

However, rather than finding it to be an aggravated felony involving theft, the Immigration Judge refers to the aggravated felony as a crime of violence. On remand, the Immigration Judge should clarify his finding of removal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. __, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).
