2015 WL 1208087 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE WILMER MEMBRENO-DELAO A.K.A WILBER DELGO MEMBRENO A.K.A. JOSE MEMBRENO A.K.A. JOSE WUILMER MEMBRENO
File: AXXX XX3 797 - Los Angeles, CA
January 29, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lorena Marquez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before us on October 31, 2014, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision, affirming the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent had not demonstrated good cause for a continuance to pursue prosecutorial discretion from the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) and that the respondent did not warrant preconclusion voluntary departure as a matter of discretion. The DHS has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent requests reconsideration of our prior decision because he argues that the decision is based on the mistaken conclusion that he was convicted of two counts of driving under the influence of alcohol, one in 2010 and another in 2011, when, in fact, he was only charged with driving under the influence in 2010, but never convicted (Resp. Mot. at 2-3; Exhs. A-B). The respondent argues that his prior counsel was ineffective in that he did not inform the court that the respondent was only convicted one time (Resp. Mot. at 2). In support of this argument, the respondent has submitted evidence for the first time which demonstrates that his 2010 charge for driving under the influence was dismissed (Resp. Exh. A at 5). Accordingly, the motion is also in the nature of a motion to reopen.
 
We see no reason to reconsider our prior decision or reopen proceedings in light of the new evidence presented. First, the respondent has not established that the evidence, which was available at the time of the previous hearing, was not submitted as a result of ineffective assistance of counsel. He has neither substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), nor is ineffective assistance plain from the administrative record. See Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999) (explaining that “[t]o establish ineffective assistance of counsel in a motion to reopen, an alien must” meet Lozada’s criteria); see Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 596-99 (9th Cir. 2004) (explaining that strict compliance with Matter of Lozada may not be required where ineffective assistance of counsel is plain from the administrative record).
 
Even if the respondent was to establish that his counsel was neglectful in failing to file evidence of his prior convictions, we do not conclude that “the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999). While it does appear that the respondent was in fact not convicted of driving under the influence in 2010, the evidence indicates that he was arrested for such an offense, made to complete alcohol awareness classes, and that his alcohol use was limited as a condition of his probation (Exh. A). That arrest, and subsequent probation, are still relevant to the discretionary determination on his application for relief, even if it did not result in a conviction. See e.g. Matter of Grijalva, 19 I&N Dec. 713, 722 (BIA 1988) (advising that in cases involving discretionary relief, all relevant factors concerning criminal history, including arrests not resulting in a conviction, and information contained in police reports should be considered). The particularly troubling aspect of this respondent’s misconduct was his 2011 driving under the influence conviction shortly after he had attended alcohol awareness classes in 2010. As emphasized by the Immigration Judge, the respondent apparently “learned nothing from those classes that he took in 2010” because he was “well aware of the consequences of driving under the influence of alcohol, but chose to get in a car again and put the lives of other people at risk by driving under the influence of alcohol.”
 
*2 As such, we conclude that the respondent was not prejudiced by counsel’s actions and that reopening for further consideration of the respondent’s application for pre-conclusion voluntary departure is not warranted. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (explaining that the party who seeks to reopen proceedings to pursue a discretionary grant of relief from removal bears a “heavy burden” of demonstrating the new evidence presented would likely change the result in the case).
 
Finally, we are unpersuaded by the respondent’s argument that his proceedings should be reopened to allow him to pursue his Form I-589, submitted for the first time with this motion, because of the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel (Resp. Br. at 2). As explained above, the respondent has not substantially complied with Matter of Lozada in making his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and nothing in the record suggests that respondent’s counsel was ineffective in not pursuing this form of relief on the respondent’s behalf previously. See Ortiz v. INS, supra, at 1153; Reyes v. Ashcroft, supra, at 596-99. The respondent’s right to apply for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture was fully explained to him at his hearing and he was given two continuances for the purpose of completing those applications (Resp. Mot. at 3; Exh. C; Tr. at 9-10, 14, 17). Accordingly, we see no reason to remand proceedings to allow the respondent to pursue that form of relief. Therefore, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 1208087 (BIA)

2015 WL 799939 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAMUEL GOMEZ A.K.A. SAMUEL MARTINEZ GOMEZ A.K.A. SAMUEL MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX1 930 - Houston, Texas
January 26, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The proceedings in this matter were last before the Board on August 22, 2014, at which time the respondent’s appeal of the decision of the Immigration Judge of May 17, 2013, was dismissed. That decision pretermitted the respondent’s application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). On November 14, 2014, the respondent filed the timely motion to reopen now before the Board alleging ineffective assistance of prior counsel. The Board may in some cases grant a motion to reopen in order to remedy an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See generally Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-640 (BIA 1988). Nonetheless, in this matter, the respondent has failed to show that but for the assistance rendered, he would have been entitled to relief from removal. In particular, the respondent contends that former counsel erred in conceding that he, the respondent, was removable as charged. However, the respondent has failed to provide to former counsel, and has continued to fail to provide to the Board, appropriate evidence to establish lawful entry. Absent such evidence, the respondent has failed to show the prejudice requisite to establishing ineffective assistance of counsel. Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 (5th Cir. 2001). To the extent that the respondent seeks humanitarian relief to enable him to remain in the United States, such relief is to be pursued with the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). Likewise, any request for relief from removal in the exercise of prosecutorial discretion must also be made to the DHS. Accordingly, the motion to reopen is, hereby, denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 799939 (BIA)

2015 WL 799924 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KULWINDER SINGH DHALIWAL
File: AXXX XX9 685 - Detroit, MI
January 20, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul W. Shonk, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jonathan Goulding
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of India who entered the United States as a conditional permanent resident based on a marriage to a United States citizen. His status was terminated by USCIS, the Immigration Judge denied his good faith waiver, sustained the charge of marriage fraud, and the Board dismissed his appeal. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent also seeks a remand based on his marriage to another United States citizen and a pending I-130 visa petition. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the granting of the motion. The motion is denied.
 
A motion to reopen “shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing . . . .” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The movant must also establish prima facie eligibility for the relief sought, INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 319 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988), and satisfy the “heavy burden” of establishing that if the proceedings were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
The respondent does not address the requirement that the evidence must not be previously available. Rather, the respondent contends that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel because he did not also pursue an extreme hardship waiver based on his alleged persecution in India, which continued against his family even after he moved to the United States, or adequately prepare him, or his witnesses, for trial (Motion, Exh. A). Many years after the fact, the respondent also argues that his mother should not have been called as a witness because she had dementia. We find no basis to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Contrary to the respondent’s assertions, the record indicates that Mr. Thomas’s representation was thorough and that he was an effective advocate on behalf of the respondent during this proceeding. Mr. Thomas filed a response to the complaint which confirms this view (Notice of Filing Counsel’s Response to Bar Complaint, filed 1/7/15). A review of the record indicates that the respondent had at least three different attorneys in this proceeding before Mr. Thomas entered his appearance (EOIR-28 filed 3/6/08 for Karen Denise Bradley; I.J. Order issued 3/27/08 substituting Candie Clement; I.J. Order issued 4/1/08 substituting David Wenger; I.J. Order issued 2/8/12 substituting Mark Jacob Thomas).
 
*2 An attorney prior to Mr. Thomas prepared and pursued the I-751 waiver with USCIS. It is well-settled that where USCIS has denied a waiver, the respondent may not pursue an alternative waiver under section 216(c)(4) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1186a(c)(4) during proceedings. See Matter of Anderson, 20 I&N Dec. 888 (BIA 1994). Further, the respondent has not presented sufficient evidence of changed circumstances occurring after the filing of the original waiver so that Mr. Thomas would have had notice and been able to seek a continuance. Id.
 
As to the question of extreme hardship, even if Mr. Thomas had pursued an untimely waiver and argued that the alleged extreme hardship arose during the requisite two year period, we do not find that the respondent’s current claims are credible (Motion, Exhs. F and G). The respondent and his father allege that due to the persecution, the respondent was in hiding at a relative’s home prior to leaving India, and his father asserts that he was forced to immigrate soon after due to the continued persecution (Motion, Exh. F at p. 045; Exh. G at p. 048). However, the documents and affidavits filed with USCIS in 2001 within several years after the last incidents of alleged harm make no mention of persecution, harm, hiding, or fear of residing in India.
 
Rather, in his 2001 filings with USCIS (long before Mr. Thomas represented him), the respondent submitted an affidavit under oath that described his courtship in India. He attested to their initial meeting in his family home in India, their visits with her children, their visits and “shopping all over the city” together, looking around for a wedding hall, their ceremony and honeymoon, visits with relatives, his playing with her children, residing at his parents’ house with his new wife, driving to the airport and waiting there until her plane took off, and obtaining his visa and coming to the United States (I.J. Dec. at 3; Exh. 2, Tab B at pp. 61-63). The respondent’s father also submitted a prior affidavit and did not mention the alleged fears or the fact that his son was in hiding (Exh. 2, Tab B at pp. 64-66), which they now claim over 13 years later.
 
Given the inconsistencies between the affidavits filed with the motion long after the alleged events occurred and the affidavits already contained in the record, we find that the respondent’s current claims are not credible. Further, given the representations made by the respondent and his father under penalty of perjury in the 2001 filing with USCIS, there was no reason for Mr. Thomas to inquire further or explore an untimely extreme hardship waiver. Accordingly, we do not find that Mr. Thomas provided ineffective assistance of counsel to the respondent such that proceedings should be reopened and remanded. Nor has the respondent shown the requisite prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
*3 The respondent has a pending I-130 based on his marriage to another United States citizen, but he is not prima face eligible to adjust his status (I.J. Dec. at pp. 27-28 sustaining marriage fraud charge of removability). Section 204(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1154(c). Further, the respondent filed a document indicating that his conditional permanent resident status has somehow been reinstated. See Notice of Filing dated 11/10/14, Tab A). Therefore, the respondent is not eligible to adjust his status under section 245. Section 245(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(d). The following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 799924 (BIA)

2015 WL 799765 (BIA)

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAJINDER SINGH
File: AXXX XX5 843 - Eloy, Arizona
January 20, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hardeep Rai, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The proceedings in this matter were last before the Board on July 28, 2014. On November 25, 2014, the respondent filed the motion to reopen now before the Board alleging ineffective assistance of prior counsels. The respondent has complied, in part, with the process requisite to asserting such a claim. See generally Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-640 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent has provided detailed affidavits setting forth the bases for his assertion of ineffective assistance of counsel; evidence of fee arrangements and payments both in the United States and abroad to former counsels; evidence of representation by former counsels; bar complaints filed against one of the attorneys in two different jurisdictions, California and Texas; adverse information from the press relating to an individual associated with former counsel who also played a part in the representation of the respondent; and, a press report naming the individual who the respondent met at the detention center who referred him to former counsel. The respondent paid substantial fees to an attorney, referred to him by an individual at the Eloy detention facility, who, with the assistance of other attorneys, appeared in proceedings on behalf of the respondent, but ultimately failed to file an application for relief by the deadline set resulting in the respondent’s inability to seek the relief of asylum in the United States.
 
The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. We accept the respondent’s detailed affidavit, which we accept as true for purposes of this motion (see Najmabadi v. Holder, 597 F.3d 983, 994 (9th Cir. 2010)). Given the circumstances detailed in the respondent’s affidavit, we find that an adequate showing of due diligence has been made, that equitable tolling is appropriate and that reopening should be granted. See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672 (9th Cir. 2011). On remand, the respondent shall have the opportunity to apply for any relief for which he may be eligible.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen is granted and the record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2015 WL 799754 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NICHOLAS JERMAINE GUMBS
File: AXXX XX9 442 - St. Thomas, VI1
January 13, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Amos W. Carty, Jr., Esquire
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Anguilla, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated June 10, 2013, ordering his removal from the United States.2 We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent is inadmissible to the United States under the provisions of section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(7)(A)(i)(I), because he is an immigrant who, at the time of his application for admission, did not possess a valid entry document (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). The respondent arrived in the United States on July 20, 2012, after having been absent for more than 1 year (Exh. 1; Tr. at 15). While, according to the Record of Deportable / Inadmissible Alien (Form I-213), the respondent did present a Lawful Permanent Resident Card (Form I-551) upon his application for admission to this country, a Form I-551 is sufficient to allow for an alien’s admission to the United States only if the alien “if seeking readmission after a temporary absence of less than 1 year.”3 8 C.F.R. § 211.1(a)(2).
 
The Immigration Judge advised the respondent that he could seek a waiver of the requirement that he hold a valid entry document by filing an Application for Waiver of Passport and/or Visa (Form I-193) (Tr. at 11). See section 211(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(b). The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent to file everything, i.e., the Form I-193, a witness list, and supporting documents, with the Immigration Court no later than June 3, 2013, and, if the documents were not timely filed, she would consider the form to be abandoned (Tr. at 20). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.31(a), (c); Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549 (BIA 1992). The respondent contends that he attempted to fax his Form I-I93 to the Immigration Court on June 3, 2013. Attempting to file a Form I-193 by fax was improper because, aside from the Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative before the Immigration Court (Form EOIR-28), “[a]ll other filings must be submitted as paper submissions to the Immigration Court.”4 Immigration Court Practice Manual, § 3.1(a)(viii) at 34 (June 10, 2013), http:// www.justice.gov/eoir/vll/OCIJPracManual/Practice_Manual_review.pdf. Accordingly, as the respondent has not established that he timely complied and properly complied with the Immigration Judge’s filing deadline, the Immigration Judge properly deemed his opportunity to file a Form I-193 to be abandoned and ordered his removal from the United States to Anguilla.
 
*2 Finally, the respondent has presented numerous documents on appeal. However, the Board does not consider evidence first provided on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv); Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984) (“The Board is an appellate body whose function is to review, not to create, a record.”). Additionally, we decline to remand these proceedings to the Immigration Judge to consider the evidence in the first instance as such evidence has not been shown to affect the outcome of this case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Even though the respondent has attached several letters of support to his appeal brief, as discussed above, he is inadmissible under the provisions of section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) of the Act and has abandoned his opportunity to file a Form I-193.
 
For the reasons set forth above, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

Removal proceedings before the Immigration Judge in this matter were completed at a Department of Homeland Security office in St. Thomas, Virgin Islands (see OPPM No. 04-06). The Immigration Judge conducted the hearings there remotely from the Immigration Court in Guaynabo, Puerto Rico, via video teleconference pursuant to section 240(b)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act.

2

The respondent, through counsel, claims that, on June 5, 2013, he mailed a Form I-193 to the Immigration Court and, on June 14, 2013, he filed a motion to reconsider. However, neither of those filing are currently present in the record of proceedings.

3

There are exceptions to the rule that a Form I-551, in itself, is sufficient to allow for an alien’s the admission to the United States only if the alien is seeking readmission after a temporary absence of less than 1 year. For example, in certain circumstances, an alien seeking readmission after a temporary absence of more than 1 year may present a Form I-551 if he is seeking readmission after any temporary absence connected with his or her duties as a crewman or he is an employee of the United States government. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 211.1(a)(2), (5), (6), (7). However, the respondent does not meaningfully argue that he is entitled to an exception to this rule.

2015 WL 799727 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NORMAN JIMINEZ
File: AXXX XX3 253 - Newark, NJ
January 9, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Helen Ramirez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and will be denied. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent filed the motion more than a decade after the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal in June 2004. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion.
 
The respondent does not dispute that his motion is untimely, but urges that the time limit for motions to reopen should be equitably tolled because he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel by two prior attorneys. The respondent has substantially complied with the threshold procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for pursuing an ineffective assistance claim. To warrant equitable tolling, however, the respondent must also demonstrate due diligence in raising the claim. See Mahmood v. Gonzales, All F.3d 248 (3d Cir. 2005).
 
The respondent avers that attorneys Pasquale F. Giannetta and Marcia S. Kasdan deficiently represented the respondent. The respondent alleges that attorney-Giannetta, inter alia, did not explain or obtain the respondent’s consent to waive appeal in order to obtain the benefit of voluntary departure, failed to explain or pursue available forms of relief on the respondent’s behalf, and did not file an appellate brief on the respondent’s behalf. The respondent alleges that in 2007 he retained attorney-Kasdan who also failed to regularly communicate with the respondent and did not pursue adjustment of status on the respondent’s behalf in connection with a labor certification approval and Form I-140 approval in April 2009.
 
The respondent should have been aware of attorney-Giannetta’s alleged deficient performance when we issued our decision in 2004. And he should have known of attorney-Kasdan’s inadequate representation - at the very latest - when she repeatedly refused to meet with him regarding his employment-related visa petition (Form I-140), which the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services approved in April 2009. The respondent has not adequately explained why he waited more than a decade to raise ineffective assistance against attorney-Giannetta, and more than 5 years for his claim against attorney-Kasdan. Under the circumstances, the respondent has not established the requisite due diligence to raise ineffective assistance in order to equitably toll the 90-day time limitation on motions to reopen.
 
While the respondent has submitted evidence that the New Jersey licensing authority has sanctioned attorney-Giannetta and attorney-Kasdan, he does not allege that counsels’ sanctions arose from deficient performance in these proceedings.
 
*2 Additionally, the respondent has not set forth “exceptional circumstances” that would warrant the exercise of our sua sponte authority to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (stating that “““[a]s a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”). The respondent requests reopening so that he may seek adjustment of status based upon a recently-approved visa petition filed by his United States citizen wife. But becoming eligible for relief from removal, such as adjustment of status, after a final administrative order has been entered is common and does not, in itself, constitute an exceptional circumstance warranting consideration of an untimely motion to reopen. Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2015 WL 799727 (BIA)
End of Document

2015 WL 799732 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CESAR LARA RIOS
File: AXXX XX8 328 - Arlington, VA
January 7, 2015
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Yousof W. Nesari, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed the October 6, 2014, decision of the Immigration Judge denying his motion to reconsider the denial of an August 4, 2014, motion to reopen proceedings in which he had been ordered removed on May 8, 2012, In his 2012 decision, the Immigration Judge also granted his request for voluntary departure. The respondent has submitted both a “motion to reopen” and a “motion to remand” on appeal. We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment arising in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo, whereas we review findings of fact in such appeals under a “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). The appeal will be dismissed, and the motions denied.
 
We initially affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reconsider the denial of his motion to reopen. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); 8 C.F.R. § 1002.23(b)(2); see also Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741 (4th Cir. 2006) (requiring that a respondent demonstrate at least “““substantial compliance” with the Lozada requirements); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Although the respondent’s motion to reopen was predicated on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, he did not substantially comply with the requirements for reopening on such a basis. Id. As noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent did not file a state bar complaint against former counsel, and did not inform counsel of the allegations of ineffectiveness and notify him that he may respond. Rather, the letter he sent to former counsel specifically stated that it was not intended for Lozada compliance (Motion, tab J, ftnt. 1).
 
We recognize that the respondent submitted a number of documents with his motion to reconsider. However, a motion to reconsider is a respondent’s opportunity to specify errors of law or fact in the previous order. It is not intended to serve as a mechanism to present new evidence, or to provide an opportunity to reiterate or expand upon arguments that have been previously raised and considered. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra; Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). As such, we find that the Immigration Judge committed no error in not addressing the evidence newly submitted with the motion to reconsider.1
 
*2 While the appeal at issue was pending, the respondent filed a “motion to reopen” on December 12, 2014, averring that former counsel provided him with ineffective assistance.2 As the respondent is seeking reopening of the Immigration Judge’s 2012 decision, the motion is untimely filed. 8 C.F.R. § 1002.23(b)(1). A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel can serve to exempt a motion from the noted deadline, as indicated supra. However, we continue to find that the respondent has not complied with the requirements for reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. To date, the respondent admittedly has not filed a state bar complaint against former counsel, despite the fact that he was informed of this deficiency in the Immigration Judge’s October 6, 2014, decision (Motion at 4). While he states that he is awaiting further instructions from the court, this vague assertion is insufficient to excuse him from compliance with this requirement.
 
This is also true of the requirement that he notify former counsel of the allegations of ineffectiveness, and inform him that he may respond. In this regard, we note that the July 22, 2014, letter sent by the respondent’s current attorney to his former counsel does not contend that the respondent informed him of any fears, experiences, or concerns forming the basis for a claim of asylum or associated relief. Rather, the letter asks why such relief was not sought, and makes factual statements in this regard. It does not allege that these concerns were ever conveyed to former counsel, and not acted upon. In addition, a letter from former counsel states that the respondent did not express any fear of return to Mexico although he was asked about the same. Counsel also reports that he was not informed that the respondent’s mother was “““completely dependent” on him, as claimed in the July 22, 2014, letter. Given these significant deficiencies, we find that the respondent has not substantially complied with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. As such, reopening on this basis is not warranted.
 
As indicated above, the respondent has also filed a “motion to remand to Immigration Judge.” He seeks a remand as he contends that he is eligible under an expansion of the provisional unlawful presence waiver (I-601A) as directed by President Obama. In this regard he cites a directive from Department of Homeland Security (DHS) Secretary Johnson regarding the noted expansion. We presume the respondent is referencing a November 20, 2014, memorandum from Secretary Johnson. Therein Secretary Johnson directed DHS to amend its regulation regarding the provisional waiver program. However, to date no such amendment has been promulgated. We are not unsympathetic to the situation faced by the respondent and his family. However, any request for a favorable exercise of prosecutorial discretion should be directed to the DHS as the Board is without authority to grant any such request.
 
*3 In sum, we find that the Immigration Judge correctly determined that reconsideration of his denial of the respondent’s prior motion to reopen was not appropriate. In addition, we further find that reopening based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as asserted in the respondent’s second motion to reopen is not appropriate. Finally, we find that a remand based on Secretary Johnson’s directive is also not warranted. Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motions are denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s arguments that the grant of voluntary departure was void ab initio because of Immigration Judge error are in the nature of a motion to reconsider the Immigration Judge’s 2012 decision, and are untimely in that regard (Resp. Br. at 4). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). To the extent that there respondent may suggest that this forms another basis for a claim of ineffective assistance, he has not complied with the relevant requirements for such a claim in this regard. As such, we shall address these contentions no further.

2

While the respondent again avers that his voluntary departure grant may be void ab initio, also alleging that the Immigration Judge neglected to appropriately question him regarding voluntary departure, such arguments are in the nature of a motion to reconsider and have been untimely filed, as previously noted (Motion at 5-7).

2014 WL 10155324 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PARTHA SAHA
File: AXXX XX0 928 - Batavia, NY
December 23, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Courtney A. Chua, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brian J. Counihan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(C), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(C)] - Convicted of firearms or destructive device violation
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Bangladesh, has timely appealed the Immigration Judge’s August 18, 2014, decision. In his decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s emergency motion for stay of removal and his motion to reopen removal proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed. The request for a stay of removal is moot.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii). The respondent’s case is governed by the provisions of the REAL ID Act. Matter of S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 42 (BIA 2006).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he is eligible for reopening because he received ineffective assistance of counsel and that, if his case is reopened, he is otherwise eligible for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture (CAT). A motion to reopen filed in Immigration Court must generally be filed within 90 days of the entry of a final administrative order of removal, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), and the motion will not be granted unless the Immigration Judge is satisfied that the evidence offered in the motion is material and not previously available or discoverable at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). In addition, a motion to reopen filed for the purpose of submitting an application for asylum and related relief may be excepted from the time and numeric limitations if the motion establishes “changed country conditions arising in the country ... to which removal has been ordered.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i).
 
*2 The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed before the Immigration Judge on August 1, 2014, nearly 4 years after he was previously ordered removed, on November 16, 2010. It is, thus, untimely. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(1), 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent requests that the proceedings be reopened based on ineffective assistance by his previous counsel. His previous counsel, apparently, withdrew representation on the morning of the merits hearing before the Immigration Judge on November 16, 2010 (Resp. Br. at 5). As noted, motions to reopen must be filed within 90 days of a final order. In certain cases, untimely motions are allowed where the respondent demonstrates not only that his due process rights have been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that he has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll, Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000). Here, the respondent was placed on notice of any ineffective service on the day his attorney withdrew from representation, the same day the Immigration Judge entered the final decision. Yet, the respondent waited almost 4 years to file the current motion. He has failed to show due diligence in pursuing his rights and has failed to explain this 4 year period. See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 130 (2d Cir. 2008) (even with egregious ineffective assistance, alien must show due diligence); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir. 2006) (same). The respondent does not challenge the Immigration Judge’s observation that at the 2010 hearing, he was informed of his right to apply for asylum and given an opportunity to find new representation (I.J. at 2; Resp. Br. at 5).
 
The respondent is also requesting that the proceedings be reopened based on changed country conditions in his native Bangladesh (Resp. Br. at 9-10). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i). The respondent claims that conditions for the Hindu population in Bangladesh have worsened in the time since the original decision in this case (Resp. Br. at 9-10). In support of this claim, he has provided affidavits of family members and recent newspaper articles (Resp. Br. at 9-10; Exhs. H-M). While Hindus may face problems or harassment in Bangladesh, the record fails to provide material evidence of any significant worsening in conditions since 2010, and it fails to demonstrate changed country conditions such that reopening is warranted at this late date. See S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247 (BIA 2007). In motions to remand or reopen proceedings, the respondent bears a heavy burden in showing such action is warranted. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). The evidence presented fails to meet this burden. In this regard, we further observe that the respondent is not eligible for asylum due to his crime (Resp. Br. at 11; Exh. 1), and the Immigration Judge issued an order of removal to the United Kingdom with Bangladesh in the alternative. The Immigration Judge properly denied the motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ane Mann
FOR THE BOARD

2014 WL 7691435 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARNOLD MANUEL WARMELS
File: AXXX XX5 818 - Chicago, IL
December 23, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Wael M. Ahmad, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Seth B. Fitter
Senior Attorney
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(E)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(E)] - Convicted of crime of domestic violence, stalking, or child abuse, neglect, or abandonment
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of an aggravated felony
 
 
APPLICATION: Termination
 
The Department of Homeland Security appeals from the September 15, 2014, decision of the Immigration Judge granting the respondent’s motion to terminate removal proceedings. The respondent opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact only to determine whether such findings are clearly erroneous. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board may review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Netherlands, was admitted to the United States on January 23, 2003, as a lawful permanent resident (I.J. at 1). On July 15, 2013, he was convicted of assault in the fourth degree, in violation of subsection 508.030(1)(a) of the Kentucky Revised Statutes, and sentenced to 12 months’ imprisonment (I.J. at 1-3). According to that statute, “““A person is guilty of assault in the fourth degree when: (a) He intentionally or wantonly causes physical injury to another person.” The parties have agreed that the term “wantonly,” as used in the statute, corresponds to the common law definition of the term “recklessly” (I.J. at 3-4).
 
Based on the respondent’s conviction, the DHS issued him a Notice to Appear, charging that he was removable under section 237(a)(2)(E)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(E)(i). The DHS subsequently lodged an additional charge under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act, charging that the respondent was convicted of a “crime of violence,” for which the term of imprisonment was at least one year, which is an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(F).1
 
In a written decision, the Immigration Judge found that the DHS had not established that the respondent had been convicted of either an “aggravated felony” or a “crime of domestic violence, a crime of stalking, or a crime of child abuse, child neglect, or child abandonment” because the respondent’s conviction was not for a “crime of violence” (as defined in section 16 of title 18 of the United States Code). See (I.J. at 2-9); see also section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), (E)(i) of the Act. Accordingly, she ordered that these proceedings be terminated (I.J. at 9).
 
*2 On appeal, the DHS argues that the Immigration Judge applied the incorrect legal standard in determining that the respondent’s statute of conviction is not “divisible” (DHS Brief on Appeal, dated Nov. 20, 2014, at 3). Specifically, the DHS argues that the Immigration Judge should not have applied the divisibility analysis from our recent decision in Matter of Chairez-Castrejon, 26 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 2014), to a case arising within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit (DHS Brief on Appeal, dated Nov. 20, 2014, at 8-10). In the alternative, the DHS argues that the Immigration Judge misapplied Matter of Chairez-Castrejon to the facts of this case (DHS Brief on Appeal, dated Nov. 20, 2014, at 10-11).
 
To determine whether a particular offense qualifies as a crime of violence, we employ the “categorical approach.” Matter of Chairez-Castrejon, supra, at 351. This “requires us to focus on the minimum conduct that has a realistic probability of being prosecuted [under the statute], rather than on the facts underlying the respondent’s particular violation of that statute.” Id. (citing Moncrieffe v. Holder, 133 S. Ct. 1678, 1684-85 (2013)). However, if a statute is “divisible,” we employ a modified categorical approach. See id. at 352.
 
In Matter of Chairez-Castrejon, supra, at 352-53, the Board acknowledged that our earlier divisibility analysis was inconsistent with the approach announced by the United States Supreme Court in Descamps v. United States, 133 S. Ct. 2276 (2013). Therefore, we adopted the Court’s approach to divisibility, such that
[A] criminal statute is divisible, so as to warrant a modified categorical inquiry, only if (1) it lists multiple discrete offenses as enumerated alternatives or defines a single offense by reference to disjunctive sets of “ “ “elements,” more than one combination of which could support a conviction; and (2) at least one, but not all, of those listed offenses or combinations of disjunctive elements is a categorical match to the relevant generic standard.
 
Matter of Chairez-Castrejon, supra, at 353 (citing Descamps v. United States, 133 S Ct. at 2281, 2283). Under this approach, an element of an offense is a fact “about a crime which ‘[t]he Sixth Amendment contemplates that a jury--not a sentencing court--will find . . . unanimously and beyond a reasonable doubt.”DD’ Id. (quoting Descamps v. United States, 133 S. Ct. at 2288).
 
*3 In our decision, we noted that we are not given deference on this issue and, accordingly, we are “bound to apply divisibility consistently with the individual circuits’ interpretation of divisibility under Descamps.” Matter of Chairez-Castrejon, supra, at 354. However, we also stated that, if a particular circuit court has not applied divisibility under Descamps in a precedential decision, the Board would follow its own understanding of divisibility as set forth in Descamps. Id.
 
We are not aware of any precedential decision from the United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit applying divisibility under Descamps, particularly in the mens rea context at issue in this case. In Groves v. United States, 755 F.3d 588, 593 (7th Cir. 2014), the circuit court recognized that “the Supreme Court adopted the divisible/indivisible distinction as discussed [in a prior circuit court case].” Id. (citing Descamps v. United States, supra). However, this statement provides no clear resolution at this point as to how the circuit court will define divisibility post-Descamps.
 
The circuit court’s decision makes only a brief and passing reference to Descamps. Id. Moreover, an application of Descamps was not the dispositive issue in Groves, which primarily concerned a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Groves v. United States, 755 F.3d at 589-90. Inasmuch as the Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit has yet to interpret divisibility under Descamps, we will apply our own understanding of the Supreme Court’s decision. Chairez-Castrejon, supra, at 354; see also Descamps v. United States, 133 S. Ct. at 2282.
 
Consistent with the foregoing, we will affirm the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent’s statute of conviction is not divisible. Although subsection 508.030(1)(a) lists two mental states that may support a conviction, those mental states are not separate “elements” of the offense because a jury would not necessarily have to decide between them unanimously. Matter of Chairez-Castrejon, supra, at 353; see also Wells v. Commonwealth, 561 S.W.2d 85, 88 (Ken. 1978) (“[A] verdict can not be successfully attacked upon the ground that the jurors could have believed either of two theories of the case where both interpretations are supported by the evidence and the proof of either beyond a reasonable doubt constitutes the same offense.”). The state of Kentucky “permits conviction without jury agreement as to mental state” in certain cases (DHS Brief on Appeal, dated Nov. 20, 2014, at 11). Therefore, intent and recklessness are merely alternative “means” by which a defendant can commit an assault, not alternative “elements” of the offense. Matter of Chairez-Castrejon, supra, at 354.
 
*4 The respondent has not been convicted of a “crime of violence” under 18 U.S.C. § 16(a). Because subsection 508.030(1)(a) can be proven by reference to reckless conduct, it does not have as an element the deliberate “““use” of violent physical force against the person or property of another. See Matter of Chairez-Castrejon, supra, at 352. We are prohibited from considering the respondent’s record of conviction to determine whether his particular conduct was intentional or reckless. See Descamps v. United States, 133 S. Ct. at 2293 (“A court may use the modified approach only to determine which alternative element in a divisible statute formed the basis of the defendant’s conviction.”). Accordingly, the Department of Homeland Security has not carried its burden of establishing by clear and convincing evidence that the respondent is deportable. See section 240(c)(3)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(3).
 
The following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The Department of Homeland Security’s appeal is dismissed.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The term “crime of violence” is defined in relevant part as “an offense that has as an element the use, attempted use, or threatened use of physical force against the person or property of another.” 18 U.S.C. § 16(a) (2012). In this context, the term “use” denotes volition. See Leocal v. Ashcroft, 543 U.S. 1, 9 (2004). The parties agreed that the respondent was not convicted of a felony and, therefore, subsection 16(b) of title 18 of the United States Code does not apply to this case (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 22).

2014 WL 7691450 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALBERTO RUIZ-HERNANDEZ
File: AXXX XX9 098 - Dallas, TX
December 17, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey T. Jones, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
APPLICATION: Termination; remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s September 10, 2014, decision denying his motion to terminate proceedings and ordering him removed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility determinations, under the “““clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent does not contest that he pled guilty to possession of a controlled substance in violation of Texas Health and Safety Code section 481.115(b) and was convicted by the Texas 69th District Court of Moore County, Texas on August 4, 2014 (Exh. 1; I.J. at 2; Respondent’s Br. at 2-3). As part of his plea agreement, the respondent waived his right to appeal this conviction (Exh. 2). The respondent was then placed in removal proceedings by the Department of Homeland Security and charged with deportability under section 237(a)(2)(B)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i), based upon his drug-related offense, other than a single conviction for possession of 30 grams or less of marijuana (Exh. 1). Subsequently, the respondent filed a motion for a new trial and a motion for arrest in judgment based on ineffective assistance of counsel alleging that he was not properly apprised of the immigration consequences of his guilty plea.1
 
The respondent argues that based on the filing of this motion and subsequent appeal of the denial of this motion, his conviction is not final for immigration purposes. However, we do not agree and affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s motion for a new trial and motion for arrest in judgment are not a direct appeal, but rather, a collateral attack of the respondent’s conviction. As such, his conviction remains binding and final for immigration purposes unless and until it is overturned by the Texas state court. See Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996); see also Okabe v. INS, 671 F.2d 863 (5th Cir. 1982). Therefore, the Immigration Judge properly found that the respondent’s conviction subjected him to deportability under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act and ordered him removed. Furthermore, we see no reason to remand based on a pending appeal of a motion for post-conviction relief. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and his motion to remand denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

According to the Notice of Appeal filed with the respondent’s motion, the 69th District Court of Moore County, Texas, denied the respondent’s motion for new trial and motion for arrest in judgment (Motion at Tab A).

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
    1  Decision Vacated by IN RE: SAMUEL RUBIO-COVARRUBIAS, BIA, May 24, 2016
2014 WL 7691453 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAMUEL RUBIO-COVARRUBIAS
File: AXXX XX9 208 - Dallas, TX1
December 17, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Patricia K. Conkright, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico who was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident on April 9, 1996, appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated August 26, 2014. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii). As the respondent did not apply for any form of relief, the Immigration Judge ordered his removal from this country. On appeal, the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his request for a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues only that the Immigration Judge erred in denying him a continuance to collaterally attack the sexual assault conviction that forms the basis for his removability. However, at no time did the respondent request a continuance before the Immigration Judge. At the final hearing on August 26, 2014, the respondent’s counsel stated that the respondent was pursuing a writ of habeas corpus with the state court (Tr. at 12). However, counsel conceded that the respondent was presently ineligible for any relief from removal, and did not request a continuance (Tr. at 9-14).
 
We decline to remand these removal proceedings to the Immigration Judge to consider the evidence that the respondent has presented on appeal. Even though the respondent has presented evidence that, subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s decision in this matter, he filed a writ of habeas corpus with the Texas courts, his conviction remains final and effective for immigration purposes. See Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992) (“The possibility of a decision on any post-conviction motion that has been filed does not affect our finding that the respondent is deportable.”). Moreover, the respondent’s desire to seek post-conviction relief does not amount to “good cause” for a continuance. See Cabral v. Holder, 632 F.3d 886, 890 (5th Cir. 2011). Even though the respondent apparently believes that, in light of Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010), his criminal defense attorney provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel, “Padilla also does not suggest that immigration proceedings must be stayed while a Padilla-type claim is being pursued in post[-]conviction proceedings.” Milton v. Holder, 425 F.App’x. 407, 409 (5th Cir. 2011). The respondent’s conviction evidently remains undisturbed, as he has yet to indicate that he has received a vacatur. Moreover, the likelihood of success on the respondent’s motion for post-conviction relief is speculative. Thus, inasmuch as the evidence offered on appeal does not establish that, if proceedings were remanded, the outcome of this case would likely be different, we conclude that the respondent has not established that a remand is warranted. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD

2014 WL 7691412 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MUHAMAD YUSUF LUWAGA
File: AXXX XX0 414 - Boston, MA
December 12, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robin N. Miller, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; waiver of inadmissibility
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s February 20, 2013, decision denying his application for a waiver of inadmissibility in conjunction with adjustment of status under sections 212(i) and 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1182(i), 1255,1 We have not received a response from the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
The respondent challenges the Immigration Judge’s ruling that he is inadmissible under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act. 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i).2 It is conceded that at the respondent’s initial merits hearing on October 25, 2007, the respondent testified falsely that the signature on the application for adjustment of status, Form I-485, was his (I.J.2 at 2; Tr. at 30). The Immigration Judge found that this false statement under oath rendered the respondent inadmissible and that the false statement was “material” to his eligibility for adjustment of status, reasoning that there was no application before the Court in the absence of a properly sworn Form I-485 (I.J.2 at 2-3; Tr. at 111). The respondent filed a new application for adjustment of status at the remanded hearing. Additional evidence was submitted and further testimony of both the respondent and his wife was taken.
 
The test for materiality of a misrepresentation is generally whether the respondent potentially would be inadmissible or ineligible for relief under the true facts, or whether the misrepresentation would tend to cut off a line of inquiry relevant to the respondent’s eligibility for admission or relief. See Matter of S- and B-C-, 9 I&N Dec. 436, 447 (A.G. 1961). The Supreme Court’s iteration of the materiality of a false representation in denaturalization proceedings is instructive in assessing a similar provision. In Kungys v. U.S., 485 U.S. 759 (1988), the Immigration and Naturalization Service sought to have the respondent’s citizenship revoked based upon false statements made as to his date and place of birth. The Court ruled that a false representation is material when it would lead to a “natural tendency to end a possible line of questioning or otherwise influence a decision on his application.” Kungys v. U.S., supra, at 772. See also Matter of P-S-H-, 26 I&N Dec. 329, 336-38 (BIA 2014); Matter of M-L-M-A-, 26 I&N Dec. 360 (BIA 2014).
 
*2 It is obvious that if the respondent had truthfully told the Immigration Judge that the signature on the application was not his, further inquiry would have been made at the first hearing. Nonetheless, there is no finding by the Immigration Judge that the information in the original Form I-485 was false or misleading. Nor is the identity of the signer of the original Form I-485 relevant to the respondent’s ultimate admissibility or eligibility for relief, particularly in the context of a substantiated claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Therefore, we do not sustain the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent was inadmissible under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act. Thus, we sustain the appeal and conclude that the respondent did not need a waiver under section 212(i) of the Act in order to be eligible for adjustment. In light of our decision in this regard the issue of whether a continuance should have been granted is rendered moot.
 
The Immigration Judge’s decision did not address the respondent’s eligibility for adjustment of status, in the event the waiver was found not to be necessary. The Immigration Judge did not make a credibility finding or a decision on whether the respondent warrants the favorable exercise of discretion. See section 245(a) of the Act. Therefore, we will remand the record for further factual findings and a new decision.
 
Accordingly, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD
Board Member Roger A. Pauley respectfully dissents without opinion.
 
Footnotes

1

This matter was previously before us and was remanded by order dated August 18, 2009, for additional fact finding and further consideration of the application for relief. The Immigration Judge’s decision of February 20, 2013, will be referred to as “I.J.2”; the earlier decision of October 25, 2007, will be referred to as “I.J.1”. The Immigration Judge initially denied the respondent’s application for adjustment of status on October 25, 2007, finding the respondent and his wife had not testified credibly and that they were engaged in a fraudulent marriage (I.J.1 at 2). The case was remanded to the Immigration Judge upon a finding that the respondent had established prejudice due to ineffective assistance of his prior attorney. Upon remand, the parties were given the opportunity to supplement the record regarding the application for relief.

2

Section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) states:
Any alien who, by fraud or willfully misrepresenting a material fact, seeks to procure (or has sought to procure or has procured) a visa, other documentation, or admission into the United States or other benefit provided under this Act is inadmissible.

IN RE: FRANCISCO OLEGARIO BUENO-PIZARRO
File: AXXX XX9 830 - Newark, NJ

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Arturo Suarez-Silverio, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joseph Silver
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; rescission
 
*1 On October 16, 2014, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind an order of removal entered in absentia on May 4, 2001. On November 3, 2014, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen and rescind which the Department of Homeland Security opposes. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3), 1003.2(b)(4)(ii). The motion will be denied.
 
In our prior order, we noted that the respondent provided no meaningful explanation for his failure to appear at the removal hearing on May 1, 2001. The respondent now claims that his former attorney did not properly investigate the facts of his case, and the respondent asserts, for the first time, that he did not receive proper notice of the hearing.
 
Initially, we find that the respondent has not offered evidence of compliance with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel before the Board as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). See Motion Exhibits. See also Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127, 132 (3d Cir. 2001). Further, ineffective assistance of counsel is not apparent on the face of the record. See Lu v. Ashcroft, supra, at 134. Indeed, the respondent has not shown that he suffered prejudice as a result of a former attorney’s handling of his case. See Fadiga v. Attorney General, 488 F.3d 142, 157, 159-60 (3rd Cir. 2007); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640. Although the respondent maintains that he did not receive notice of the hearing, he has not elaborated any claim that notice of the hearing was improperly served.1
 
In sum, the respondent has not set forth a viable ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Moreover, he has neither shown that notice of the removal hearing was improper, nor provided a persuasive explanation for his failure to appear at the hearing. See sections 240(b)(5)(A), (C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(5)(A), (C). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion, which is untimely and number-barred, will be denied. See sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is also denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen and rescind is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

The Notice to Appear was personally served on the respondent on October 23, 2000, and the warning regarding the requirement to notify the Immigration Court of any change of address was provided to the respondent in Spanish at that time. See Exhibit 1. The record also reveals that on February 22, 2001, the Immigration Judge granted a motion to change venue which was accompanied by a form indicating that the respondent’s address had changed and that the respondent was reminded of the requirement to notify the Immigration Court of any change of address. See section 239(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a).

IN RE: KOYODE AKINNIYI
File: AXXX XX4 174 - York, PA
December 9, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fatai Suleman, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Nigeria, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s July 24, 2014, decision denying his motion to reopen his removal proceedings.1 The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. See generally, Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). For the reasons articulated in his decision, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established an exception to the time limitations for filing a motion to reopen or that sua sponte reopening is warranted.2 See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (Board exercises its sua sponte authority “sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of the time and number limited in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations.”)
 
The respondent has not shown that his prior representatives provided ineffective representation or, even if they did, that he was prejudiced by it (I.J. at 7, 8). Moreover, he has not shown that he exercised due diligence in his removal proceedings (I.J. at 7). Accordingly, he has not shown that a truly exceptional situation warrants reopening. Matter of G-D-, supra. For these reasons, we further conclude that the respondent has not established that ineffective assistance of counsel warrants the equitable tolling of the filing deadline for his motion to reopen. See Alzaarir v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 639 F.3d 86, 90 (3d Cir. 2011).
 
We further agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established that changed conditions arising in Nigeria provide an exception to the filing deadline for his motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i). The respondent has not presented evidence from which it is reasonable to conclude that conditions in Nigeria have materially changed since the time of his hearing in such a way that would provide a factual basis that did not previously exist for his claim for asylum, withholding of removal, or protection under the Convention Against Torture. We are not persuaded by the respondent’s contention on appeal that the Immigration Judge required “a conclusive showing” of eligibility for asylum for the purpose of establishing that reopening is warranted3 (Respondent’s Notice of Appeal). Rather, the Immigration Judge properly determined that the respondent has not shown prima facie eligibility for asylum, withholding of removal, or protection under the Convention Against Torture (I.J. at 9-12). See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471-72 (BIA 1992); see also INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988) (a motion to reopen may be denied when a movant does not establish prima facie eligibility for the underlying relief sought). For these reasons, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision and will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD

3

Sections 208(b)(2)(A)(ii) and 208(b)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1158(b)(2)(A)(ii), (B)(i). The respondent’s allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel are not related to the Immigration Judge’s January 29, 2003, determination that he is removable as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony.

APPLICATION: Voluntary departure; continuance; remand; termination

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    1  Case Reopened by IN RE: RENATO DE BARTOLO, BIA, December 11, 2015
2014 WL 7508457 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RENATO DE BARTOLO
File: AXXX XX0 617 - Bedford, OH
November 18, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Margaret W. Wong, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Bruce D. Imbacuan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Italy, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 9, 2014. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Before the Immigration Judge, on June 18, 2014, the respondent was granted a continuance to enable him to pursue post-conviction relief with regard to the conviction that serves as the basis for removal, and renders him ineligible for relief from removal. The respondent alleged that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of this conviction, and he had filed a motion to vacate it.2 However, on July 9, 2014, the Immigration Judge denied a further continuance (I.J. at 2). On appeal, the respondent challenges denial of the continuance. However, the respondent also indicates that the motion to vacate the conviction was denied.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).3 We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
While the respondent was free to pursue the post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction did not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010), the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. ___, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

3

In determining whether good cause exists to continue removal proceedings, a variety of factors may be considered, including, but not limited to: the Department of Homeland Security=s response to the motion to continue and the reason for the continuance and any other relevant procedural factors. See Matter of Hashmi, supra.

U.S. Department of Justice

IN RE: JONI PINEDA-SANCHEZ

November 14, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Derek Cavada, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on June 16, 2014, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on June 2, 1999. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider our decision. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, the respondent has two timely motions to reconsider filed on his behalf by two different attorneys. Attorney Meighan L. Vargas filed a timely motion to reconsider on the respondent’s behalf alleging ineffective assistance of counsel of the respondent’s prior representation below and on appeal by the Aponte and Associates firm. Simultaneously, Derek Cavada of Aponte and Associates filed a timely motion to reconsider on the respondent’s behalf. Subsequently, on September 22, 2014, Attorney Vargas withdrew her representation of the respondent before the Board.1 Therefore, we will deny the motion to reconsider filed by Attorney Vargas as moot.
 
Turning to the motion to reconsider filed by Derek Cavada of Aponte and Associates. This motion does not convince us that reconsideration of our previous decision is warranted. A party seeking reconsideration requests that the original decision be reexamined in light of alleged legal or factual errors, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). Here, the respondent largely raises the same or similar arguments in the motion to reconsider as were raised in his prior motion to reopen and subsequent appeal. We considered those arguments and found them unpersuasive when viewed within the record as a whole before we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent has not rebutted the presumption of delivery of the Notice of Hearing (“NOH”) in his proceedings and dismissed his appeal. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 58 (stating that “a motion to reconsider is not a process by which a party may submit, in essence, the same brief presented on appeal . . .”). Disagreement with the result is not sufficient.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent also filed a motion for substitution of counsel with the Board on September 8, 2014, requesting that he be represented by Derek Cavada of Aponte and Associates.
