2014 WL 6883012 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IFEANYI TED PHILIPS A.K.A. ONUNKWO PHILIPS
File: AXXX XX0 608 - Chicago, IL
November 10, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Danielle Beach-Oswald, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Marc A. Jones
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Nigeria, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s March 28, 2013, decision denying his request for a continuance and ordering him removed. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record reflects that these removal proceedings were instituted with the filing of a Notice to Appear in November 2007. Pursuant to the respondent’s concession, the Immigration Judge found him removable as charged (I.J. at 2-3; Tr. at 3). Thereafter, following a change of venue, these proceedings were continued nine times over the course of more than 5 years to await adjudication of two visa petitions consecutively filed by the respondent’s United States citizen spouse. At all except the final hearing in this case, the respondent was represented by counsel. On the date on which the Immigration Judge issued his decision, both visa petitions filed by the respondent’s spouse had been denied, and the appeals of those denials had been dismissed. The respondent requested an additional continuance in order to pursue a petition as an abused spouse of a United States citizen, Form I-360, with the United States Immigration and Citizenship Services (“USCIS”). The Immigration Judge denied the request for a continuance and, after ascertaining that the respondent did not seek voluntary departure, ordered the respondent removed.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred by finding that section 204(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1154(c) would bar any subsequent visa petition filed on his behalf because the Board did not reach the merits of either appeal as they were dismissed on procedural grounds. He also contends on appeal that his application for relief was materially prejudiced by ineffective assistance of counsel and that the Immigration Judge erred by denying his request for a continuance because he was unrepresented and because he had a pending I-360 petition.
 
*2 Contrary to the respondent’s contention on appeal, the Immigration Judge did not make a finding that section 204(c) of the Act would necessarily bar the approval of a visa petition filed on his behalf. Rather, the Immigration Judge noted that the DHS had found that the section 204(c) bar applied (I.J. at 4). The Immigration Judge further noted that the respondent had been unable to establish prima facie eligibility for permanent residency (I.J. at 4).
 
The respondent contends that he received ineffective assistance of counsel in visa petition proceedings relating to the petitions filed by his spouse on his behalf because the appeals of the USCIS’s denial of the visa petitions were both dismissed on procedural grounds resulting from errors by the attorneys who filed them. However, visa petition proceedings are separate and apart from removal proceedings. Matter of Aurelio, 19 I&N Dec. 458, 460 (BIA 1987). Moreover, only the respondent’s spouse as the petitioner, not the respondent as the beneficiary, is the affected party in those visa petition proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.3(a)(2); see also Matter of Sano, 19 I&N Dec. 299 (BIA 1985); Matter of DaBaase, 16 I&N Dec. 720 (BIA 1979); Matter of Kurys, 11 I&N Dec. 315 (BIA 1965). Even if we were to consider the respondent as an affected party for the purposes of this appeal, he has not shown that he was prejudiced by the attorneys’ errors. The denial of a visa petition for failure to meet the required burden of proof does not preclude the filing of another petition by the same petitioner on behalf of the same beneficiary. However, the respondent has not presented evidence from which it is reasonable to conclude that there is any likelihood that he would be the beneficiary of an approved visa petition that would support an application for adjustment of status.
 
We are not persuaded by the respondent’s contention on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred by not granting him an additional continuance. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987) (the decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge, if good cause is shown, and that decision will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. The Immigration Judge granted the respondent several lengthy continuances (I.J. at 2-3). At the time the Immigration Judge issued his decision, there was no indication in the record that any visa petition filed on the respondent’s behalf had been approved or remained unadjudicated. Moreover, the record does not include, and the respondent has not presented on appeal, evidence that he has filed a Form I-360 with the USCIS. Furthermore, although the respondent was unrepresented at the final hearing, he had been represented by counsel for many years. Under these circumstances, the record does not support the conclusion that a continuance was warranted as a result of the respondent appearing pro se at the last hearing. Accordingly, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent had not established good cause for an additional continuance. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983) (holding that a decision denying a motion for a continuance will not be reversed on appeal unless the alien establishes, by a full and specific articulation of the particular facts involved or evidence which he would have presented, that the denial caused him actual prejudice and harm, and materially affected the outcome of his case).
 
*3 The respondent has submitted additional evidence with his appeal brief, comprising documentation related to the visa petitions filed by his spouse. The Board generally does not consider evidence that was not previously considered because we are an appellate body and, accordingly, we review the record that was created before the Immigration Judge. Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984). We therefore construe the respondent’s submission of additional evidence with his appeal brief as a motion to remand, which must conform to the same standards as a motion to reopen. To warrant remand, the motion must be supported by evidence that was previously unavailable and that “““would likely change the result in the case.” Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). This evidence, which reflects that the visa petitions were denied and the appeals dismissed, is unlikely to change the result in his case. See Matter of Coelho, supra, at 472. Accordingly, the respondent has not demonstrated that a remand of the record is warranted based on this additional evidence. See Matter of Coelho, supra, at 471. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 6883012 (BIA)

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANGELO NONNATO YZQUIERDO LORENZO CARMENCITA U LORENZO

AXXX XX8 857
November 6, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Eva L. Carrasco, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status
 
The respondents appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 10, 2013, reissuing his decision dated November 7, 2012, denying their application for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1255. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii). As the respondents’ applications were filed after May 11, 2005, they are governed by the provisions of the REAL ID Act. Matter of Almanza Arenas, 24 I&N Dec. 771, 774 75 (BIA 2009).
 
The Immigration Judge found the respondents, natives and citizens of the Philippines, removable as charged (I.J. Dec. dated November 7, 2012, at 1; Tr. at 15-21, 26-32, 36-37). The Immigration Judge found the respondents ineligible for adjustment of status under sections 245(a) and 245(i) because they did not (other than through no fault of their own or for technical reasons) maintain continuous lawful status as required by section 245(c)(2) of the Act (I.J. Dec. dated November 7, 2012, at 1-2).
 
We have considered the respondent’s appellate arguments. However, we adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994).
 
The Immigration Judge properly concluded that the respondents have not maintained the continuous lawful status necessary for adjustment of status. See section 245(c)(2) of the Act. The respondents’ period of lawful status terminated on the date their nonimmigrant status expired. 8 C.F.R. § 1245.1(d)(1)(ii) (“lawful immigration status” refers to the immigration status of, inter alia, an alien admitted to the United States in nonimmigrant status whose initial period of admission has not expired or whose nonimmigrant status has been extended). The respondents do not dispute that they failed to maintain lawful status (Respondent’s Brief at 10).
 
The Immigration Judge properly rejected the respondents’ argument that their failure to maintain continuous lawful status since entry into the United States was through no fault of their own or for technical reasons due to their reliance on the incorrect advice of counsel before the USCIS. Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004) (finding that erroneous advice received outside and in advance of immigration proceedings did not affect fairness of proceedings and did not provide basis for reopening due to ineffective assistance of counsel). The regulations define the phrase “other than through no fault of his or her own or for technical reasons,” explicitly limiting the meaning of that phrase to four scenarios. 8 C.F.R. § 1245.1(d)(2). Even assuming, arguendo, that the respondents established ineffective assistance of former counsel in providing advice on maintaining their lawful status,1 such a scenario does not fall within one of the enumerated circumstances. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.1(d)(2) (providing that the meaning of the phrase “(other than through no fault of his or her own or for technical reasons)” is limited to those circumstances described in the regulation). See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003); Colmenar v. INS, 210 F.3d 967, 971 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
*2 Thus, the respondents are not eligible for adjustment of status because they did not maintain continuous lawful status as required by section 245(c)(2) of the Act, and they cannot benefit from the exemption in section 245(k) of the Act because the period during which they were not in lawful status exceeded 180 days. See section 245(k)(2)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(k)(2)(A). We therefore affirm the Immigration Judge’s pretermission of the respondents’ applications for adjustment of status and will dismiss the appeal.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondents’ appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
*3 Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge properly rejected the respondents’ claim of ineffective assistance of counsel based on their failure to comply with the procedural requirements outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Moreover, former counsel’s legal representation before the USCIS and prior to these removal proceedings, is beyond the scope of these removal proceedings. Balam-Chuc v. Mukasey, 547 F.3d 1044, 1050-51 (9th Cir. 2008) (holding that “the Fifth Amendment simply does not apply to the preparation and filing of a petition that does not relate to the fundamental fairness of an ongoing [removal] proceeding.”); Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004) (noting that “unfortunate immigration-law advice” prior to a removal proceeding does not taint the fairness of the removal hearing itself in violation of the Fifth Amendment), amended by 404 F.3d 1105 (9th Cir. 2005).

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALBINO GARCIA-PACHECO
File: AXXX XX5 204 - Los Angeles, CA
November 6, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard Bambl, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reissuance
 
*1 On December 14, 2000, the Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s January 25, 1999, decision, and on October 1, 2014, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen the proceedings. The motion, which is untimely by nearly 14 years, will be denied. See sections 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The motion does not demonstrate that an exception to the filing deadline applies or that an exceptional situation is present in this case to warrant reopening sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that “[t]he power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(a), (c)(3).
 
To the extent that the respondent seeks equitable tolling of the filing deadline, he has not shown that he suffered prejudice as a result of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent asserts that former counsel did not inform him that an appeal had been filed and dismissed. He further claims that until 2014, former counsel led him to believe that his case remained pending. The respondent, however, has neither contested his removability nor identified any form of relief for which he may have been eligible either at the time of the Immigration Judge’s decision or during the period in which a timely motion to reopen could have been filed in this case. See Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004) (explaining that to demonstrate prejudice, the alien must show that counsel’s performance was so inadequate that it “may have affected the outcome of the proceedings”).
 
The respondent also seeks reopening for reissuance of our prior order to enable him to file a petition for judicial review. The respondent, however, has not alleged any error in the Board’s affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s decision nor has the respondent demonstrated that reissuance is warranted. Therefore, we decline to reissue our prior order. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 323 (1992) (noting that motions to reopen immigration proceedings are disfavored, especially where, as a general matter, every delay works to the advantage of the alien who wishes merely to remain in the United States).
 
*2 For the foregoing reasons, the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HECTOR RAMIREZ-GUTIERREZ
File: AXXX XX8 204 - Pearsall, TX
November 3, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Juan R. Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Justin Nielsen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 30, 2014. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
At the hearing, the respondent requested a continuance of proceedings to enable him to pursue post-conviction relief with regard to the November 12, 2013, conviction that serves as the basis for removal, and renders him ineligible for relief from removal. The respondent alleged that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of this conviction, and he has filed a motion to vacate it. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) objected to a continuance (Tr. at 8-11).2 The Immigration Judge denied the requested continuance (I.J. at 2). On appeal, the respondent challenges denial of the continuance. However, the respondent does not indicate that the conviction has been vacated.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009). We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue the post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010), the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. ___, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

IN RE: YURY VITALYERICH PARYEV
File: AXXX XX6 987 - Chicago, IL
October 31, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sarah Elizabeth Dill, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Marc A. Jones
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent moves the Board to reopen his removal proceedings and remand the record to the Immigration Court for further proceedings regarding his application for adjustment of status. Sections 240(c)(7), 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7); 1255(a); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). We will deny the motion.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including determinations of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We will deny the motion to reopen because the respondent has not presented new, previously unavailable evidence that is material to his application for adjustment of status. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). In our April 7, 2014, decision, we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s application in the exercise of discretion because the respondent’s criminal history outweighed the positive equities in the record. See Portillo-Rendon v. Holder, 662 F.3d 815 (7th Cir. 2011).
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen cites the equities in his favor, particularly his family ties in the United States, and is supported with a number of letters from friends and family, including a letter from the respondent’s United States citizen wife (Respondent’s Motion at 10-11 and Exhs. B-C). The respondent cites the fact that he and his wife now have twin sons, an equity that was not presented during the February 7, 2012, individual hearing below because the respondent’s wife was 1-month pregnant at the time (Respondent’s Motion at 10-11). The respondent overlooks the fact that the Board applied de novo review to the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 7, 2012, decision, and fully considered the positive equities established in the record, including the fact that the respondent became a father to twin sons during the pendency of the appeal, as well as evidence that he stopped drinking alcohol and completed a state-sponsored traffic safety program to regain his driving privileges (April 7, 2014, Order at 2). We nevertheless held that the respondent’s criminal history, which involved numerous serious driving offenses, including one during the pendency of his removal proceedings where he drove under the influence and flipped his car, outweighed the equities in his favor, and we dismissed his appeal (see April 7, 2014, Board Decision).1
 
*2 The respondent also contends that the development of political unrest and hostilities in the eastern portion of Ukraine between the Ukrainian government and those aligned with Russia is evidence of changed country conditions that supports reopening (Respondent’s Motion at 10-11 and Exhs. D-F). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii). The respondent’s argument is without merit because he does not seek reopening in order to pursue a claim for asylum and related relief. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii); Matter of S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247 (BIA 2007). Moreover, even if he did seek reopening to pursue asylum and related relief, we would deny the motion because generalized conditions affecting large segments of the population do not, by themselves, prove that an individual faces persecution. Raghunathan v. Holder, 604 F.3d 371, 378 (7th Cir. 2010).
 
The respondent also seeks reopening on the grounds that his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Motion at 3-4, 11). The respondent has not complied with any of the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). See also Marinov v. Holder, 687 F.3d 365, 368-70 (7th Cir. 2012) (no demonstration of ineffective assistance of counsel when the respondent fails to comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639).
 
Furthermore, the respondent has not demonstrated the requisite prejudice to support such a claim because he has not presented any evidence that would change the outcome. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640 (successful ineffective assistance of counsel claim requires a showing that the respondent was prejudiced by counsel’s deficient performance). We reviewed de novo the equities in the respondent’s favor, including the equities that developed and were presented on appeal, and we decided that the respondent’s criminal history outweighed those positive equities (see April 7, 2014, Board Decision). The respondent’s motion to reopen presents no evidence that would change our calculation, and he has therefore not demonstrated any prejudice from his prior attorney’s assistance.
 
Finally, the respondent argues that his due process rights were violated in the prior proceedings (Respondent’s Motion at 4-5, 10). The respondent’s argument is without merit as he was provided a full and fair hearing in support of his application for adjustment of status, and he has made no showing that he was in any manner prevented from fully presenting his case. Hamid v. Gonzales, 417 F.3d 642, 645-46 (7th Cir. 2005) (successful due process challenge requires applicant to show that he was deprived of a meaningful opportunity to be heard, and that the deprivation potentially affected the outcome). Neither the Immigration Judge nor the Board abused its discretion in deciding that the respondent’s criminal history outweigh the equities in his favor. See Marinov v. Holder, supra, at 368 (no demonstration of abuse of discretion unless Board decision is “made without rational explanation, inexplicably departed from established policies, or rested on an impermissible basis.”).
 
*3 For these reasons, we will deny the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In this regard, the respondent’s motion is more akin to a motion to reconsider as he essentially argues that we should have weighed the equities differently, and in his favor. The respondent’s argument is without support as he has not demonstrated any material error of law or fact in our April 7, 2014, decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); see Matter of 0-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006).

MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:

Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopen
 
*1 ORDER:
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Brazil. On June 10, 2014, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal and extended the time within which he may voluntarily depart.1 On August 8, 2014, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Since the respondent filed his motion prior to expiration of the voluntary departure period, his grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1). The Department of Homeland Security objects to the granting of this motion.
 
The respondent seeks reopening based on a number of allegations listed by new counsel regarding the performance of former counsel. The motion shall be denied because the respondent has not complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent has not submitted sufficient documentary evidence indicating that he filed a state bar complaint or that former counsel was notified of the complaint and afforded the opportunity to respond to the respondent’s specific claims. Former counsel’s response would have been particularly helpful here in assessing the respondent’s allegations. Since the respondent has not complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, the motion is denied. The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

The Immigration Judge mistakenly granted the respondent a period of voluntary departure beyond the statutorily mandated period of 60 days (Cf. I.J. Dec. at 12 with I.J. Order dated 10/9/12). Section 240B(b)(2), 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b)(2). Since the respondent’s voluntary departure grant has been automatically terminated by the filing of this motion, the error is moot.

2014 WL 6882893 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GABRIEL RAMON RAMIREZ-SOLIS
File: AXXX XX2 547 - Harlingen, TX
October 30, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George E. Crimarco, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s February 27, 2014, decision denying his motion to reopen removal proceedings. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on September 12, 2005. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2013). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion and judgment. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent challenges the Immigration Judge’s determination that, although ineffective assistance of counsel is an exceptional circumstance that may warrant reopening, he did not comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent argues that compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, is impossible where the individual who gave ineffective assistance is a notario, rather than an attorney. Additionally, he asserts that proceedings should have been reopened sua sponte to allow him to apply for a provisional unlawful presence waiver.
 
We concur with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s motion to reopen, which was filed more than 8 years after the entry of the removal order, was untimely (I.J. at 1-2). Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i) (requiring that a motion to reopen to rescind an in absentia order of removal based on exceptional circumstances be filed within 180 days of the entry of the removal order). The respondent has not shown that he acted diligently, such that the Immigration Judge should have equitably tolled the deadline for filing a motion to reopen.
 
Moreover, compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, is not impossible even in cases involving notarios or other individuals who provide legal advice but are not licensed attorneys. At a minimum, an aggrieved individual can submit an affidavit setting forth the details of the agreement entered into with the notario and notify the notario of the allegations against him and provide him the opportunity to respond. Further, in many states, an aggrieved individual can complain to appropriate disciplinary authorities, who may choose to sanction the individual for unauthorized practice of law. See, e.g., Consumer Pamphlet: Filing an Unlicensed Practice of Law Complaint, Florida Bar (last revised March 2014), available at http:// www.floridabar.org/TFB/TFBConsum.nsf/48e76203493b82ad852567090070c9b9/59cac57c8be11c2085256b2f006c58a5? OpenDocument; Online Complaint Form, Texas UPL Committee, http:// www.txuplc.org/complaint/Home/Index (last visited Oct. 7, 2014). In situations where a bar complaint is not possible or practical, Matter of Lozada only requires an explanation of why such a complaint was not filed. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. Thus, we see no justification for the respondent’s failure to comply with Matter of Lozada.
 
*2 In addition, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated an exceptional situation warranting reopening sua sponte. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Becoming eligible for an immigration benefit years after the entry of a removal order is not an exceptional situation, and does not warrant the extraordinary equitable remedy of circumventing the statutory and regulatory restrictions on reopening.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 6882893 (BIA)
End of Document

2014 WL 6882890 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BYRON ESTUARDO MATEO AGUSTIN
File: AXXX XX5 121 - Hartford, CT
October 30, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard H. G. Cunningham, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)] - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Guatemala. On January 31, 2012, while represented by former counsel, the respondent conceded removability and did not seek relief from removal (I.J. at 1). Therefore, the Immigration Judge ordered his removal and the respondent waived appeal (I.J. at 1). The respondent now appeals the August 2, 2013, denial of a motion to reopen he filed with the Immigration Court on July 22, 2013. The Immigration Judge concluded that the motion was untimely and not subject to an exception to the time bar. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review issues of law, discretion, or judgment de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The January 31, 2012, decision ordering the respondent’s removal became final upon the waiver of appeal. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39. As the respondent did not file his motion to reopen within 90 days of this date, we affirm the holding that the motion to reopen is time-barred (I.J. at 1). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Furthermore, the motion is not to apply for asylum, withholding of removal, or protection under the Convention Against Torture on the basis of previously unavailable evidence of changed country conditions. The respondent thus has not shown eligibility for the exception to the time bar at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i) (I.J. at 1).
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that since the hearing, he has become eligible for Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals. However, this form of relief is granted in the discretion of United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”) after filing of Form I-821D. Immigration Judges and the Board do not have jurisdiction to grant Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals. The respondent has not explained how the possibility of USCIS granting his Form I-821D qualifies him for an exception to the time bar in removal proceedings (I.J. at 1). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). Moreover, while the respondent submitted a Form I-821D with his motion, he has not presented evidence that it was filed with USCIS or shown prima facie eligibility for deferred action (I.J. at 1). Cf. Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009) (recognizing that a continuance may be warranted where an alien has demonstrated that he is the beneficiary of a pending immigrant visa petition and established a likelihood of success on an application for adjustment of status).
 
*2 Finally, the respondent states that his former attorney committed ineffective assistance by waiving his right to pursue other avenues of relief. In support of this allegation, the respondent submits a personal affidavit. The respondent has not substantially complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 46-48 (2d Cir. 2005); see also Matter of Compean, Bangaly, and J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (reaffirming the Matter of Lozada framework). Also, since the respondent has not articulated prima facie eligibility for any form of relief available in removal proceedings, he has not shown prejudice. See Rabiu v. INS, 41 F.3d 879, 882 (2d Cir. 1994). Therefore, the respondent has not demonstrated that tolling of the 90-day reopening deadline is appropriate on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 134, 129-30 (2d Cir. 2000).
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 6882890 (BIA)
End of Document

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROMUALD BOROWSKI
File: AXXX XX4 925 - Chicago, IL
October 28, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mariusz Zdyrski, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sarah E. Zeld
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Poland, has filed a timely appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 19, 2013, denying his request for a continuance. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The factual findings of the Immigration Judge are reviewed to determine whether they are “clearly erroneous.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). All other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges, including legal and discretionary determinations and applications of law to fact, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We find no clear error in the factual findings of the Immigration Judge in this case. Upon de novo review of the questions of law, discretion, and judgment presented, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance.
 
The Immigration Judge correctly noted that the Notice to Appear was issued in February 2009; the first hearing was held in March 2009, and the respondent appeared with counsel and was granted a continuance for attorney preparation; and continuances were granted on October 26, 2010 and August 23, 2011, so that counsel could provide proof of what efforts were being made in pursuing a U-Visa for the respondent (I.J. at 1-2; Tr. at 4, 9, 11, 14). On September 20, 2011, prior counsel submitted a status letter to the court regarding the potential of a U-Visa for the respondent, indicating that the agencies they had contacted no longer wished to pursue the matter or act on the respondent’s behalf (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 27; Status Letter from Y. Judd Azulay of Azulay Seiden Law Group dated September 20, 2011). On February 7, 2012 a master calendar hearing was held at which the respondent appeared with a new attorney and requested a continuance so that he could pursue a visa petition filing by the respondent’s brother and a request for the exercise of prosecutorial discretion by DHS (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 16-20). The Immigration Judge warned both the attorney and the respondent directly that this was the last continuance in the respondent’s case, and that if he did not complete applications for relief by the date of the next scheduled hearing, he would only have voluntary departure as available relief at the final hearing (Tr. at 20-21). On January 28, 2013, the Immigration Judge granted a motion to withdraw to the respondent’s new attorney (Motion to Withdraw, Jesus Ruiz-Velasco, Esquire, dated January 28, 2013). At the final master calendar hearing held on February 19, 2013, the respondent appeared pro se and requested a continuance to find another attorney (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 24). The Immigration Judge then asked several questions and determined that the respondent was not eligible for cancellation of removal, that the respondent was seeking a continuance to await his brother naturalizing and filing a visa petition on his behalf, and to await possible comprehensive immigration reform legislation, and the respondent chose not to pursue voluntary departure (I.J. at 2-3; Tr. at 24-26).
 
*2 The respondent failed to establish good cause for a continuance. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 355 (BIA 1983); see generally Calma v. Holder, 663 F.3d 868 (7th Cir. 2011) (I.J. consideration of speculative nature of respondent’s hopes and lengthy amount of time it would take for processing applications was appropriate); Afzal v. Holder, 559 F.3d 677 (7th Cir. 2009) (jurisdiction to review continuance denials); Hadayat v. Gonzales, 458 F.3d 659 (7th Cir. 2006) (no sibling visa immediately available); compare Subhan v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 591 (7th Cir. 2004) (continuance denial based only on length of proceedings not upheld). We agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that a continuance was not warranted where the respondent was not eligible for any relief on the date of the last hearing, no applications or petitions were pending or had been approved, and the hope of comprehensive immigration reform by Congress was merely speculative (I.J. at 3).
 
Contrary to the respondent’s argument on appeal, we do not find that he was denied “right to counsel,” especially where his case had been pending for 4 years, his hearings had been continued several times, and he had been represented by various attorneys at all but his last hearing, which was the result of an attorney withdrawal 3 weeks before the hearing, of which the respondent was fully aware (Resp. Br. at 3-4; I.J. at 1-3; Motion to Withdraw). See, e.g., Matter of Compean, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009) (aliens in removal proceedings have a statutory privilege to retain private counsel but they have no constitutional, statutory, or regulatory right to counsel). For the same reasons, we find that Matter of C-B-, 25 I&N Dec. 888 (BIA 2012), is not applicable here (Resp. Br. at 4).
 
Furthermore, the respondent has not shown any prejudice which resulted from the denial of a continuance. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987); Matter of Sibrun, supra. To date, he has not shown that he has any pending prima facie approvable applications for relief from removal or any approved applications. Id.
 
The denial of a continuance will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing, and here the respondent has not shown such (Respondent’s Appeal Brief; I.J. at 1-3; Tr. at 1-28). See Matter of Perez-Andrade, supra; Matter of Sibrun, supra.
 
*3 To the extent the respondent is claiming ineffective assistance by one or more of his prior attorneys, he has not complied with any of the requirements under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Also, we are not persuaded that there was any per se ineffective assistance of counsel in this case, regarding pursuit of a U-Visa or otherwise (Resp. Br. at 5; I.J. at 1-3; Tr. at 1-21; Status Letter; Motion to Withdraw).
 
Finally, the respondent has proffered new evidence during the pendency of his appeal, to wit: e-mail correspondence between the respondent’s niece and Attorney Ruiz-Velasco. We deem this to constitute a motion to remand. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4). However, we are not persuaded that consideration of the new evidence would likely change the outcome in this case; therefore, the motion to remand will be denied. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SALIM MOHAMAD ANAS ZANTOUT
File: AXXX XX9 799 - Houston, TX
October 24, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pablo Rodriguez, Esquire
*1 This case was last before us on May 11, 2004, at which time we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and reconsider our previous decision dated November 17, 2003. The respondent has now filed an untimely and number-barred motion to reopen proceedings on July 14, 2014. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied
 
The respondent argues that we should reopen proceedings based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel that he received from representatives during his prior proceedings before the Immigration Judge and this Board in 2002 and 2003. In so arguing, the respondent also requests a remand to apply for adjustment of status.1 However, the respondent submitted a similar ineffective assistance of counsel claim before this Board in 2004, and he has provided no valid reason for us to reconsider his claim at this time. While the respondent argues that he is now eligible for adjustment of status, the respondent has not demonstrated that his current claimed eligibility for adjustment of status was, in any way, affected by his prior attorneys’ actions or inactions in 2002 and 2003. Further, the respondent also cannot prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as the ultimate relief he is seeking is discretionary in nature. See Gutierrez-Morales v. Homan, 461 F.3d 605 (5th Cir. 2006).
 
Lastly, the respondent’s motion does not demonstrate an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretion to reopen these proceedings under our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). While we sympathize with the respondent’s desire to remain in the United States, becoming potentially eligible for adjustment is common and is not generally a basis for granting an untimely motion to reopen. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 105 (BIA 2009). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent briefly mentions his intention to apply for withholding of removal, but he has submitted no application on behalf of this request.

2014 WL 6882880 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TINASHE ROLAN MANGWIRO
File: AXXX XX6 130 - Dallas, TX
October 22, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
J. David Swaim, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Zimbabwe, appeals the Immigration Judge’s October 4, 2012, decision denying his motion to reopen his removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent contends that he has submitted sufficient evidence to establish that his prior counsel, Ms. Cynthia K. Rigney, Esquire (formerly Cynthia K. Velarde), committed ineffective assistance by not reserving his right to appeal the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to continue proceedings on June 19, 2012 (Resp. Br. (11/05/12) at 2). He also argues that the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen violated his due process rights to a fundamentally fair proceeding (Resp. Br. at 4). Mr. J. David Swaim, Esquire, respondent’s current counsel before the Board, is the managing partner of the firm for which Ms. Rigney was employed when she was the respondent’s counsel of record before the Immigration Court in June 2012 (I.J. at 2).
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings as the respondent did not comply with the requirements to establish ineffective assistance by Ms. Rigney, and that the actions of Ms. Rigney did not constitute ineffective assistance (I.J. at 1-2). We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s October 4, 2012, decision. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994).
 
The respondent has not submitted a formal grievance with the appropriate state bar disciplinary authority regarding the performance of his prior counsel, and contends that such a formal grievance is not required to establish a claim of ineffective assistance (I.J. at 2; Resp. Br. at 10-11). See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (an ineffective assistance claim should reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation, and if not, why not). The filing of a state bar complaint, containing the notification of breaches of professional conduct, not only serves to deter meritless claims of ineffective representation but also highlights the standards which should be expected of attorneys who represent persons in immigration proceedings. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639-40; Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996), aff’d by Rivera v. INS, 122 F.3d 1062 (4th Cir. 1997). In Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647 (5th Cir. 2014), the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit upheld a Board decision that denied a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel where the alien did not establish he had met all requirements outlined in Matter of Lozada, supra. The Fifth Circuit explicitly considered precedents of the Ninth Circuit that found a claimant need not strictly comply with all the Matter of Lozada requirements to establish ineffective assistance. Id. The Fifth Circuit rejected the reasoning in these decisions and affirmed that compliance with the Matter of Lozada requirements was needed to demonstrate ineffective assistance. Id.
 
*2 We are not persuaded by the respondent’s explanations on appeal that a grievance to the Texas state bar is not required in the current case. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. The respondent has not established through objective evidence that he does not have the resources to pursue a disciplinary complaint against his former counsel (Resp. Br. at 12). In addition, the “““confidential” nature of disciplinary proceedings before the Texas state bar or the time it would take to resolve a claim are not valid excuses for failure to file the claim (Resp. Br. at 12). See Bouchikhi v. Holder, 676 F.3d 173, 180 (5th Cir. 2012).
 
Furthermore, the prior counsel’s tactical decision to waive appeal, after the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to continue to seek adjustment of status based on a third visa petition by the same spouse, which was contrary to the managing partner’s strategy for the case, does not indicate ineffective assistance (I.J. at 1-2; Motion to Reopen (08/13/12) at 2, item 10).1 See Morales v. Thaler, 714 F.3d 295, 305 (5th Cir. 2013), cert. denied 134 S. Ct. 393 (2013) (a counsel’s legitimate and reasonable tactical decisions, that may ultimately have an adverse effect on a client’s case, do not establish ineffective assistance). Moreover, the Immigration Judge, who the respondent and his prior counsel appeared before in June 2012, assessed the claims presented in the sworn statements and found that he was unable to conclude that Ms. Rigney disobeyed the respondent’s instructions (I.J. at 2; Resp. Br. at 8). See Matter of D-R-, 25 I&N Dec. 445, 454 (BIA 2011) (explaining that an Immigration Judge may make reasonable inferences from direct and circumstantial evidence in the record as a whole and is not required to accept a respondent’s account where other plausible views of the evidence are supported by the record).
 
The respondent contends that his former attorney’s actions were unreasonable as he had established good cause to warrant a grant of a continuance in order for his spouse to pursue a visa petition on his behalf (Resp. Br. at 7-9). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23. The spouse’s two previous visa petitions had been denied and there is no showing in the record to establish facts that would demonstrate that it is likely the third visa petition would be successful (I.J. at 1). See Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785, 790 (BIA 2009).
 
As the respondent has not met his burden to establish that Ms. Rigney provided ineffective assistance in June 2012, we need not address the respondent’s contentions that the denial of his motion to reopen has prejudiced his federal district court case against the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Service (“USCIS”) (Notice of Appeal, Attachment at 8-10). Moreover, the respondent’s contentions about the USCIS’s processing of visa petitions, and any actions of the federal court, are not within the purview of the Board to evaluate.
 
*3 Based on the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent states in his Motion to Reopen that “it is unclear whether or not Mrs. Rigney’s actions violate a specific ethical obligation under the Texas Disciplinary Rules of Professional Conduct.” See Motion to Reopen at 5, item 21.

2014 WL 6882885 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE GUADALUPE CABRERA-YURIAR
File: AXXX XX7 058 - San Diego, CA
October 21, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
J. Manuel Sanchez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kori LaPoint
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has filed an appeal from the Immigration Judge’s March 5, 2013, written decision denying his application voluntary departure as abandoned, cancelling a hearing scheduled for May 8, 2013, and ordering the respondent’s removal from the United States. The respondent has also filed, through his new counsel, a motion to remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel. In response, the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has moved for summary affirmance and expressed its opposition to the motion. We will dismiss the appeal and deny the motion.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. We find that the Immigration Judge properly determined that the respondent abandoned his opportunity to apply for relief from removal by not complying with the Immigration Judge’s November 14, 2012, order to file, no later than March 1, 2013, a written brief stating whether he was interested in seeking voluntary departure (I.J. at 1-2; Tr. at 15-16; Immigration Judge’s Record of Master Calendar Pre-Trial Appearance and Order, Form EOIR-55, dated November 14, 2012). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c) (providing that the Immigration Judge may set and extend time limits for the filing of applications for relief and that applications not filed within the time limit set by the Immigration Judge shall be deemed waived). The Board has long held that applications for benefits under the Immigration and Nationality Act are properly denied as abandoned when the alien fails to timely file them. See Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547 (BIA 1992); see also Matter of Jean, 17 I&N Dec. 100 (BIA 1979), modified, Matter of R-R-, supra.
 
The respondent initially argued, when he filed his appeal brief through the same counsel who represented him before the Immigration Judge, that the Immigration Judge erred in not entertaining his previously submitted request for voluntary departure and that there was ambiguity in the instructions pertaining to the deadline imposed, which both parties understood was not for a request of voluntary departure, but for additional applications for relief (Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 2-10). We disagree. As argued by the DHS in its brief in response to the respondent’s appeal brief, the Immigration Judge’s instructions were clear and the DHS understood that the March 1, 2013, deadline was set for the respondent to decide whether he wanted to request voluntary departure (DHS’s Reply Brief at 1-2; Tr. at 15-16). Moreover, we note that the respondent, who is now represented by new counsel, seems to have withdrawn this particular argument as he now asserts that his prior counsel “failed to submit the written request for [v]oluntary [d]eparture,” resulting in the Immigration Judge’s determination that he “had abandoned my request for” such form of relief (Respondent’s Affidavit accompanying the Motion to Reopen, at 3; see also Motion to Reopen at 2, 6, 9).
 
*2 Given the foregoing, we decline to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s application for voluntary departure as abandoned because of the respondent’s noncompliance with the March 1, 2013, deadline set by the Immigration Judge in his November 14, 2012, order.
 
We will deny the respondent’s motion to remand. We agree with the DHS that the respondent has not complied with the requirement set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), of indicating whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate State bar and, if not, why not (DHS’s Opposition to the Respondent’s Motion to Remand at 1-2). Additionally, while the respondent has submitted proof that he informed his prior counsel of the allegations he is now making against him, we note that the date on that particular letter indicates that the letter was sent, at the latest, only four days prior to the respondent’s filing of his motion to remand. Thus, we are not satisfied that this communication suffices as proof that the respondent’s prior counsel was given an adequate opportunity to respond to the allegations being made against him. Also, while the respondent argues in his motion that he was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s failure to advise him that he could file an application for cancellation of removal, the respondent has not submitted such an application with his motion nor sufficiently identified the evidence that would establish that he is prima facie eligible for this or any other form of relief from removal. As such, we are unable to find that the respondent was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s handling of this matter. For these reasons, we will deny the respondent’s motion.
 
Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 6882885 (BIA)
End of Document

2014 WL 6882887 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VILI ALOFA
File: AXXX XX1 180 - Honolulu, HI
October 21, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Burton D. Gould, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Heith M. Kaneshige
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Samoa, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 24, 2014. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable and found him ineligible for relief from removal. On appeal, the respondent challenges removability and says he was denied his right to counsel and a fair hearing. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Respondents in immigration proceedings have the statutory and regulatory “““privilege of being represented” by counsel of their choice at no expense to the Government. See sections 240(b)(4)(A), 292 of the Act; 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(4)(A), 1362. See also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.16(b), 1240.3, 1240.10(a), 1240.11(c)(1)(iii). In order to meaningfully effectuate the privilege, the Immigration Judge must grant a reasonable and realistic amount of time and provide a fair opportunity for a respondent to seek, speak with, and retain counsel. See Matter of C-B-, 25 I&N Dec. 888, 889-890 (BIA 2012). See also sections 239(a)(1)(E) and 239(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229(a)(1)(E); (b)(1).
 
In the present case, after advising the respondent of his right to counsel and providing him a list of attorneys who could help him, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent a 1-month continuance to find an attorney (Tr. at 6, 10). At that time, the respondent said he was unable to hire an attorney and wished to represent himself (Tr. at 13). The Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent waived his statutory and regulatory privilege of legal representation.1 Had the respondent requested a continuance or advised the Immigration Judge that he still wished to try to retain counsel, good cause for a continuance would have been shown. See Matter of C-B-, supra. However, the respondent did not do either. Consequently, we find that the statutory and regulatory privilege of legal representation was waived.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent also claims that his due process rights were violated because of allegedly faulty translation of the advisals of his rights at the removal hearing. An alien who faces removal is entitled to a full and fair removal hearing. The alien has a fundamental right to participate meaningfully in the removal proceedings by having the proceedings competently translated into a language he understands. See Matter of D-, 20 I&N Dec. 827 (BIA 1994); Matter of K-L-, 20 I&N Dec. 654 (BIA 1993); Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105 (BIA 1984); Matter of Exilus, 18 I&N Dec. 276 (BIA 1982). In order to establish that her due process rights were violated, the respondent must prove (1) that the interpreter performed incompetently, and (2) that he was prejudiced by the errors. See Matter of Santos, supra; Matter of Exilus, supra. Incompetent testimony is shown by (1) direct evidence of incorrectly translated words, (2) unresponsive answers by a witness, and (3) a witness’s expression of difficulty understanding what was said to her. See Perez-Lastor v. INS, 208 F.3d 773 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
In this case, the record reflects that the translator had to ask the Immigration Judge to repeat some of his statements (Tr. at 5-6). However, the record also reflects that the respondent said that he understood the translator (Tr. at 4), and after being given the advisals, said that he understood his rights (Tr. at 6). Consequently, we find no error.
 
The Notice to Appear alleges that, on July 19, 2013, the respondent was convicted of Theft Second Degree, in violation of Hawaii law, and sentenced to 1 year in jail. A record of this conviction was entered into the record of proceedings (Exh. 2). See section 240(c)(3)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(3)(B); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.41(a). Cf. United States v. Snellenberger, 548 F.3d 699, 701-02 (9th Cir. 2008) (en banc). In addition, the respondent admitted the conviction and sentence (Tr. at 15-16). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.41(d), 1240.10(c). Cf. Pagayon v. Holder, 675 F.3d 1182, 1189-1190 (9th Cir. 2011); Barragan-Lopez v. Mukasey, 508 F.3d 899, 905 (9th Cir. 2007). Based on this evidence, the Immigration Judge found the conviction proved (I.J. at 2). We do not find this factual finding to be clearly erroneous.
 
*3 The respondent was charged with being removable for having been convicted of an aggravated felony involving theft2 and a crime involving moral turpitude committed within 5 years of admission for which a sentence of 1 year or longer may be imposed (Exh. 1). The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged (I.J. at 2).
 
On appeal, the respondent also challenges removability. Specifically, he asserts that he has not been convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude. However, theft offenses have long been held to involve moral turpitude. See Matter of Khourn, 21 I&N Dec. 1041 (BIA 1997); Matter of Alarcon, 20 I&N Dec. 557 (BIA 1992); Matter of Esfandiary, 16 I & N Dec. 659 (BIA 1979); Matter of Chouinard, 11 I&N Dec. 839 (BIA 1966). Based on the respondent’s admissions and the evidence of the conviction in the record, we agree with the Immigration Judge that removability on both grounds has been proved by clear and convincing evidence. See section 240(c)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(3); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(a); Woodby v. INS, 385 U.S. 276, 286 (1966). Cf. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.15, 1240.10.
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of his conviction. He says he has filed a motion to vacate his criminal conviction before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).3 While the respondent is free to pursue the motion, it does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The motion does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
We also find the respondent ineligible for relief from removal. An alien who has been convicted of an aggravated felony, is statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). See section 240A(a)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a)(3); Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7 (BIA 1998). In addition, an alien who entered the United States as a lawful permanent resident and who has been convicted of an aggravated felony is statutorily ineligible for relief under section 212(h) of the Act. See sections 212(a), (h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1182(a), (h); Matter of Ayala, 22 I&N Dec. 398 (BIA 1998); Matter of Pineda-Castellanos, 21 I&N Dec. 1017 (BIA 1997); Matter of Yeung, 21 I&N Dec. 610 (BIA 1996, 1997).
 
*4 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In order for a waiver to be valid, an Immigration Judge must generally: (1) inquire specifically as to whether petitioner wishes to continue without a lawyer; and (2) receive a knowing and voluntary affirmative response. See Matter of C-B-, supra. Failure to obtain such a waiver is an effective denial of the right to counsel. See Matter of C-B-, supra.

2

The term “aggravated felony” includes a theft offense (including receipt of stolen property) or burglary offense for which the term of imprisonment is at least 1 year. See section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G). See also Gonzales v. Duenas-Alvarez, 549 U.S. 183 (2007).

3

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. ___, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

2014 WL 4966364 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUANA NIDIA RAMIREZ-DE MENDEZ A.K.A. JUANA NIDIA RAMIREZ PENA
File: AXXX XX1 699 - Boston, MA
September 25, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tony V. Blaize, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on May 25, 2005, when we dismissed as moot the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s May 3, 2004, decision. The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removable as charged after determining that the evidence of record overcame her claim to United States citizenship. See Immigration Judge’s Decision at 1, 5. The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal, however, because after unsuccessful attempts to contact the respondent by mail, we deemed the appeal to have been abandoned. On June 2, 2014, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen. The respondent seeks reopening to apply for adjustment of status as the parent of a United States citizen. The motion is untimely by nearly 9 years and will be denied. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The respondent does not claim, and it does not appear, that any exception to the filing deadline imposed on motions to reopen applies to her case. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 105 (BIA 2009) (emphasizing that untimely motions to reopen to apply for adjustment of status generally do not fall within any of the statutory or regulatory exceptions for motions to reopen and are generally denied); 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(c)(3). Further, we are not persuaded to exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen the proceedings. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (discussing the Board’s limited authority to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte in exceptional situations).
 
Insofar as the respondent now challenges the Immigration Judge’s decision, including the finding with regard to her alleged United States citizenship, we emphasize that such issues are deemed waived as the respondent was found to have abandoned her appeal. See generally Matter of Edwards, 20 I&N Dec. 191, 196, n.4 (BIA 1990) (noting that issues not addressed on appeal are deemed waived on appeal).
 
In her motion, the respondent alleges ineffective assistance of counsel against Lawrence Perlmutter, the attorney who represented her before the Immigration Judge.1 See generally Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (discussing ineffective assistance of counsel claims before the Board), affirmed, Lozada v. I.N.S., 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). She also claims that Lawrence Perlmutter represented her on appeal before the Board even though the Notice of Appeal was filed “pro se.”
 
*2 The respondent has offered evidence that in 2010, Mr. Perlmutter was charged in a Federal money-laundering case after being accused of conspiring to conceal illegal drug trafficking proceeds beginning in 2006. The respondent has not shown, however, that such activity was in any way related to Lawrence Perlmutter’s handling of her case. Further, although the respondent claims that her former attorney did not adequately prepare for her hearing because he did not secure witnesses and documentary evidence regarding her alleged United States citizenship, she has not offered any evidence with this motion in support of her citizenship claim. Further, with regard to her appeal, the respondent has not shown that the Board’s unsuccessful attempts to contact her with regard to her appeal were related to her former attorney’s handling of the appeal. Indeed, she has offered no explanation addressing why the Board’s attempts to contact her at her last address of record were unsuccessful. We are not persuaded, therefore, that Mr. Perlmutter’s allegedly deficient performance may have altered the outcome of her case. See Zeru v. Gonzales, 503 F.3d 59, 72 (1st Cir. 2007) (stating that to succeed on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, petitioners must show a reasonable probability of prejudice resulting from their former representation).
 
The respondent has also not shown that she exercised due diligence in bringing her ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board. See Bead v. Holder, 703 F.3d 591, 594 (1st Cir. 2013) (finding that the Board’s determination that an alien who sought reopening based on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim had not diligently pursued his rights, as required for equitable tolling doctrine, was not abuse of discretion); Matter of Lozada, supra, 639. Although the respondent claims that she was unaware of the Board’s decision until she contacted her current attorney, she has not set forth any arguments or evidence of any attempt to contact her former attorney or this Board with regard to her appeal at any time following the filing of the Notice of Appeal on June 11, 2004.
 
In sum, we are not persuaded that equitable tolling or reopening sua sponte based on the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim is warranted in this case. We acknowledge that the respondent desires to remain in the United States due to the status of her health. We emphasize, however, that we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of the time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999). The respondent’s case does not present any such exceptional situation. See Matter of J-J-, supra. Accordingly, the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen will be denied. The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is also denied.
 
*3 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Mr. Perlmutter has been suspended from the practice of law before the Board since November 22, 2011.
