2014 WL 4966373 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANTHONY OLIVER CAMPBELL
File: AXXX XX6 016 - York, PA
September 22, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaime Jasso, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on April 8, 2014, when we denied the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen. On July 24, 2014, the respondent filed another motion to reopen. The respondent subsequently submitted additional documents. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and number-barred. Section 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent, however, argues that his proceedings should be reopened based on the ineffective assistance by his former counsels who represented him before the Immigration Judge, on appeal, and in his prior motion to reopen.1
 
At his hearing before the Immigration Judge, the respondent through counsel indicated that he intended to seek adjustment of status based on a marriage to a United States citizen and that his fiancée was in the process of obtaining a divorce from a previous spouse at the time. The respondent therefore requested a continuance so that the fiancée’s divorce would be finalized, they would marry, and she would file a Form I-130 visa petition for him. On May 9, 2013, the Immigration Judge denied the continuance and ordered the respondent removed, after examining factors discussed in Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009). Among the factors cited by the Immigration Judge was that the respondent’s application for adjustment of status would not merit a favorable exercise of discretion (I.J. at 3). Although the Immigration Judge acknowledged positive factors presented by the respondent, including the fact that his mother and fiancée are United States citizens, the Immigration Judge nevertheless found that they were outweighed by negative factors including his criminal record (I.J. at 3-5). Since the respondent did not establish that his application for adjustment of status would merit a favorable exercise of discretion, the Immigration Judge denied his motion for a continuance (I.J. at 5).
 
On appeal, the respondent through counsel challenged the denial of continuance under Matter of Hashmi, supra, and also presented evidence that the respondent and his fiancée were married and his spouse filed a visa petition for him on July 10, 2013. On September 12, 2013, the Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying further continuance. The Board also declined to remand the proceedings to the Immigration Judge based on the pending visa petition, because the respondent did not show that the pendency of the visa petition would likely change the result of his case.
 
*2 On March 31, 2014, the respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen, showing that the visa petition was approved by the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services on February 19, 2014, and requesting that the Board sua sponte reopen his proceedings to allow him to apply for adjustment of status. On April 8, 2014, as noted above, the Board denied that motion.
 
The respondent argues in the current motion that his former counsels provided ineffective assistance because they did not advise him to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1). The respondent argues that he was statutorily eligible for cancellation of removal because he in fact first came to the United States in 2001 and his mother was a United States citizen. The respondent explicitly admits that he is not arguing that he would have been able to establish “““exceptional and extremely unusual hardship” for his mother upon his removal, or that his application for cancellation of removal would have been granted (Motion, at 4-5, 23-25). However, he suggests that, if he had applied for cancellation of removal, the Immigration Judge would have granted him further continuance to allow him to prepare and submit an application for cancellation of removal, and this delay would have allowed him more time for his fiancée to finalize her divorce, marry the respondent, and file a visa petition for him (id.). Therefore, the respondent argues that he was prejudiced by the former counsels’ failure to advise him to apply for cancellation of removal.
 
The respondent has not shown that, if he had applied for cancellation of removal and obtained continuance, there was “reasonable likelihood that the result would have been different.” Fadiga v. U.S. Attorney General, 488 F.3d 142, 158 (3d Cir. 2007). As noted above, the respondent does not argue that there was reasonable likelihood that his application for cancellation of removal would have been granted. Rather, his argument is that his application for cancellation of removal would have afforded him a delay in his proceedings, so that his application for adjustment of status would have been decided on the merits by the Immigration Judge (Motion, at 6). However, as noted above the Immigration Judge addressed the merits of his application for adjustment of status, and found that he did not show that this application would be granted in the exercise of discretion. Also as noted above, the Board agreed with this decision on appeal. Under the circumstances of this case, the respondent has not shown “reasonable likelihood” that the outcome of his application for adjustment of status would have been different if this application were decided on the merits. Therefore, the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by the former counsels’ failure to advise him to apply for cancellation of removal for the purpose of delaying his proceedings.
 
*3 The respondent next argues that the former counsels provided ineffective assistance by failing to argue, in his prior motion to reopen,2 that his motion should not be governed by the standard set forth in Matter of Hashmi, supra, and rather should be governed by the standard set forth in Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413 (BIA 1996), and Matter of Pena-Diaz, 20 I&N Dec. 841 (BIA 1994). The respondent argues that, after the visa petition filed by his spouse for him was approved in February 2014, his case was no longer governed by Matter of Hashmi, supra, which is premised upon a pending visa petition rather than an approved visa petition. Instead, the respondent argues that the applicable standards are those set forth in Matter of L-O-G-, supra, and Matter of Pena-Diaz, supra, under which he is not required to conclusively show that he should be granted relief, but only required to show reasonable likelihood that relief would be granted in the exercise of discretion (Motion, at 26-30). The respondent also argues that the Board erred in the prior decision by prejudging the likelihood of the merits of his case rather than allowing the matter to be decided by the Immigration Judge (id.).
 
However, the Board’s decision in Matter of Pena-Diaz, supra, predated section 240(c)(6)-(7) of the Act and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2, which set forth the legal standards applicable to motions in this case. Similarly, Matter of L-O-G-, supra, did not address the implication of timeliness or untimeliness of a motion to reopen. As noted above and in the Board’s prior decision, the respondent’s prior motion to reopen was untimely. Furthermore, his prior motion did not fall within any exception to the motion to reopen time limitations enumerated in section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Act and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3). The former counsels correctly requested that the Board sua sponte reopen his proceedings under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a) in the exercise of discretion. Therefore, the former counsels did not provide ineffective assistance, in submitting the prior motion to reopen, by failing to identify the correct legal standard. In addition, the issue of whether the respondent showed that he would merit adjustment of status as a matter of discretion was previously addressed by the Immigration Judge and the Board, as noted above.
 
Based on the above, the respondent has not shown that there was “reasonable likelihood” that the outcome of his proceedings would have been different but for the claimed ineffective assistance by his former counsels. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
*4 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claims are made against both attorneys on a collective basis, thus we will address the respondent’s claims in the same manner.

2

The respondent also argues in one part of his motion that the former counsels provided ineffective assistance by failing to make this argument “on appeal” (Motion, at 2). However, at the time of the respondent’s appeal, the visa petition filed on behalf of the respondent was still pending and had not been approved. In the detailed discussion of this issue, the respondent argues that the former counsels should have made this argument in his motion to reopen (Motion, at 26-30).

2014 WL 4966519 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ENCARNACION COJ-GARCIA
File: AXXX XX8 145 - Baltimore, MD
September 17, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Emmanuel A. Fishelman, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Termination
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated December 14, 2012, denying her motion to withdraw her admission of alienage and admission of removability.1 The Department of Homeland Security has not filed a brief in opposition to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision in this case. In denying the motion, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent, through counsel, admitted and conceded she was from Guatemala (I.J. at 2).2 The Immigration Judge also found that the respondent conceded she entered the United States without inspection in her asylum application (I.J. at 2).
 
The respondent contends that she had a valid “ineffective assistance of counsel” claim, and that she did not need to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) in a pretrial motion (Resp. Br. at 11-15). The respondent cites to Figeroa v. INS, 886 F.2d 76, 77-79 (4th Cir. 1989) in support of her argument. In citing to Figeroa, she asserts that like the respondent in Figeroa, she did not speak English, was not educated, and was unfamiliar with the procedures by which one could file a bar complaint (Resp. Br. at 13). We reject this argument inasmuch as the respondent was represented by counsel before the Immigration Judge who could have complied with the Lozada requirements. We also reject the respondent’s argument that the lawyer was “properly” focused on the respondent’s deportation case, and not on prosecuting a civil action or bar complaint against the respondent’s former attorney (Resp. Br. at 13-14).
 
We disagree with the respondent’s contention that concessions in a motion to change venue without the respondent’s consent should not be used (Resp. Br. at 17-18). The respondent has not cited to legal authority for her argument. To the degree that the respondent asserts that her attorney made the concession of removability in the motion to change venue without her consent, this argument is without merit inasmuch as the respondent has not complied with the Lozada requirements as described above.
 
*2 We disagree with the respondent’s contention that information in the respondent’s application of relief should not be used as a concession of alienage (Resp. Br. at 18). Although the respondent cited to 8 C.F.R. § 1240.11(e) for this proposition, the relevant section proscribes using an application to constitute a concession of alienage only in a case in which the alien does not admit her alienage. In this case, the respondent admitted her alienage in her motion to change venue.
 
The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge did not consider whether the DHS violated 8 C.F.R. § 287.3. However, we note that the applicable DHS regulations listed under 8 C.F.R. § 287 expressly provide that they “do not, are not intended to, shall not be construed to, and may not be relied upon to create any rights, substantive or procedural, enforceable at law by any party in any matter, civil or criminal.” 8 C.F.R. § 287.12; see also Navarro-Chalan v. Ashcroft, 359 F.3d 19, 23 (1st Cir. 2004) (citing 8 C.F.R. § 287.11 (1995) and the current version at 8 C.F.R. § 287.12 to explain that even if DHS regulations were applicable in the alien’s matter and were in fact violated, the regulations by its own express terms do not confer any individual rights that may be enforced in any matter). Thus, DHS regulations are not enforceable by the respondents to seek termination of proceedings or to suppress evidence. Further, even when a regulatory violation occurs, termination of proceedings or suppression of evidence is not the proper remedy. See Arias v. Rogers, 676 F.2d 1139, 1143 (7th Cir. 1982) (“To give a person who has been illegally arrested total immunity from either criminal punishment or deportation is an excessive sanction for the illegal arrest.”); cf. United States v. Guzman-Padilla, 573 F.3d 865, 890 (9th Cir. 2009) (“the government’s violation of its own rules does not provide a basis for the suppression of evidence in a criminal action”); United States v. Ani, 138 F.3d 390, 392 (9th Cir. 1998) (“Absent a constitutional violation or a congressionally created remedy, violation of an agency regulation does not require suppression of the evidence.”).
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Although the Immigration Judge also denied the respondent’s husband’s motion to withdraw his admission of alienage and admission of removability, the respondent’s husband did not appeal these findings.

2

Although the Immigration Judge did not explicitly state in the order where the admission and concession were made, they were made in the respondent’s “motion to change venue” filed in March 2011.

IN RE: MAXIMO FERMIN RODRIGUEZ
File: AXXX XX4 405 - Lumpkin, GA
September 10, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Danielle M. Conley, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Remand; continuance
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s March 13, 2014, decision, denying his request for a continuance to await the vacation of his criminal conviction which barred him from eligibility for adjustment of status. While the appeal was pending, the respondent filed a motion to remand, which the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not opposed. The motion will be granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
In support of his motion to remand, the respondent submitted a certified copy of an order from the Criminal Court, City of New York, Borough of the Bronx, dated May 13, 2014, vacating the respondent’s November 19, 2007, conviction for possession of marijuana pursuant to section 440.10(1)(h) of the New York Criminal Procedure Law (Motion to Remand, tab A). The respondent also submitted the Motion to Vacate, which was based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. In light of this evidence, and the lack of opposition from the DHS, the record will be remanded to allow the respondent to pursue his application for adjustment of status. See Matter of Rodriguez-Ruiz, 22 I&N Dec. 1378 (BIA 2000) (a conviction that has been vacated pursuant to section 440 of the New York Criminal Procedure Law does not constitute a conviction for immigration purposes within the meaning of section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48)(A)).1
 
ORDER: The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Roger Pauley

2014 WL 4966494 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUADALUPE LORENZO RAMOS A.K.A. LORENZO RAMOS
File: AXXX XX5 877 - Houston, TX
September 10, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Felipe Millan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico who was ordered removed from the United States on October 7, 2005, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated April 18, 2014, denying his motion to reopen, which he filed on April 8, 2014. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.1
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely (I.J. at 2). See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent’s request for equitable tolling, a request which was not presented before the Immigration Judge, is not properly before this Board for purposes of appellate review. See Matter of Jimenez, 21 I&N Dec. 567, 570 n.2 (BIA 1996). Moreover, even if such a request was properly before this Board, equitable tolling on the basis of the respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance of counsel would not be warranted because he has not demonstrated due diligence. See Irwin v. Dep’t of Veterans Affairs, 498 U.S. 89, 96 (1990); Rashidi v. Am. President Lines, 96 F.3d 124, 128 (5th Cir. 1996); Cavazos v. Gonzales, 181 F.App’x. 453, 460 (5th Cir. 2006). Accordingly, as the respondent’s arguments on appeal do not persuade this Board to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

On appeal, the respondent has presented numerous documents. The Board does not consider evidence first provided on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv); Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984). Moreover, a request for a remand for the Immigration Judge to consider the evidence is untimely and number-barred. See Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SEDRICK KNIGHT
File: AXXX XX0 800 - New York, NY
August 29, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brian Figeroux, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider the Board’s decision of February 27, 2014, in which the Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s first untimely motion to reopen. The respondent has also submitted additional evidence, and to that end this motion is in the style of the respondent’s second, untimely motion to reopen since the respondent’s final administrative decision of January 23, 2001. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent does not allege any specific errors of fact or law in the Board’s prior decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); see also Matter of 0-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (noting that a motion to reconsider is not a vehicle by which a party can request reopening by generally alleging error in the prior decision). The request for reconsideration is denied.
 
Insofar as the respondent reiterates his request for reopening, we are not persuaded that equitable tolling of the time and number limits on motions to reopen is warranted in this case. The time and number limits on motions may be tolled due to ineffective assistance of counsel, where competent counsel would have acted otherwise and that ineffective assistance prejudiced the alien. Rabiu v. INS, 41 F.3d 879, 882B83 (2d Cir. 1994). In addition, the alien must show due diligence in attempting to correct the error during “both the period of time before the ineffective assistance of counsel was or should have been discovered and the period from that point until the motion to reopen is filed.” Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 131-32 (2d Cir. 2008). Finally, the alien must substantially comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent alleges ineffective assistance surrounding the filing of a visa petition after he married his United States citizen spouse on May 2, 2001, and subsequent advice regarding when to reopen his removal proceedings (Motion at 2, 4). The alleged ineffective assistance did not prejudice the respondent, as even if an I-130 petition had been filed and approved the day of his marriage any motion to reopen would still have been filed outside the 90 day period for motions to reopen. As we conclude no prejudice has been shown, we do not address the respondent’s assertion that he has established due diligence in attempting to discover and correct the alleged errors by prior counsel.
 
*2 While the Board does have the discretionary authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte, that authority is reserved for rare, “exceptional” situations not present here. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). This motion to reopen will be denied as untimely and number-barred.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2014 WL 4966425 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DENG YI LEI A.K.A. DENGYI LEI
File: AXXX XX7 940 - San Francisco, CA
August 27, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
CERTIFICATION1
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lien Uy, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Juliette C. Gonsalves
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen his removal proceedings to allow him to apply to the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”) for a hardship waiver under section 216(c)(4)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1186a(c)(4)(A) [extreme hardship waiver]. In our March 18, 2014, decision we dismissed his appeal from the Immigration Judge’s March 29, 2012, decision which found him removable and determined upon review that there was no good reason to disturb the USCIS’s denial of his application for a hardship waiver under section 216(c)(4)(B) of the Act [good faith marriage waiver]. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The Board does not have any authority to reopen proceedings to allow the respondent to apply for a hardship waiver under section 216(c)(4)(A) of the Act. We issued a final administrative decision on March 18, 2014. The regulation at 8 C.F.R. § 1216.5(a)(2) provides, in pertinent part, that a conditional resident who is in removal proceedings may apply for the [hardship] waiver only until such time as there is a final [administrative] removal order. It is well established that regulations promulgated by the Attorney General are binding on the Board and Immigration Judges. Matter of Akram, 25 I&N Dec. 874, 880 (BIA 2012). There is thus no basis for us to grant reopening.
 
The respondent, however, alleges ineffective assistance of (present) counsel. In Matter of Compean, Bangaly, & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), the Attorney General directed the Board to continue to apply the previously established standards for reviewing motions to reopen based on claims of ineffective assistance of counsel pending the outcome of a rulemaking process.
 
In Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988), we set forth the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit stated in Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 597 (9th Cir. 2004) that the court presumes, as a general rule, that the Board does not abuse its discretion when it obligates aliens to satisfy the literal requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*2 The respondent does not show substantial compliance with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent does not present his sworn affidavit or declaration made under penalty of perjury, and thus does not meet the first requirement in Matter of Lozada, supra. Compliance with the second requirement is excused because counsel obviously knows about the respondent’s allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel, as they are made against him.
 
The respondent does not meet the third requirement in that he did not file a complaint against counsel with the State Bar of California, and does not offer a satisfactory explanation of why no complaint was made. Counsel states in her June 17, 2014, declaration (Motion Exh. B) that she did not know that even while the renewal of the good faith marriage waiver was before the Immigration Judge, she could have filed on the respondent’s behalf another Petition to Remove Conditions on Residence (Form I-751) with the USCIS based on extreme hardship. While counsel states that she takes responsibility for the oversight, she does not indicate that she self-reported her alleged ineffective representation to the State Bar of California. See generally California Business and Professions Code section 6068(o) (duties of attorney).
 
When an alien retains prior counsel to pursue an argument of inadequacy of that same attorney, the reporting requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, are likely more meaningful. It is not for the attorney to decide the issues regarding her own prior conduct. The self-reporting requirement does not equate to an obvious need for discipline. The self-reporting requirement lets a state bar decide and puts the state bar on notice if there are further problems. We conclude that the respondent does not show substantial compliance with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra, to allege ineffective assistance of (present) counsel. Cf Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 825 (9th Cir. 2003) (the failure to file a bar complaint is not fatal to the alien’s ineffective assistance claim where former counsel submitted a letter of self-report to the state bar; this self-reporting shows that the alien’s former counsel regarded his delinquency as a serious breach of services promised to his client).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.2
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2

Any request for the exercise of prosecutorial discretion must be addressed to the DHS.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANTONY KAHUMBU
File: AXXX XX4 271 - Dallas, TX
August 27, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Karen H. Pennington, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)] - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
 
Sec. 237(a)(3)(D), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(3)(D)] - False claim of United States citizenship
 
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Kenya, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 17, 2013, sustaining the charges of removability and denying his application for adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge’s decision will be affirmed. On April 23, 2013, the respondent, through his former attorney, admitted all factual allegations contained in the Notice to Appear and conceded both charges of removability (Tr. at 3). Based on the respondent’s concession that he was removable pursuant to section 237(a)(3)(D) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(3)(D), the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for adjustment of status (I.J. at 2). On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in not permitting him to withdraw his concession of removability under section 237(a)(3)(D) of the Act.
 
Absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal admission made during a proceeding by an attorney acting in his professional capacity binds his client as a judicial admission. See Matter of Velazquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). In the instant case, the respondent’s admissions and concessions were formal and distinct and were made by the respondent’s former counsel in his professional capacity because they were made at a time when he was the respondent’s official attorney of record. Although the respondent asserts that he was not advised of the consequences of the concession of removability made on his behalf by his former attorney, he has not proffered any evidence to show that the concession was the result of unreasonable professional judgment or was so unfair that it has produced an unjust result.
 
*2 We are not persuaded by the respondent’s assertion that he should not be bound by the concession of his former attorney because it was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Br. at 4-7). In Mai v. Gonzalez, 473 F.3d 162, 473 F.3d 162 (5th Cir. 2006), the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit concluded that an attorney’s concession of removability based on a false claim of United States citizenship that cut off all available avenues of relief to the alien constituted ineffective assistance of counsel for purposes of a motion to reopen based on Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 67 (BIA 1988). However, unlike the alien in Mai, the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, and has not provided a persuasive reason for not doing so. Although the record reflects that the respondent’s former attorney was aware of the respondent’s allegations (Tr. at 10), the respondent has not provided an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with his former attorney or indicated whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding the allegations and we are not persuaded that former counsel’s awareness of the allegations obviates the need for such evidence (Respondent’s Br. at 6). See, e.g., Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497-99 (5th Cir. 2006) (upholding the need to comply with the Lozada requirements). As such, the respondent has not demonstrated that his former attorney provided ineffective assistance in conceding removability under section 237(a)(3)(D) of the Act. Because the respondent has not established that the concession was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel or other egregious circumstances, the Immigration Judge properly denied his request to withdraw his concession of removability under section 237(a)(3)(D) of the Act.
 
In sustaining the charge of removability under section 237(a)(3)(D) of the Act and denying the respondent’s application for adjustment of status, the Immigration Judge relied solely on the respondent’s concession of removability (I.J. at 2). Consequently we do not reach the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge erred in admitting the Form I-9, Employment Eligibility Verification submitted by the DHS (Respondent’s Br. at 7-11; Exh. 2). But see Downs v. Holder, 2014 WL. 339 77 88 (8th Cir. July 14, 2014).
 
In light of the foregoing, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed. The following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
*3 FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: MARIO ANICETO-EUGENIO
File: AXXX XX3 671 - Bloomington, MN
August 27, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Patrick Uke, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alexandra R. Tinkham
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen and national of Mexico, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 14, 2013. The Immigration Judge denied a request for a continuance and granted voluntary departure with an alternate order of removal from the United States. Neither party appealed the grant of voluntary departure. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 10Q3.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The record demonstrates that the Immigration Judge continued this case at the hearing on December 29, 2011, at which time the respondent was represented by an attorney, and at the hearings on January 17, 2012, January 30, 2012, and May 22, 2012, in which the respondent was acting pro se. At the hearing on May 22, 2012, the Immigration Judge gave the respondent another opportunity to complete the Form EOIR-42B he initially submitted to apply for cancellation of removal and clearly warned him that at the next hearing, if the application and any attachments were not ready, the application would be deemed abandoned or dismissed, pretermitted or denied. The Immigration Judge provided the respondent with another copy of the application for cancellation of removal (Tr. at 32).
 
At the next hearing on February 14, 2013, the respondent appeared with the above noted counsel, who requested more time to help the respondent complete the cancellation of removal form and also indicated that the respondent had informed him that he did not have a Notice to Appear (Tr. at 38-41). The respondent’s counsel indicated that the respondent initially met with the firm beginning the October prior to this hearing and that the respondent had claimed that he did not have a Notice to Appear (Tr. at 38-41). It was clarified at the hearing that the respondent acknowledged receipt of the Notice to Appear at the hearing on December 29, 2011 (Tr. at 41-42). The Immigration Judge deemed the submitted, incomplete application for cancellation of removal abandoned and also pretermitted and denied that application as incomplete. She further determined that any new application was deemed abandoned and dismissed (I.J. at 1-2; Tr. at 43, 45).
 
The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge abused her discretion and denied him due process by the Immigration Judge by pretermitting, denying, dismissing and deeming his application for cancellation of removal abandoned. Further the respondent argues that he was denied the opportunity to have meaningful representation.
 
There is no indication that the respondent was denied the opportunity to obtain representation. The record indicates that he was represented at his first and last hearings. He was also given several continuances which provided him sufficient opportunity to obtain representation. It is unclear what the respondent is claiming when he argues that he was denied the opportunity to have “meaningful representation.” To the extent that the respondent may have attempted to raise a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, we find that he has failed to comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). See 8 C.F.R. § 1208.4(a)(5)(ii).
 
*2 The Board does not find that the Immigration Judge abused her discretion in denying another request for a continuance at the hearing on February 14, 2013. She clearly notified the respondent at the prior hearing that she was providing him with one final opportunity to complete the Form EOIR-42B, to apply for cancellation of removal, and if, at the next hearing, the application and attachments were not ready, the application would be deemed abandoned or dismissed, pretermitted or denied (Tr. at 32). The decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge if good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; see also Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1996); Matter of Silva-Rodriguez, 20 I&N Dec. 448 (BIA 1992); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). The Immigration Judge provided several prior continuances for the respondent to complete his application for cancellation of removal and she did not abuse her discretion in denying yet another request for a continuance to complete such an application.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
*3 WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
Edward R. Grant

2014 WL 4407738 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MIGUEL TALLEDO-GARCIA
File: AXXX XX9 652 - Las Vegas, NV
August 21, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Astrid Guzman-Ugarte, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s February 26, 2013, decision denying his second motion to reopen. We will dismiss the appeal.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Peru. He was personally served with a Notice to Appear for removal proceedings, but the Notice did not specify the time and date of his hearing. On June 13, 2011, after the respondent was released from detention, a hearing notice was mailed to the address he provided upon his release, notifying him that he was scheduled for a removal hearing on February 27, 2012. That hearing notice was returned undelivered by the postal service due to “insufficient address.”
 
The respondent did not appear at his removal hearing, and the Immigration Judge entered an in absentia order of removal. The respondent filed a timely motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel but failing to comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The Immigration Judge denied that motion on July 31, 2012. The respondent did not learn the motion was denied until October 2012, after the appeal period had expired. The respondent filed a second motion to reopen, alleging ineffective assistance of both of his prior counsel. The Immigration Judge denied the motion as number-barred. That denial is the subject of this appeal.
 
An alien who is ordered removed in absentia can file only one motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The Immigration Judge, therefore, properly denied the respondent’s second motion. This holding resolves the issue presented in this appeal.
 
Nonetheless, given the respondent’s claim of double receipt of ineffective assistance of counsel and the fact that the respondent has amply addressed the issues raised in his first motion to reopen, we will review by certification the denial of the respondent’s first motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(c).
 
*2 First, lack of receipt of the hearing notice is not a basis for reopening and rescinding the in absentia order. The respondent had actual notice of the hearing because, he claims, his attorney’s assistant called the Immigration Court and inquired about the date and time of the hearing. The record shows, however, that the respondent did not receive the Notice of Hearing that was mailed to him. It was returned as undeliverable due to “““insufficient address.” Section 239(a)(2) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(2), allows service of the Notice of Hearing by regular mail. An alien is charged with receiving proper notice if the notice reaches the correct address but does not reach the alien. Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181, 189 (BIA 2001).
 
In this case, the record suggests the respondent bears responsibility for the Notice of Hearing being undeliverable. The Form I-830 indicates that the address reported by the respondent upon his release from detention did not include his apartment number (Exh. 2). A subsequent change of address form the respondent submitted indicates he moved from apartment 131 to apartment 173 at the address to which the Notice of Hearing was mailed. It, thus, appears likely that the respondent’s apparent failure to provide his apartment number is the reason the hearing notice was undeliverable due to “insufficient address.” Because the Notice of Hearing was properly mailed to the respondent’s address on file, the respondent’s failure to receive it does not provide an adequate basis for reopening and rescinding the in absentia removal order.
 
Second, the respondent did not sufficiently establish ineffective assistance of counsel. To warrant reopening based on the ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien should (1) submit an affidavit describing the alleged misconduct and detailing the agreement for representation, (2) provide former counsel with an opportunity to respond to the allegations, (3) indicate whether a bar complaint has been filed, and (4) demonstrate that he was prejudiced by counsel’s deficient performance. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639-40. Neither of the respondent’s motions to reopen satisfied the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada
 
The failure to fully comply with the Lozada requirements will not bar consideration of the motion where ineffective assistance of counsel is clear from the record. Granados-Oseguera v. Gonzales, 464 F.3d 993, 998 (9th Cir. 2006); see also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that ineffectiveness was clear on the face of the record where counsel failed to file application for relief). In this case, however, ineffective assistance of counsel is not apparent from the record.
 
*3 The respondent claims that he did not attend his removal hearing because he was told his counsel would attend and his presence was not needed. As the Immigration Judge observed, no counsel entered an appearance in the proceedings, and the respondent did not sufficiently establish that he had obtained counsel. The declaration submitted with his second motion to reopen asserts that he signed a contract with counsel for representation before the Immigration Court, but the respondent has not provided a copy of any such agreement nor asserted that he is unable to provide a copy (second motion to reopen at Tab HI). He does not describe any particular details of the agreement, other than that it was for representation before the Immigration Court. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639 (claim of ineffective assistance of counsel should include “a statement that sets forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken”).
 
The respondent provided two receipts as evidence of his retention of counsel for the proceedings (second motion to reopen at Tab I). The receipts state that they are for attorney’s fees, but do not identify the payee. Thus, they are not strong evidence that the respondent retained counsel as he claims. There is also a discrepancy between the receipts - one for $850 dated February 16, 2012, and one for $150 dated February 25, 2012 - and the respondent’s assertion in his bar complaint that he paid $1,000 on the day he signed the contract with counsel (see second motion to reopen at Tab V).
 
For these reasons, the respondent did not meet his burden of showing he had an agreement with counsel to represent him at the February 27, 2012, removal hearing.
 
Additionally, the respondent did not give his first attorney an opportunity to respond to his claim of ineffective assistance as required by Matter of Lozada. Because it is not apparent from the record that the first attorney was actually retained by the respondent, providing counsel an opportunity to respond to the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance is an important part of his burden of showing that reopening is warranted. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639 (“the potential for abuse is apparent where no mechanism exists for allowing former counsel, whose integrity or competence is being impugned, to present his version of events if he so chooses, thereby discouraging baseless allegations”); accord Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 598-99 (9th Cir. 2003) (describing importance of Lozada’s requirement that former counsel be notified of allegations and given opportunity to respond). The respondent’s assertion that he tried to confront the attorney at his office but was instead “received by the attorney’s assistant” did not provide the attorney a meaningful opportunity to respond to the respondent’s claims (respondent’s brief at unnumbered page 7).
 
*4 In these two ways, the respondent did not satisfy his burden under Matter of Lozada to show deficient performance by counsel.1 Having not shown ineffective assistance of counsel or a deficiency in the Notice of Hearing, the respondent has not shown error in the denial of his first motion to reopen.
 
For these reasons, we will dismiss the appeal.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Given the lack of showing of deficiency in the performance of the respondent’s first counsel, no prejudice results from any deficient performance by the respondent’s second counsel, whose firm concedes they did not provide the respondent notice of the denial of his first motion to reopen.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAVIER MEJIA A.K.A. JAVIER MAJIA
File: AXXX XX7 194 - Houston, TX
August 20, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Olivieri Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James E. Manning
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from the May 22, 2014, decision of the Immigration Judge denying his motion to reopen the proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including determinations of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether or not the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The basic facts of this case are not in dispute. The respondent adjusted his status to that of a lawful permanent resident on September 29, 1989, and on September 22, 2004, he was convicted in Texas of possession of cocaine. A Notice to Appear was issued the next day and, on December 12, 2004, he was ordered removed under sections 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) (aggravated felony) and 237(a)(2)(B)(i) (controlled substance violation) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii) and (B)(i). The respondent appealed this decision to the Board, but withdrew his appeal on February 3, 2005, and he departed the United States.
 
In 2006, the Supreme Court issued a decision holding that convictions such as the respondent’s were not aggravated felonies. See Lopez v. Gonzalez, 549 U.S. 47 (2006) (holding that a first-time conviction for simple possession of drugs that is neither an illicit trafficking offense nor a federal felony does not constitute an aggravated felony for immigration purposes). On April 11, 2011, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, which was denied on May 22, 2014. The Immigration Judge found that the motion was untimely and declined to reopen the proceedings sua sponte (I.J. at 2-3). The Immigration Judge further observed that, although the respondent appears to be claiming ineffective assistance of counsel, this is not grounds for equitable tolling or reopening the proceedings at this late date. We agree.
 
The respondent’s request for reopening was untimely under the general rule requiring aliens to file a motion to reopen within 90 days of the entry of a final administrative order of removal. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent also failed to demonstrate that any of the statutory or regulatory exceptions to the time limitation on motions to reopen apply to his case. See sections 240(c)(7)(C)(ii)-(iv) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(4)(i)-(iv). The motion was properly denied as untimely.
 
*2 Further, pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), the respondent’s departure from the United States while under a final order of removal barred consideration of the motion before the Immigration Judge sua sponte. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (“A motion to reopen or to reconsider shall not be made by or on behalf of a person who is the subject of removal ... proceedings subsequent to his or her departure from the United States.”); Ovalles v. Holder, 577 F.3d 288, 296 (5th Cir. 2009) (holding that the Board acted reasonably in concluding that we lacked sua sponte regulatory authority to reopen the proceedings based on a change in intervening law, pursuant to our comparable departure bar provision at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d)); Navarro-Miranda v. Ashcroft, 330 F.3d 672, 676 (5th Cir. 2003) (holding that the Board’s interpretation of our authority to sua sponte reopen proceedings based on an intervening change in law as being subject to the requirement that the alien had not been deported was a reasonable interpretation of applicable regulations).
 
Contrary to the respondent’s arguments on appeal (Resp. Br. at 8-10), we do not construe Garcia-Carias v. Holder, 697 F.3d 257 (5th Cir. 2012), to allow consideration of the instant motion sua sponte. In that case, the Fifth Circuit addressed the interplay between the Immigration Courts’ departure-bar provision at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), our own comparable departure bar provision under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d), and the statutory right to file a motion to reopen under the Act. Garcia-Carias, supra, at 261-65. In finding those regulations invalid as applied to statutory motions to reopen, the court expressly refused to address the alien’s arguments regarding the timeliness of his motion to reopen. Id. at 260-261 & n.1. In Garcia-Carias, supra, the Court also expressly distinguished the case before it from Navarro-Miranda, supra, and Ovalles, supra, which resolved the issue of the applicability of the departure bar to the regulatory authority to reopen cases sua sponte. Id. at 265. Under these decisions, the Immigration Judge lacked regulatory authority to reopen the proceedings sua sponte. See Navarro-Miranda, supra; Ovalles, supra.
 
*3 Here, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion on timeliness grounds and sua sponte due to the respondent’s departure from the United States (I.J. at 2-3). To the extent the respondent relies on any claimed ineffective assistance of counsel and equitable tolling principles, under Fifth Circuit precedent, an argument for equitable tolling of the time limitation on motions is construed as a challenge to our refusal to exercise our regulatory power to reopen the proceedings sua sponte under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 219-20 (5th Cir. 2008). The Immigration Judge properly found he was unable to reopen the proceedings sua sponte.
 
We also find no miscarriage of justice. Regardless of any change in law pertaining to the classification of the respondent’s conviction as an aggravated felony, the respondent was still removable for being convicted of an offense related to a controlled substance. See section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act. The respondent’s final administrative removal order was lawfully executed upon his departure from the United States. Further, at the time of his proceedings, the law of the Federal Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit did support removal on all grounds. Based on the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
2014 WL 4407934 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OSCAR ANTONIO GONZALEZ
File: AXXX XX1 147 - Newark, NJ
August 18, 2014
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rafael Figueroa, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Review of denial of Temporary Protected Status
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated February 27, 2013, finding him ineligible for temporary protected status (“TPS”) and ordering him removed to El Salvador. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii). In that the respondent filed his application after May 11, 2005, these proceedings are subject to the REAL ID Act. Matter of S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 42 (BIA 2006).
 
An applicant for TPS may seek de novo review by an Immigration Judge in removal proceedings. See Matter of Ismael Lopez-Aldana, 25 I&N Dec. 49 (BIA 2009); see also Matter of Barrientos, 24 I&N Dec. 100 (BIA 2007). The initial period for registration for TPS for Salvadoran nationals was from March 9, 2001 to September 9, 2002. See 66 Fed. Reg. 14214 (Mar. 9, 2001). The respondent submitted an application for TPS on May 10, 2001, during the initial registration period. However, the respondent’s application was rejected by the legacy Immigration and Naturalization Service on September 6, 2001, for insufficient funds and a rejection notice was sent to the respondent at his home address of record (Exh. 2 at illegible page number).
 
The respondent next filed an application for TPS on September 14, 2006. On December 13, 2006, United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”) informed the respondent that his application would be denied because he did not establish that he was eligible for the late registration provisions of the TPS regulations.1 The application was denied on May 29, 2007.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is ineligible for TPS (I.J. at 3-4). Although he filed an application during the initial registration period for El Salvador, the application was denied. The respondent alleges that he did not receive a notice that the check provided for the filing fee bounced, which also provided a limited opportunity to present the correct fee. Although the record does not contain a copy of the original notice, or the return of that notice, a second notice advising the respondent that his application was rejected for failure to pay the fee is contained in the record (Exh. 2 at illegible page number). The notice was sent to the respondent’s address of record. Id. The respondent also claims that he filed a subsequent application during the initial registration period, but there is no evidence of this filing in the administrative record. Therefore, the respondent did not establish that he had a TPS application pending when the initial registration period ended on September 9, 2002.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent reasserts his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, based on a notary’s handling of his TPS application (Respondent’s Brief at 7-8). However, even if we consider the respondent’s claim that there was the equivalent of ineffective assistance of counsel in the notary’s actions, the claim fails because the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements.2 See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (requiring alien to set forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel; that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and that the alien indicate whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not).
 
The Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit has rejected a strict compliance rule regarding the third prong of Lozada, i.e., filing a bar complaint. See Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127 (3d Cir. 2001); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 559 (BIA 2003) (the Board will apply the applicable circuit court law in each respected circuit regarding Lozada strict compliance). Since the respondent asserts that it was not an attorney, but a notary who gave him erroneous advice, he cannot comply with the third prong of Lozada, i.e., filing a bar complaint. However, he can comply with the equivalent of the first and second prongs, and has not done so. The respondent has not set forth in any detail the agreement that was entered into with the notary, and although he testified that he does not remember the notary’s name, it is listed on his original application for TPS, of which there is a photocopy in the record (Exh. A at 22-25; Tr. at 47-48; Respondent’s Brief at 1-2). Thus, he has not substantially complied with the Lozada requirements (I.J. at 5). See e.g., Omar v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 647 (2d Cir. 2008); Bardales-Montes v. Att’y Gen. of U.S., No. 09-4703, 2011 WL 1370564 (3d Cir. Apr. 6, 2011) (unpublished).
 
Moreover, we agree with the Immigration Judge that even if the respondent were able to establish that he substantially complied with the Lozada requirements, he has not shown that the principle of equitable tolling is warranted in this matter, as he has failed to establish that he exercised due diligence “over the entire period for which tolling is desired” (I.J. at 4-5). Alzaarir v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 639 F.3d 86, 90 (3d Cir. 2011) (citing Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008)).
 
*3 Inasmuch as the Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent is ineligible for TPS and ordered him removed to El Salvador, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD

See section 244(f) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1254(f); 8 C.F.R. § 244.2(f).

2

This would also be assuming the respondent could pursue his ineffective assistance claim in these proceedings, even though it relates to conduct which was not part of the removal proceedings. See Contreras v. Att’y Gen. of U.S., 665 F.3d 578 (3d Cir. 2012); see also Balam-Chuc v. Mukasey, 547 F.3d 1044, 1050-51 (9th Cir. 2008).
